## **THE STONE AND THE VEIL**

### **Chapter 1**

The stag was dead, but the forest was not done with it.

Iain MacAnndra’s knife, a grey sliver of old steel, parted the hide at the throat. Steam rose, not in a cloud, but in slow, spiralling threads that seemed to hesitate in the still, cold air of the Glen of Shadows. It was not the heat of life escaping; it was the last breath of the hill, being called back. He worked silently, his hands sure and red, the only sounds the soft *shush* of blade through tissue, the drip of dark blood on bronze moss, and the distant, eternal complaint of the Allt Coire na Saobhaidh burn over its stones. The pines stood witness, their trunks the colour of dried blood in the late afternoon light.

He was not a large man, Iain, but the mountains had carved him into something dense and enduring. His face was a topography of wind and solitude, his eyes the grey of the sea-loch under a winter sky. He wore the landscape like a second skin: tweed the hue of dead bracken, boots scarred by granite, a salt-and-pepper beard that held fragments of last week’s mist. As he lifted the stag’s liver, smooth and purple as a thunderhead, he held it not as a butcher would, but as a reader lifts a page. He turned it in his hands, his gaze tracing the lobes and fissures.

“Aye,” he murmured, the word barely disturbing the silence. “You took the old path by the cairn. Drank from the black pool.” He spoke to the dead animal, to the organ in his hands, to the glen itself. It was not sentiment. It was notation.

A twig snapped, a sound as sharp as a bone breaking. Iain did not startle. His knife-hand stilled, but his eyes lifted, scanning the lattice of pine and birch. The birds had stopped singing a minute past. He’d noted it, filed it away. Now it presented its bill.

“Show yourself,” he said, his voice low, carrying not with volume but with a peculiar density, like stone speaking. “Or watch from the shadows and learn nothing.”

For a moment, nothing. Then, from behind the skeletal embrace of a fallen rowan, a man stepped into the dappled light. He was city-cut, his waterproof jacket a garish blue against the muted greens and browns, his face pale and sharp. A leather satchel was slung across his chest. He looked at the gutted stag, at Iain’s bloody hands, and a flicker of revulsion crossed his features before being schooled into neutrality.

“Iain MacAnndra?” The man’s voice was English, public school, a clean blade of sound in the organic murmur of the glen.

“I am.”

“Alistair Thorne. I wrote.” He took a cautious step closer, his boots slipping on the wet moss.

“You did.”

“You didn’t reply.”

“I don’t own a telephone. The post comes twice a week.” Iain returned to his work, slicing the liver free and laying it on a square of clean linen. “You’re early. And you’re in my larder.”

Thorne cleared his throat, attempting to reclaim some authority. “I’ve come a long way to see you, Mr. MacAnndra. The university sent me. Regarding your… work.”

“My work is here.” Iain gestured with his knife, not at the stag, but at the encircling hills, the water-cut rock, the specific way the light fell through the pines at that hour. “If you’ve come for stories of kelpies and fairy hills, you’ve wasted your petrol. I’m not a folklorist.”

“No. We know what you are.” Thorne’s eyes gleamed, a scholar’s hunger in them now. “Or what you claim to be. A *Fear an Fhiosaiche*. A ‘Knowing Man’. The last of them.”

The old term hung in the air, heavier than the scent of blood. Iain wiped his knife on a tuft of grass, slowly, meticulously. “Words,” he said. “Empty cups. What do you want to drink from them?”

“The Cailleach’s Stone,” Thorne said, the words rushed, as if afraid of being interrupted. “The records in the Advocates’ Library, the kirk session notes from the 17th century, they all point to you. To your family. You’re the keeper. The last link to a… a system of knowledge we’ve lost. A mapping of the land not by geography, but by meaning. By symbol.”

Iain stood, his knees popping softly. He looked at Thorne properly for the first time. The man was a bundle of sharp angles and nervous energy, a scribe of dead things trying to understand a living one. “The Stone is a stone,” Iain said flatly. “It sits on the moor. It gets rained on. Sheep scratch their arses on it. There’s no map.”

“But the carvings—”

“Are worn by weather and time. As all things are.”

Thorne’s composure cracked, frustration showing at the edges. “We have lidar scans! Spectral imaging! There are layers of inscription, patterns that correspond to stellar alignments, to watersheds, to the migration patterns of geese three hundred years ago! It’s not just a stone, it’s an archive. A key. And you hold the cipher.”

The wind shifted, sliding down from the high corries. It carried a new smell, not of pine or peat, but of something cold and metallic, like the air before lightning strikes, but without the storm. Iain’s nostrils flared. He looked past Thorne, up to the head of the glen where the crags bit into the grey sky. The light was wrong. It had a thin, leaching quality, as if the colour were being drained from the world.

“Your theory is a room,” Iain said, his voice dropping, becoming almost conversational. “You’ve built it from old books and scanned images. You have the measurements perfect. But you have no sense of the house it sits in. Or the storm outside the door.”

“What storm?” Thorne asked, following his gaze, seeing only landscape.

Iain didn’t answer. He was listening. The burn’s voice had changed. It wasn’t just complaining now; it was speaking in a lower, quicker register. The pines, which had been still, now sighed with a concerted breath, their needles whispering not of wind, but of warning. He bent and began to wrap the stag’s heart, the kidneys, the liver in the linen, tying it with a length of twine.

“The storm that comes when you read a text aloud in a silent place,” Iain said finally. “When you wake a thing by naming it. Your lidar saw lines on rock. It didn’t see the hook those lines are baited with.”

He shouldered the meat parcel and picked up his rifle, a weathered .303. “You’ll follow me now, Mr. Thorne. And you’ll step where I step.”

“Why? What’s happening?”

“The glen has decided you’re a disturbance.” Iain began to move, not back down the path Thorne had come, but laterally across the steep hillside, his steps finding purchase on invisible ledges, his body flowing through the undergrowth like water. “It’s trying to settle you. To quiet you. Like a body pushing out a splinter.”

Thorne, bewildered, scrambled after him. “That’s absurd. It’s a landscape. It has no consciousness.”

Iain paused, turning just his head. The grey eyes held Thorne immobile. “You’re a man of learning. Tell me, what is the fundamental quality of a symbol?”

“It… it represents something else. An idea, an object.”

“No,” Iain said, turning and moving again, his voice floating back. “Its fundamental quality is relationship. A stone is just a stone. A stone placed on a grave is a symbol. The relationship *is* the meaning. This glen… it is in a relationship with everything in it. Including you. And you have just shouted in its sacred ear.”

The metallic smell intensified. The light dimmed further, not with the approach of evening, but as if a sheer veil were being drawn across the sun. Thorne, panting, his fine boots soaked and slipping, felt a prickle of fear that had nothing to do with falling. It was a deeper, older unease. The world felt *attentive*.

Iain stopped at the base of a colossal outcrop of schist, furred with moss and dripping with moisture. There was no obvious path. He placed a hand on the rock, fingers splayed, as if feeling for a pulse. Then he leaned into what looked like solid stone and vanished.

Thorne gasped. He stumbled forward, hands out. It was a fold, a cunning crack in the rockface, angled so it was invisible from three paces away. He squeezed through, the cold stone brushing his shoulders, and emerged into a small, hidden bowl of land. A bothy, little more than a stone hut with a sod roof, nestled against the far side. A thin plume of peat smoke rose from a chimney. But it was the bowl itself that stole Thorne’s breath.

It was a perfect, natural amphitheatre. A tiny lochan, black and still, lay at its centre. And around it, in a circle, stood nine rowan trees, their branches bare but for a few clusters of wizened, blood-red berries. They stood in silent, deliberate sentry. This was not a random arrangement. This was a made place.

Iain was already at the door of the bothy. “Inside. Quickly.”

Thorne needed no second urging. The moment he stepped out of the lee of the rocks, the air in the bowl had grown preternaturally still and cold. The lochan’s surface was like polished obsidian, reflecting nothing.

The bothy was a single room, dominated by a smouldering peat fire. The air was thick with the smells of smoke, dried herbs, and old paper. Books were stacked on crude shelves, alongside bundles of feathers, stones of peculiar shapes, and jars of unidentifiable preserves. Iain deposited the meat on a table and went to the small, deep-set window.

“What is it?” Thorne whispered, the scholarly arrogance utterly gone from his voice.

“A forgetting,” Iain said, his back to him. “The land has moods. This is one. A quiet, hungry one. It blurs edges. Makes paths forget themselves. Makes a man forget why he walked into a wood, until he just… becomes part of it.”

He turned. In the firelight, his face was ancient, carved from the same stuff as the hills outside. “You came for a symbol, Mr. Thorne. You wanted to put it under your glass and measure it. But a symbol isn’t a dead butterfly on a pin. It’s a door. And you’ve been knocking on it very, very loudly. Something has answered. Not the Stone. The thing the Stone was placed there to guard.”

Thorne sank onto a three-legged stool, his satchel clutched to his chest. “I don’t believe you.”

“Belief is a luxury of safe rooms and electric light,” Iain said, not unkindly. It was a statement of fact. “Out here, there is only knowing, or not knowing. You are in the second state. Stay by the fire. Don’t look out the window after full dark. However much you think you hear your name.”

“What are you going to do?”

Iain took a long, wooden staff from beside the door. It was topped with a cap of tarnished silver, etched with worn patterns. “I,” he said, “am going to remind the glen who I am. And re-negotiate the relationship.”

He opened the door. The world outside was monochrome, drained of hue, silent as a held breath. Iain MacAnndra stepped into it, pulling the door shut behind him, leaving Alistair Thorne alone with the crackling fire and the crushing, silent weight of a world that was suddenly, terrifyingly, alive.

---
### **Chapter 2**

The silence was not an absence. It was a substance.

It pressed against the bothy’s thick walls, against the small window, a palpable, woolly quiet that made the crackle of the peat fire seem obscenely loud. Alistair Thorne sat rigid on the stool, his mind a riot of dissonant thoughts. The rational catalogue of his education—post-structuralist theory, historiography, material culture analysis—clashed against the animal fear in his hindbrain, the one that knew the shape of a predator in the dark. *It’s a psychosomatic response*, he told himself. *Suggestion, isolation, the power of a charismatic personality over a suggestible subject*. He was the suggestible subject. Iain MacAnndra was a fascinating anachronism, a performance artist of the wilderness, weaving spells with words and weather.

He stood, legs stiff, and approached the window. The deep-set pane of old, flawed glass showed a world rendered in shades of charcoal and milk. The nine rowans stood like iron filings drawn into a perfect circle by a magnet. The black lochan was a hole cut in the earth. Nothing moved.

Then, something did.

At the edge of the trees, a shift. A suggestion of a form, taller than a man but not shaped like one. It was less a thing seen and more a *coagulation* of the gloom, a place where the shadows decided to become denser, vertical. Thorne blinked, hard. Pareidolia. The brain seeking pattern. When he looked again, it was gone. But the space where it had been seemed to vibrate with a lingering wrongness, like the air after a bell has stopped ringing.

A low sound seeped into the bothy. Not through the walls, but somehow *under* them. It was the sound of roots moving, of stone grinding deep in the earth, a sub-audible groan that Thorne felt in his molars. The water in a tin cup on the table developed a fine, trembling ripple.

He backed away from the window, his heart a frantic bird in his chest. *Suggestion. It’s all suggestion.* He needed data. Something to anchor himself in the real. His satchel. He fumbled it open, pulling out his notebook, his phone (useless, no signal, the battery icon blinking red), and a folder of printouts. The lidar scans of the Cailleach’s Stone.

He spread them on the table, smoothing the glossy paper with trembling hands. The scans were beautiful, a ghostly topographical map in neon colours. The stone was a jagged tooth of basalt, three metres high. The carvings were a complex palimpsest: concentric circles, spirals that mirrored the double spiral of the galaxy, interlocking labyrinths, and what looked like representations of plants, animals, and strange, angular runes that matched no known alphabet. Thorne’s own annotations crowded the margins: *“Cf. Orkney spiral motifs, 2500 BCE.” “Hydrological marker? Lines align with seasonal burn courses.” “Possible stellar sightlines – Cygnus?”*

He had been so proud of this work. It was going to be his monograph, his tenure ticket. *The Cailleach’s Stone: A Proto-Scientific Cartography of the Neolithic Mind*. He saw it now not as a revelation, but as a child’s drawing of a dragon, all scales and claws, with no conception of the fire in its belly.

The sub-audible groan rose in pitch, becoming a thin, keening whine that seemed to come from inside his own skull. The fire in the grate guttered, the flames sucking down to blue embers for a moment before flaring again, casting leaping, monstrous shadows on the walls. The shadows of the drying herbs looked like hanging bodies. The shadow of a chair became a crouching beast.

“Stop it,” Thorne whispered to himself, squeezing his eyes shut. “You are a Fellow of the Royal Society of Antiquaries. You are not afraid of the dark.”

But he was. He was afraid of this dark. Because this dark knew his name. Iain had said not to listen, but now he heard it, not with his ears, but in the base of his spine, a susurration that was almost language: *…thorne…come see…thorne…the quiet is nice…*

“No,” he gasped, clapping his hands over his ears. It didn’t help. The sound was internal.

With a surge of desperate defiance, he grabbed his pen and wrote in his notebook, the letters jagged: *“Observation: Subjective experience of auditory and visual hallucination. Likely induced by environmental stress, suggestion, and possible infrasound from wind patterns in geological formation.”* He underlined *infrasound* twice. It was a good, solid, scientific word. It tamed the horror, caged it in syllables.

The door latch rattled.

Thorne froze, pen poised. The rattling was soft, inquisitive. Not a wind-blown branch. It was the deliberate testing of a lock.

*…open…*

He stood, his stool scraping loudly. He looked for a weapon. A peat cutter? A fire iron? He was an academic, not a fighter. The rattling stopped. Silence rushed back in, deeper than before.

Then, a knock. Three precise, measured taps on the thick wood.

It was the politeness of it that froze his blood. The horrible, mock civility.

“Iain?” he called out, his voice a dry croak.

No answer. Only the echo of his own voice in the silent room.

The knock came again. *Tap. Tap. Tap.*

Thorne did the only thing he could think of. He began to talk. To lecture. His voice, shaky at first, gained strength as he fell into the familiar rhythm of exposition.

“The Cailleach’s Stone,” he said loudly, addressing the door, “is a classic example of a *axis mundi* symbol. A world centre. A point of connection between the mundane and the spiritual realms. In Celtic tradition, the Cailleach is the goddess of winter, the shaper of landscapes. Her stone would be a place of power, a… a conduit.”

He was babbling, but the act of speech held the silence at bay. He picked up a lidar scan, holding it towards the door as if it were a crucifix.

“The carvings are a language. Not of words, but of relationships. You see here, the spiral—it doesn’t just represent a whirlpool or a galaxy. It *is* the motion of becoming and unbecoming. The labyrinth isn’t a puzzle; it’s the path of a life, or of a season. To ‘read’ the stone is not to decode a message, but to… to *walk its pattern*.”

He paused, breathless. The silence from beyond the door was now attentive. Hungry. He realized, with a sickening lurch, that he was feeding it. His words, his theories, were the offering. Iain had warned him: you wake a thing by naming it.

The latch began to turn, slowly, of its own accord.

Thorne stumbled backward, knocking over his stool. The scan fluttered from his hand. As he bent to grab it, his eyes fell on the other side of the paper, where he’d printed an early 19th-century drawing of the stone. In the margin of that drawing, in spidery ink, was a note he’d copied from a kirk session record: *“Old Mairi MacAnndra, called the ‘Keeper’, was questioned on the nature of the Great Stone in the Moor. She said: ‘It is not a page to be read, but a lock to be held. We keep the key. The key is the pattern of living here. When the pattern is forgotten, the door will open, and the cold will come in.’”*

*The key is the pattern of living here.*

The latch clicked. The door began to swing inward, revealing not the twilight bowl, but a wall of impenetrable, swirling grey mist. A cold that had nothing to do with temperature poured through the opening, a cold that smelled of deep soil and forgotten things.

Thorne was going to die. He knew it. He would step into that mist and be unmade, his neat categories and theories dissolved into the hungry quiet.

A new sound cut through the silence. Not a knock. A *strike*. A sharp, resonant *clack*, like stone on stone, from somewhere out in the bowl.

The mist in the doorway hesitated.

*Clack. Clack. Clack.*

The sound was rhythmic, deliberate. A counter-rhythm to the silent pulse of the glen. With each strike, Thorne felt the oppressive pressure in the room lessen a fraction. The fire blazed up, normal and warm. The shadows retreated to their proper corners.

He crept to the doorway. The mist was receding, flowing back like a tide across the grass towards the black lochan. And there, standing between the third and fourth rowan tree, was Iain.

He held his staff in one hand. In the other, he held two river stones, about the size of goose eggs. He struck them together again, a clean, percussive *clack* that seemed to shear the thickening air. He was not chanting. He was not performing a ritual. He was *working*. Like a carpenter hammering a nail, like a smith shaping iron. He struck the stones, then moved his staff, tracing a shape in the air—a simple, angular sigil that Thorne recognized from the stone: the symbol for ‘boundary’.

Iain’s movements were economical, utterly focused. He was not fighting the mist-thing. He was *correcting* a fault. Re-drawing a line that had been smudged. He was the keeper of the pattern, and he was mending a tear in the fabric.

The mist pooled at the lochan’s edge, seethed for a moment, and then sank into the black water without a ripple. The unnatural grey light began to bleed away, replaced by the deep, clean blue of true twilight. The rowan berries, invisible a moment before, now glowed like distant coals.

Iain lowered the stones. He stood for a long moment, head bowed, listening. Then he turned and walked back to the bothy, his boots crunching on the frost-stiffened grass. He looked weary, not with physical exertion, but with a profound, moral fatigue.

He stepped inside, closed the door, and slid a heavy iron bar across it. He placed the two stones carefully on the mantelpiece, then went to the sink and began washing his hands.

Thorne could not speak. The reality of what had just happened—what he had seen and felt—had shattered the lens of his old worldview. There was no theory to fit it into. It simply *was*.

Iain dried his hands, then turned. He looked at Thorne’s ashen face, at the scattered papers on the table.

“You spoke to it,” Iain said. It wasn’t a question.

“I… I tried to explain it. To define it.”

“Aye. You gave it a shape to wear. Made it easier for it to find the latch.” Iain’s voice was flat, without accusation. “You have the knowledge of symbols, Mr. Thorne. But you lack the wisdom of the symbol. You see the map and think it’s the territory. Out here, the territory reads *you*.”

Thorne found his voice, small and broken. “What was it?”

“A *bodach*. A ‘old man’ of the place. Not a creature. A… a tendency. A forgetting given form. They gather where the old patterns are weak, where the relationship is broken. Your interest, your scans, your papers—they are a loud noise in a quiet relationship. It drew him.”

“And the stones? The knocking?”

“The oldest language,” Iain said, stirring the pot over the fire. A rich, gamey smell filled the room. “Older than words. The sound of order. Of one thing distinct from another. A wall being built. A boundary struck.” He ladled stew into a bowl and handed it to Thorne. “Eat. You’re in shock.”

Thorne took the bowl, the warmth seeping into his icy fingers. “The note… from Old Mairi. ‘The key is the pattern of living here.’ What does that mean?”

Iain sat opposite him, his own bowl in hand. He ate slowly, deliberately. “It means the Stone is not a lock on a door to another world. It is a lock on a door in this one. A door in *us*. In how we live with the land. The carvings are reminders of the pattern: when to plant, when to hunt, when to be silent, when the geese will fly, where the fresh water rises. Live that pattern, and the door stays shut. Forget it…” He gestured with his spoon towards the now-quiet night outside. “…and the cold gets in. The *bodach* is the cold.”

Thorne stared into his stew. His grand monograph was ashes. He had come to study a key and had almost turned it in the wrong lock. “What do I do?” he asked, not as a scholar, but as a lost man.

Iain studied him for a long time. “You have two choices. You can leave at first light, go back to your university, and tell them you found nothing but a mad old hermit. You can forget this, or call it a psychosis. You will be safe.”

“And the second choice?”

“You can learn the pattern. Not from scans. From the land itself. You can help me mend what’s been broken. It is harder work than writing papers. And it is dangerous.” Iain’s grey eyes held his. “The *bodach* knows your name now. It will listen for it.”

The fire crackled. The safe, scholarly life in a dusty office beckoned like a warm bed. But Thorne knew, with a certainty that terrified him, that if he left, he would spend the rest of his days seeing that swirling grey mist at the edge of every thought, hearing that polite, hungry knock in every quiet moment.

He put down his spoon. His hands were steady.

“Teach me,” said Alistair Thorne.

---
### **Chapter 3**

Dawn came not with colour, but with a slow dilution of grey.

Iain was already outside when Thorne emerged, stiff and sore from a night on the bothy’s hard floor. The scholar stood in the doorway, hugging himself against the bite in the air. The hidden bowl was transformed. The black lochan was now a sheet of pale steel, reflecting a sky the colour of a woodpigeon’s breast. The nine rowans stood stark and beautiful, their berries like drops of sealing wax against the monochrome world. The silence was no longer hungry, but clean and vast, the silence of a page before the first word is written.

Iain was by the water’s edge, perfectly still. He wasn’t looking at the lochan; he was listening to it. Thorne approached cautiously, his city shoes slipping on the frosted grass.

“It’s… peaceful,” Thorne ventured, his breath pluming.

“Peace is not the absence of disturbance,” Iain said without turning. “It is the presence of order. Listen.”

Thorne listened. He heard the faint drip of moisture from the trees, the distant cry of a raven, the soft lap of water against peat. Normal sounds.

“No,” Iain said, as if reading his thoughts. “Not with your head. With your bones. The water is speaking. What is it saying?”

Thorne closed his eyes, frustrated. He heard water. Just water. “I don’t know how.”

Iain finally looked at him. The weariness of the night was still there, but overlaid with a patient focus. “You spent a lifetime learning to hear words. Now you must unlearn it. The land doesn’t speak in sentences. It speaks in relationships. The pitch of that raven’s call—it’s sharper than yesterday. It’s speaking of a fox on the high crag. The pattern of these ripples…” He pointed to the water’s surface where a subtle, intersecting network of tiny waves spread from several points. “…the trout are rising in a specific sequence. They’re feeding on midges hatching from the reed bed on the south side. The water is telling you the wind is about to shift, from the east to the north. It will be colder by noon.”

Thorne stared, first at Iain, then at the water. It was chaos to him. Just ripples.

“This,” Iain said, his gesture taking in the bowl, the trees, the sky, “is the first page of the text. The Stone is the index. You tried to read the index without knowing the alphabet. Today, you learn the alphabet.”

The lesson began not with lore, but with labour. Iain handed Thorne a wooden bucket. “The south-east rowan. The one with the split trunk. Collect the berries that have fallen on the moss beneath it. Only those. Not from the tree. Not from any other tree.”

It seemed a simple, pointless task. But as Thorne crouched in the cold, searching for the small, wrinkled berries in the damp moss, he began to notice things. The moss was thicker on the north side of the tree’s roots. The fallen berries here were more numerous, but also more shrivelled, than those on the other side. The tree itself seemed to lean slightly away from the lochan, as if in deference or caution.

When he brought the bucket back, Iain sorted through the berries meticulously, discarding a few. “These are from the north side. They taste of shadow. They’re for another purpose.” He placed the chosen berries in a stone mortar and began to crush them with a pestle. A sharp, astringent scent filled the air—not unpleasant, but clean and penetrating. “Rowan. The keeper of the threshold. Its roots drink from the same water that feeds the lochan. Its berries hold the memory of the summer sun. They mark a boundary between what is safe and what is not. We use them to re-paint the lines the *bodach* tries to blur.”

“You make a paint?”

“A reminder,” Iain corrected. He mixed the crushed berries with a little water from a specific, moss-covered seep at the base of the rockface, not from the lochan. The mixture became a viscous, pinkish paste. He dipped his thumb in it. “Come.”

He led Thorne to the entrance of the hidden bowl, to the narrow crack in the schist. On the inside surface of the rock, almost invisible, were dozens of faint, old marks. Some were the same angular sigil Iain had traced in the air the night before. Others were simple dots, lines, tiny spirals. Iain pressed his berry-stained thumb against the rock and drew a fresh, small spiral next to a cluster of ancient ones.

“Not a spell,” he said, seeing Thorne’s expression. “A note. A stitch in the fabric. It says: *attended to*. It tells the glen the pattern is remembered. The relationship is honoured.”

The day unfolded as a series of such tasks, each a tiny, precise act of participation. They gathered specific lichens from the north-facing stones for a poultice Iain kept in a jar. They repaired a section of the bothy’s sod roof with fresh turf cut from a particular slope where the grass grew thick and sweet. With each action, Iain offered a fragment of context, not as doctrine, but as observation.

“We cut the turf here because the sheep don’t graze it. They know the herbs here are for healing, not for their bellies.”
“This lichen only grows where the air is clean and the stone remembers the morning sun. It has a light in it, even when dried.”
“We fill the water jug from the seep, not the lochan. The lochan is for looking into, not for taking from. It is a mirror, not a well.”

It was a language of distinctions, of subtle, vital boundaries. Thorne’s mind, trained for abstraction, struggled with the concrete specificity of it all. It was humbling. He was a PhD rendered illiterate.

In the afternoon, Iain shouldered his staff and a small pack. “We’ll go to the high moor. To the Stone. You need to see it not as your scan sees it, but as the land holds it.”

The path was not a path. It was a memory of movement over the land, a faint suggestion of wear on heather and grass. Iain moved with an effortless, flowing gait, pausing sometimes to touch a rock, to examine a particular plant, to listen. Thorne stumbled behind, his lungs burning in the thin air, his clothes snagged by gorse.

As they climbed, the character of the land changed. The sheltered glens with their pines and birches gave way to open, rolling moorland, a sea of bronze and purple heather under a vast, scudding sky. The wind here was a constant, living force, singing in the wires of a lone fence post, sculpting the clouds. It felt older, less forgiving.

And then, they crested a rise, and Thorne saw it.

The Cailleach’s Stone stood in a shallow depression, a sentinel in the emptiness. It was more imposing than in the scans, a brutal, dark thrust of rock from the earth’s heart. It was not majestic. It was *authoritative*. From a distance, it looked natural, a random erratic left by a glacier. But as they drew closer, the truth of it became undeniable. Its surfaces, though weathered to a smooth, grey patina, were covered in the carvings. They were not just on one face; they spiralled around it, climbed it, covered it like a skin of forgotten scripture.

Thorne approached it, his scholar’s heart pounding, but with a new trepidation. This was no longer an artifact. It was a presence.

Iain stopped a dozen paces away. “Walk around it. Slowly. Don’t touch it yet. Tell me what you feel.”

Thorne did as he was told. The wind seemed to quieten as he entered the stone’s sphere. The carvings were mesmerizing—the deep, sure grooves of spirals, the intricate labyrinths, the stark, beautiful lines of a running deer, a soaring goose, a leaping salmon. He felt a powerful urge to lay his hands on them, to trace their paths with his fingers, to absorb their meaning by touch.

But he also felt something else. A deep, resonant hum, not a sound, but a vibration in the ground beneath his feet. A sense of immense, patient attention. The stone was not dead. It was *listening*.

“It’s… waiting,” Thorne said, surprised by his own words.

Iain gave a slow nod. “Aye. It’s a fulcrum. A place of balance. The carvings are the weights on the scale. They are the memories of the right relationships—the salmon in the river, the deer on the hill, the star in its season. They hold the balance steady.”

Thorne completed his circuit, stopping before the stone’s broadest face. Here was the largest carving: a series of concentric circles surrounding a deep, drilled hole about the size of a man’s fist. “And this?”

“The heart of it,” Iain said, his voice low. “On the longest night, when the moon is full and stands in that notch of the southern hills…” He pointed to a distant, V-shaped cleft on the horizon. “…the moonlight passes through that hole and strikes a point on the ground there.” He indicated a flat, pale stone half-buried in the peat ten feet away. “It marks the turn of the year. The moment the Cailleach’s grip begins to loosen, and the seed of summer is sown in the dark. It is the axis of the pattern.”

Thorne looked from the hole to the distant notch, understanding dawning. This was a clock. A calendar. A model of the cosmos, rooted in this specific patch of earth. Its function was not to point to something *elsewhere*, but to mark the precise, sacred *hereness* of time and place.

“It’s beautiful,” he whispered, and for the first time, the word held its full meaning—not just aesthetic, but full of a terrible, solemn awe.

“It is necessary,” Iain corrected. He stepped forward now, placing his hands not on the carvings, but on the bare, uncarved stone at the very base. He leaned his forehead against it, closing his eyes. He stood like that for a full minute, in silent communion.

When he straightened,



---

# Chapter 4

## **Chapter 4**

### **I**

The old man’s name was Ruairidh, and he lived in the wound of the world.

Iain led Thorne down from the high, patterned stone, not by the way they had come, but along a seam in the land so subtle it was more a suggestion of passage than a path. It followed the spine of a hidden ridge, dipped into a hollow where the air grew thick and still, and ended at the edge of a loch that Thorne was certain had not been on the map. The water was the colour of tarnished slate, its surface unruffled by any wind, holding the inverted image of the pines and the bruised evening sky with a perfect, glassy stillness. On its far shore, nestled against a cliff of raw, weeping rock, was a cottage. Smoke, pale and straight as the stag’s last breath, rose from its chimney into the twilight.

They crossed in a shallow, tar-black boat that Ruairidh kept tethered to an alder root. Iain rowed with slow, practised strokes that left no wake, the oarlocks muffled with rags. The loch drank the sound. By the time they grounded on the gravel beach, the last of the day had bled from the west, and the world was rendered in tones of iron and ash.

The cottage was older than any building Thorne had ever seen. Its walls were not mortared stone, but drystack, each rock chosen and placed with a logic that seemed organic, as if the structure had grown from the ground. The roof was sod, long grass waving from it like hair from a scalp. A single, small window, its glass thick and warped, shone with the orange pulse of firelight.

The door opened before they reached it.

Ruairidh stood in the frame, not filling it, but defining it. He was thin to the point of austerity, his face a topography of deep lines and sharp bones, his hair a wild mantle of white that fell to his shoulders. His eyes were the unsettling, clear grey of the winter sea. He wore a simple tunic of undyed wool, patched at the elbows, and trousers of the same. He looked at Iain, then at Thorne, his gaze neither welcoming nor hostile, but appraising in the way a rock might appraise the rain.

“You’ve brought the stone’s echo,” Ruairidh said. His voice was dry, the sound of pebbles shifting in a slow current.

“He felt the fulcrum,” Iain replied, as if this were a sufficient introduction. “He listened.”

Ruairidh’s eyes lingered on Thorne a moment longer. Then he gave a single, shallow nod and stepped back into the warmth.

The interior was a single room, a cave shaped by human need. The floor was packed earth. A peat fire smouldered in a hearth of flat stones, its smoke finding its way to a hole in the roof with a weary familiarity. The air was rich with the smells of burning peat, drying herbs, woodsmoke, and something older: the scent of stone and deep, undisturbed soil. Along one wall were shelves holding an array of objects that defied immediate categorization: bundles of feathers, stacks of lichen-covered stones, coils of root that still held the shape of the earth, pots of pigments, and tools of flint and antler and bronze. It was not clutter, but a curated collection, a physical lexicon.

Ruairidh did not offer them food or drink. He gestured to two low stools near the fire, then settled himself on a third, his movements economical. He picked up a piece of pale, dry wood and a small, curved knife and began to whittle, the *shush-shush* of the blade a quiet counter-rhythm to the pop of the peat.

“You come from the world of lines,” Ruairidh said, not looking up from his work. “Straight lines on maps. Lines of ownership. Lines of reason. You see a hill and you think *boundary*. You see a river and you think *resource*. You see a forest and you think *timber* or *obstacle*.” A long curl of wood peeled away and fell into his lap. “The land is not made of lines. It is made of relationships. A web, Thorne from the south. A living pattern. The stone you touched today is a knot in that web. A place where the relationships are remembered, made visible.”

Thorne found his voice, hushed by the room’s gravity. “Iain said it was a fulcrum. A balance.”

“It is. And what is balanced?” Ruairidh paused his knife, his grey eyes catching the firelight. “The seen and the unseen. The giving and the taking. The song and the silence.” He resumed his whittling. “Your civilization has forgotten the second half of every pair. It takes, and does not listen for what it should give. It shouts its own song, and hears no other. The balance sickens. The pattern frays.”

“And the Veil?” Thorne asked, the words leaving him before he could consider them.

The knife stilled. Ruairidh looked at Iain, a silent conversation passing between them in the flickering light. Iain gave a slow, almost imperceptible nod.

“The Veil is not a curtain between worlds,” Ruairidh said, his voice dropping to the murmur of the fire itself. “That is a child’s story. It is the *sensitivity* of the pattern. The receptiveness of the relationships. When the balance is true, the Veil is… permeable. A gentle exchange. The hill dreams, and the dream touches a man’s sleep. The river remembers, and the memory colours a poet’s verse. The stone holds time, and a woman feels the turn of the season in her bones.” He pointed the tip of his knife at Thorne. “But when the balance is wounded—when the taking is too great, the noise too loud—the pattern recoils. The relationships contract. The sensitivity becomes a raw nerve, then a scar. The exchange becomes a trickle, then a shout across a gulf, then silence. What you call ‘hauntings’ are the last, desperate echoes in that growing silence. The pattern’s pain, mistaken for ghost stories.”

Thorne sat very still. The academic framework he had brought with him—folklore, psychology, superstition—crumbled like dry clay. This was an older, sterner science. An ecology of the intangible.

“The fulcrum stones,” he said slowly. “They’re… anchors. To hold the sensitivity open.”

“They are prayers,” Ruairidh corrected, his tone final. “Not spoken, but carved. A prayer is not a request. It is an act of alignment. By carving the salmon, the deer, the star, the old ones aligned themselves with those relationships. They placed a weight on the scale, to steady it against the storm.” He set down the wood and the knife, now shaped into a small, elegant bird in flight. “My role, and Iain’s, and those before us, is to be the keepers of the alignment. To tend the prayers. To listen for the fraying of the web, and to mend it where we can.”

He stood, moving to the shelves. He selected a small, leather-wrapped bundle and brought it back to the fire. Unwrapping it, he revealed a set of tools: a fine bronze chisel, a hammer of dark stone, a needle of bone, a palette of dried pigments in hollowed stones.

“These are the tools of mending,” he said. “Not with mortar and brick, but with attention and symbol. To re-carve a line worn by rain. To place a drop of ochre where the memory has faded. To whisper the name of a place that has been forgotten by maps.” He looked at Thorne, his gaze piercing. “This is the training. Not of combat, but of perception. To see the world not as a collection of things, but as a chorus of conversations. To hear the land’s voice, which is not in words, but in the shape of a branch, the taste of a spring, the weight of the air before snow.”

He extended the bundle, not to Iain, but to Thorne. “Your hands recorded dead words on dead paper. Can they learn to feel a living line?”

The offer hung in the peat-scented air. Thorne looked at the tools. They were ancient, intimate, sacred. To take them was to accept a definition of the world that would unravel his own. It was to cross from observer to participant. He thought of the humming stone, of the listening silence, of the pattern vast and fragile and aching.

He reached out, and his fingers closed around the cool leather.

“I can learn,” he said.

### **II**

The lesson began at dawn, in the mist that clung to the loch like a shroud.

Ruairidh led Thorne to a low, flat rock by the water’s edge, its surface scarred with countless fine, aimless grooves. “This is a practice stone,” the old man said. “It has no pattern of its own. It receives what is given.” He placed a smaller, smoother river stone in Thorne’s hand, and a flint flake with a sharp edge. “You will not carve today. You will feel.”

For hours, Thorne sat, running his fingers over the practice stone’s surface. He felt the granular texture of the granite, the slickness of lichen in one patch, the gritty roughness of weathering in another. Ruairidh stood nearby, silent, occasionally pointing at a birch leaf trembling in a breeze Thorne hadn’t felt, or at the concentric ripples from a trout’s rise far out on the loch.

“The line you will one day carve,” Ruairidh said, his voice blending with the drip of mist from pine needles, “does not live on the stone. It lives in the relationship between the stone and the thing it signifies. Your hand does not impose the line. It *invites* it up from the memory within the rock, from the spirit of the creature, from the space between them. You are a conduit. A bridge. To carve a salmon, you must first know the salmon—not its anatomy, but its *essence*. The flash of silver in the dark pool. The relentless drive against the current. The cool, fluid strength.”

He made Thorne wade into the loch, to stand still until the cold numbed his feet and the water’s pulse became his own. He made him lie on his back in the moss, watching the clouds tear themselves apart on the mountain peaks, to understand the sky’s vast, indifferent movement. It was a deconstruction of everything Thorne knew. Knowledge was not accumulation; it was immersion. Meaning was not decoded; it was felt in the marrow.

In the afternoons, by the fire, Ruairidh shared the backstory of the pattern. It was not a history of kings or battles, but a genealogy of attention.

“Long ago,” he said, stirring a pot of thin broth, “the people of these glens knew no separation. The hill was their shoulder. The river their bloodstream. Their stories were not *about* the land; they were the land speaking itself. The carving of the stones began not as ritual, but as necessity. As the first seeds of what you would call ‘civilization’ took root—the desire to own, to fence, to name and claim—a faint echo of separation was felt. A quiet anxiety. The wisest among them felt the first, subtle fraying. So they began to make the pattern visible, to remind themselves and the land of the wholeness. They were not worshipping nature. They were participating in a covenant.”

He told of the Seven Fulcra, stones placed at points of great sensitivity across the highlands. Each held a different aspect of the balance. The one Thorne had touched was the Keeper of Time. Another, near a sacred spring, was the Keeper of Flow. A third, on a high, wind-scoured peak, was the Keeper of Breath.

“But the world of lines grew louder,” Ruairidh continued, his face grim in the firelight. “Forests fell. Rivers were bent to mills. The songs of iron and steam drowned out the older songs. The pattern was wounded, not in one blow, but by a thousand small cuts. The keepers grew fewer. Some stones were buried by peat, or tumbled into lochs by men who saw only useless rock. The Veil, the sensitivity, grew taut, then ragged. Now, the echoes in the silence are becoming screams. Places where the wound is deep fester. They become… thin. And things that belong to the raw, un-patterned chaos on the other side of creation can sometimes press through.”

Thorne understood now. The haunting in the library, the oppressive dread in the Glen of Shadows—they were not random hauntings. They were symptoms. Infections in a weakened system. The academic’s quest for a singular “Veil Stone” was a profound misunderstanding. The stone was not a key to a door. It was a medicine for a sickness. And the sickness was everywhere.

“The man I seek,” Thorne said. “Alasdair. He believed in the single stone. A grand fix.”

Ruairidh snorted, a short, dry sound. “A man of lines, seeking a line-solution. He came here, years ago. Full of fire and theory. He wanted to *use* the pattern, to harness it. He saw a power to be tapped. I showed him the practice stone. He grew impatient. He left, seeking a legend, not an understanding.” The old man’s eyes met Thorne’s. “I believe he found a place where the Veil is not just thin, but torn. And a torn thing can be manipulated, for a time, before it consumes the manipulator.”

The lesson was vast, terrifying. It put Thorne’s entire quest, his brother’s fate, into a context that was both larger and more intimate than he had imagined. This was not a treasure hunt. It was a triage.

### **III**

A week passed in this rhythm of silent feeling and stark revelation. Thorne’s city-soft hands grew rough, his senses stretching like muscles long unused. He could now sit by the practice stone and feel not just its texture, but a faint, sleeping potential within it. He could distinguish between the different silences of the forest—the alert hush of the hunt, the peaceful quiet of growth, the aching stillness of a place where the pattern was strained.

One evening, Ruairidh did not return from his solitary walk at dusk. Iain, who had been mending nets by the loch, came inside, his expression unreadable. He went to the back of the cottage, to a shadowed corner behind the hearth, and knelt. Thorne heard the scrape of stone on stone. Iain returned holding an object wrapped in oiled cloth.

He laid it on the rough table between them and unfolded the cloth.

Inside was a knife. But it was like no knife Thorne had ever seen. The blade was not metal, but a long, dark shard of obsidian, its edges wickedly sharp, its core holding smoky, captured light. The hilt was antler, worn smooth by generations of palms, bound with strips of leather that were black with age and sweat. It was beautiful, and it was utterly savage.

“This is not a weapon for fighting men,” Iain said, his voice low. “It is a tool for cutting threads.”

Thorne stared, uncomprehending.

“The tears in the pattern,” Iain explained. “Sometimes, they are not just wounds. Sometimes, things… *attach*. Parasitic threads, feeding on the dissonance, making the tear wider. They can manifest as the clinging shadows, the voices that whisper madness, the cold that kills from the inside out. A steel blade passes through them like air. This,” he nodded at the obsidian, “is forged in the fire of the earth’s heart. It holds an echo of the world’s first making. It can sever what does not belong to the pattern.”

He picked up the knife, and for a moment, he was no longer the quiet guide, but something older, more formidable. The firelight gleamed on the volcanic glass. “Ruairidh believes your path will lead you to such a tear. Where your brother was lost. This is his gift. The tangible tool for the intangible war.”

He reversed the knife and offered the hilt to Thorne.

Thorne took it. The antler fit his grip as if it had been waiting for it. The blade was lighter than he expected, yet it seemed to pull downward with a gravity of its own. It felt alive in his hand, humming with a silent, potent frequency. It was not a comforting gift. It was a sentence. A responsibility.

“The advice that accompanies it,” Iain said, locking eyes with him, “is this: Do not seek to fight the chaos. You cannot. It is the void from which the pattern was sung. Your only task is to mend the melody. Cut away what corrupts it. Then let the land’s own song heal the rest. The knife is the scalpel. You are not the surgeon; you are the hand of the surgeon, who is this living world.”

Thorne rewrapped the knife, the weight of it profound against his chest as he tucked it into his jacket. He had asked for a map, for clues. He had been given a purpose, and a terrifying tool. He was no longer an academic following a trail. He was being conscripted into a silent, ancient war of preservation.

### **IV**

Ruairidh returned in the deep night, his clothes beaded with cold moisture. He said nothing of where he had been. He simply stood before Thorne, his grey eyes weary.

“The wind from the east carries a taste of rust and rot,” the old man said. “The tear your brother sought is there. In the place called An Sròn Dubh—the Black Nose. It is a cliff that faces nothing but the empty sea and the old dark. A place the pattern has always struggled to hold. The wound there is now active. It is calling to the lost, the angry, the hungry things. It is spreading its stain.”

He placed a small pouch in Thorne’s hand. It contained a mix of dried leaves and crumbled lichen. “When the whispers come, burn a pinch of this. It is made of clarity—juniper, bell heather, lungwort. It will not protect you, but it will help you remember your own name, and the taste of the air before the wound. It is a anchor for your mind.”

Thorne looked from the pouch to the old keeper’s face. The threshold was here, now, in the smoky cottage. To leave was to turn his back on the last outpost of understanding and walk directly toward the source of the sickness. To use the tools and lessons not in theory, but in the raw, infected flesh of the world.

He thought of Alasdair, not as the frustrating older brother, but as a man drowning in a sea of echoes he could not comprehend. He thought of the humming fulcrum stone, the beautiful, terrible balance it represented. He thought of the obsidian knife, a sliver of primordial order, resting against his heart.

The decision was not a dramatic leap. It was a quiet settling, a final alignment. He had come to find a man and a myth. He would go forward to mend a tear in the fabric of being.

“I will go to An Sròn Dubh,” Thorne said, his voice steady in the quiet room.

Ruairidh nodded, a look of profound sorrow and faint pride in his ancient eyes. “Then you cross the threshold. Go with the dawn. Iain will take you to the edge of the high moor. From there, you walk alone. The wounded place will tolerate no other presence.”

The night was endless. Thorne sat by the dying fire, the knife and the pouch on his lap. He ran his fingers over the carvings on the practice stone one last time, feeling not just grooves, but the ghost of intention. He was afraid. But the fear was clean, sharp, like the obsidian—a tool in itself.

When the first grey light touched the loch, turning its surface to milk, he stood. Iain was already at the boat. No words were exchanged. Ruairidh stood in the doorway of the cottage, a silhouette against the fire’s last embers. He lifted a hand, not in farewell, but in a gesture of blessing, or perhaps of transfer.

Thorne stepped into the boat.

Iain rowed. The mist swallowed the cottage, the loch, the safe harbour of knowledge. When the boat ground onto the opposite shore, Thorne climbed out. Iain did not follow. He simply pointed east, toward a rising slope of heather and broken rock that vanished into a ceiling of low, sluggish cloud.

“The moor will test you,” Iain said, his first words of the morning. “It will show you what you are made of, before the cliff does. Listen to it. Even its cruelty is part of the pattern.”

Thorne adjusted the pack on his shoulders, felt the weight of the knife. He met Iain’s gaze and nodded once. Then he turned and began to climb.

The heather tugged at his boots. The wind, sharp and smelling of salt and peat, pushed against him. With every step away from the loch, the world seemed to grow quieter, not in peace, but in a watchful, waiting tension. The comforting chorus of relationships Ruairidh had described felt distant here, muted. The pattern was thin.

He reached the crest of the rise and looked back. The glen was gone, hidden by the mist and the fold of the land. Ahead lay the high moor—a vast, treeless expanse of ochre grass, black peat hags, and pools of water the colour of tarnished lead. And beyond, at the moor’s far edge, he could just make out a stark, black line against the grey sky: a cliff, cutting off the world.

An Sròn Dubh. The Black Nose.

He took a deep breath of the cold, metallic air. He touched the leather-wrapped bundle at his chest. Then he stepped onto the moor proper.

The ground, which had been firm at the loch’s edge, immediately became treacherous. The thick, springy turf gave way without warning to sucking, black pools of peat slurry. He had to move from tussock to tussock, his progress a slow, zigzagging dance. The wind was constant, a low moan that seemed to come from the ground itself. The cloud ceiling pressed down, erasing all horizon, leaving him in a sphere of grey and brown.

An hour in, the whispers began.

At first, he thought it was the wind, tricking his ears. A sibilant rush that almost formed words. Then he heard his name. Not shouted, but breathed from behind a peat hag. He spun, heart hammering. Nothing but waving grass.

*Thorne…*

It came from his left. A voice like dry reeds rubbing together.

*Alone… you are alone…*

It was not the voice of the moor. It was something using the moor’s voice. A parasitic thread. The infection, sensing his presence. The cold he remembered from the Glen of Shadows began to seep into his bones, a deep, marrow ache.

He fumbled for Ruairidh’s pouch, his fingers numb. He scraped a pinch of the mixture into his palm and, sheltering it from the wind with his body, struck a match. The herbs caught, flaring with a sharp, clean, aromatic smoke that smelled of sun on pine and high, clear meadows. He inhaled it deeply.

The whispering didn’t stop, but it receded, becoming background noise again. The cold in his bones eased a fraction. His own mind, which had begun to fog with a creeping despair, cleared. *Remember your name. Remember the air before the wound.*

He trudged on, the ritual of focus and resistance becoming his new rhythm. The moor was endless. Time lost meaning. His world shrank to the next tussock, the next breath of clarity-smoke, the weight of the knife.

As the bleak, unchanging light began to fade towards a darker grey, he saw it. A change in the texture of the land ahead. The peat hags and pools ended abruptly at a line of scree—jagged, broken stone that sloped downward. And beyond that line, there was nothing. Only a vast, empty greyness.

He had reached the edge.

Cautiously, he picked his way across the scree. The stones clattered and shifted underfoot, a harsh, grinding sound. The wind here was no longer a moan, but a shriek, funnelled up from the void. He reached the final lip of land.

An Sròn Dubh was not just a cliff. It was an amputation. The land sheared away in a sheer, black wall of rock that plunged down into a seething, white mist hundreds of feet below. There was no sight of the sea, only that boiling, silent cloud. The cliff face was unnaturally smooth, as if scoured by something other than weather. And the sense of attention he had felt at the fulcrum stone was here a thousandfold, multiplied into a predatory, hungry focus. This was not a place listening. This was a place *consuming*.

This was the tear.

And there, on a narrow, precarious ledge about twenty feet down the cliff face, was a splash of colour that did not belong. A torn shred of bright, modern Gore-Tex, snagged on a rock. Blue. The colour of his brother’s jacket.

Thorne’s breath caught. The whispers surged in triumph, a cacophony in his mind. *He is here… he is below… come… come…*

The hook was set. The threshold was not just crossed; it lay gaping before him. To find Alasdair, alive or dead, he would have to descend into the mouth of the wound.

He knelt on the cold, hard edge of the world, the shrieking wind tearing at his clothes, and looked down into the silent, mist-choked abyss. The obsidian knife felt suddenly very small in his hand.



---

# Chapter 5

## **Chapter 5**

The wind was a blade, honed on the edge of the world. It did not blow; it sliced, shearing away thought, leaving only the raw, animal imperative of the rock beneath his palms and the void beneath his boots. Thorne had descended. There was no other word for it. Not climbed, not traversed. He had descended into the tear.

The cliff was not natural. Up close, the smoothness was an obscenity. No cracks welcomed fingers, no ledges offered rest. It was a wall of polished jet, cold as a tombstone and just as forgiving. He found his way by fault lines, hair-thin fractures in the stone’s perfection, by the occasional cruel jut of rock that seemed less a hold and more a tooth. The obsidian knife, tucked back into his belt, was useless here. His tools were his own breaking strength, the grinding of his boots on slick stone, the shriek of the wind that sought to pluck him from the face and cast him into the white silence below.

The mist did not rise. It hung, a ceiling of churning milk perhaps fifty feet beneath the cliff’s rim, and as he lowered himself towards it, the world contracted. The grey sky vanished, replaced by the oppressive, luminous grey of the cloud. The wind’s scream muted, swallowed by the dense vapour until it became a damp, directionless sigh. The last sound from the world above was the final clatter of a dislodged stone, which fell into the mist and made no sound of impact.

Then his boot touched not rock, but slope.

He lowered himself, trembling from strain, onto a surface of loose, dark scree that slid and whispered under his weight. He was on a shelf, or the top of a colossal fan of debris, hidden from above by the mist-curtain. The air was still and cold, heavy with the smell of wet stone and something else—a sharp, mineral tang, like ozone after a lightning strike, but older, deeper.

The cliff face was gone. Behind him, the wall of jet vanished upward into the gloom. Before him, the scree slope descended at a steep angle, disappearing into the thicker mist below. This was not the bottom. This was the throat.

Thorne stood, his legs unsteady, and looked at the scrap of blue Gore-Tex clutched in his left hand. He had torn it from the snag on his way down. It was a cheap, modern fabric, alien in this primeval gullet. Proof. Alasdair had come this way. The whispers had fallen silent, not in retreat, but in a watchful hush, as if waiting for him to move deeper into a trap already sprung.

He began to pick his way down the slope. The scree was treacherous, each step an avalanche of black shale that hissed like a nest of snakes. The mist thickened, coalescing into a damp veil that beaded on his wool and skin. Light did not fade so much as change its nature; it emanated from the mist itself, a sourceless, shadowless grey that flattened the world into two dimensions. There were no landmarks, only the gradient of the slope and the occasional jagged spine of bedrock thrusting through the shale like a forgotten rib.

Time, which had lost meaning on the moor, ceased to exist entirely. There was only the next step, the next sliding stone, the next breath of metallic air. His mind, starved of reference, began to cast about for patterns in the formless grey. He saw shapes in the tendrils of mist: a reaching hand, a hunched back, the curve of a watching eye. They dissolved when he stared, only to reform in his periphery. The whispers remained quiet, but the silence they left was worse. It was a listening silence.

He did not know how long he had descended when the slope began to level. The scree gave way to firmer ground—a damp, peaty soil littered with stones. And there, the first thing that was not stone or mist or slope.

A wall.

It was low, perhaps knee-high, and crudely made of the same black shale, but it was undeniably a construction. The stones were stacked, one upon the other, in a rough, linear curve. It was not a barrier; it was a boundary. Thorne stopped, his breath loud in the muffled air. He knelt, the cold of the peat seeping through his trousers. The wall was ancient. Lichen, not the vibrant green of the surface but a leprous, grey-white growth, crusted the stones. It ran left and right until it faded into the mist in both directions.

Civilization. Or its ghost.

He stepped over it.

The ground changed instantly. The random litter of stones was gone. The earth was dark, almost black, and unnaturally even. A path, worn by feet or by time, led forward into the gloom. He followed it, the hair on his neck rising. The mist began to thin, not lifting, but pulling back like a theatre curtain, revealing the set.

He stood at the edge of a dead forest.

They were pines, or had been once. Now they were skeletons. Their trunks, stripped of bark, were bone-white and smooth, twisted into agonised contortions as if they had frozen in a final, silent scream. No branches remained, only sharp, splintered stumps. They stood in rows, perfectly spaced, a plantation of the dead. The ground between them was bare, swept clean of needle or debris, just the naked black earth. The path led straight into their midst.

Thorne walked into the forest of bones. The silence here was absolute. No wind sighed through the stumps. No creature scurried in the dirt. The only sound was the soft crush of his own footsteps. The trees watched him with their knot-hole eyes. The air grew colder. The ozone-tang was stronger here, mixed with the faint, sweet-sour smell of old rot.

He was perhaps a hundred yards into the wood when he saw the first house.

It was a *bothan*, a shepherd’s shelter, but wrong. Its low walls were made of the same bone-white timber as the trees, lashed together with withes of blackened heather. Its roof was a thatch of grey lichen. It had no door, only a low, dark opening. It stood empty, a perfect, terrible replica of a human thing, built from the corpse of this forest. As he passed it, he felt a pressure, a focused emptiness from that dark doorway, as if something had just stepped back out of sight.

There were more. They dotted the dead wood, some small, some larger, all built of the same blanched, skeletal timber. A village. A settlement of ghosts. This was no wild place. This was a curated desolation. A civilization of absence.

The path ended at a clearing.

In the centre of the clearing stood a tree larger than the others. It was not dead. It was *un*dead. Its trunk was a column of the same polished black stone as the cliff, but veined with pulsating threads of silver, like slow lightning captured in obsidian. From its branches hung not leaves, but shapes. Curved, pale, and brittle. They were antlers. Dozens of them, from tiny spikes to great, palmated spreads, all bleached white and silent. They clattered softly against one another in a breeze that did not touch Thorne’s face.

Beneath the tree, seated on a stool of fused stone, was a man.

He was dressed in rags that might once have been fine wool, now the colour of moss and earth. His hair was long and white, merging with a beard that fell over his chest. His hands, resting on his knees, were large and knotted, the hands of a worker. He was looking at the ground, motionless.

Thorne stopped at the edge of the clearing. His every instinct screamed to turn, to flee this crafted, waiting silence. But the hook was in his soul. He took a step forward. The peat under his boot sighed.

The man’s head lifted.

His face was old, but not with the softness of age. It was worn, like river stone, sharp and austere. His eyes were the colour of the mist—a pale, luminous grey, without pupil or white. They fixed on Thorne, and there was no welcome in them, only a cold, assessing recognition.

“You are not the one who belongs here,” the man said. His voice was dry, the sound of shale sliding over shale. It held no echo, absorbed by the dead wood.

Thorne’s own voice felt thick, disused. “I am looking for my brother.”

“A brother.” The man’s mist-eyes drifted to the antler tree. “A young stag. Yes. He passed this way. He did not stop to talk. He was… bleeding light. In a hurry to reach the heart of the wound.” His gaze returned to Thorne. “You are bleeding only confusion. And a little courage. It is not the same currency.”

“Where is he?”

“Below.” The man gestured with a slight tilt of his chin towards the far side of the clearing, where the path seemed to continue into deeper mist. “Where the land remembers how to hate.”

“Who are you?” Thorne asked.

A smile, thin and humourless, touched the man’s lips. “I was the Keeper of the Gate. Now I am the Rememberer of the Threshold. I keep the accounts.” He leaned forward slightly. “Your brother passed. He paid no toll. The debt stands.”

Thorne’s hand drifted to the knife at his belt. “What toll?”

“The story,” the man said, as if it were obvious. “This is a place of forgetting. The mist drinks memory. The soil consumes it. To pass, you must leave a memory behind. A strong one. A true one. It becomes part of the landscape. Your brother had none to spare. He was all one memory, a single, burning thread. He could not unpick it. So he ran.” The Rememberer’s blank eyes seemed to sharpen. “You are different. You are a tangled skein. You have memories to spare. Leave one with me. Then you may follow his trail.”

Thorne stared. The request was absurd, monstrous. “I won’t give you my memories.”

“Then you will stay here,” the man said, settling back on his stool. “You will walk the dead wood. You will forget why you came. You will forget your name. In time, you will sit on a stool of your own, and wait for the next confused soul to wander by. This is the first rule here: nothing is free. Not even passage. Especially not passage.”

This was the test. Not of strength, but of self. Thorne felt the truth of it. The path behind him felt closed, the mist a solid wall. The dead houses watched. The ordinary rules—of bargaining, of force, of reason—were grains of sand in this place. They held no weight.

“How?” The word was torn from him.

“Think of the memory. Hold it in your mind, clear and bright. Then speak it aloud to me. I will take it. You will feel it… go.”

Thorne’s mind recoiled. Which memory? A happy one? To leave joy here, in this desolation? A painful one? To be rid of a burden? But the pain was his, a landmark in his own interior map. To lose it would be to lose his bearings. He thought of Alasdair, laughing on the boat, the sun on the water. No. That was not his to give away. He thought of the cold phone, the static hiss. That was a wound, but it was *his* wound.

The Rememberer watched, patient as stone.

A memory surfaced, unbidden. Not of Alasdair, but of the world above. A simple thing. It was early morning, last autumn. He was on the high moor above his cottage, repairing a stone dyke. The dawn mist was burning off, and a shaft of sunlight struck the heather, setting a million dewdrops ablaze. For a moment, the world was made of fire and diamond. He had stopped, his hands sore and cold, and simply breathed. The peace of it. The utter, unasked-for gift of it. A moment of pure, unowned beauty. His. His alone.

It was a small memory. But it was complete. A perfect, self-contained pearl of light.

He closed his eyes. He built the memory in his mind: the smell of damp peat and cold stone, the ache in his lower back, the sudden, shocking gold on the purple heather, the silence that was not empty but full. He held it until it shimmered.

He opened his eyes and looked at the Rememberer. “I was on the moor. The sun caught the dew on the heather. It was all on fire. Just for a moment.”

He spoke the words. As the last syllable left his lips, he felt a *twist*, a soft, internal rupture, like a suture giving way. The memory did not fade. It was there, but it was no longer his. It was a story he had read about someone else. The emotional weight, the visceral truth of it, was gone. A cold, hollow space existed where it had been.

The Rememberer’s eyes glowed faintly. On the black earth at his feet, a single, tiny shoot of heather pushed through the soil. It was a sickly grey-green, and at its tip, a dewdrop caught the sourceless light and glimmered with a dull, captive fire.

“The toll is paid,” the Rememberer said. He gestured again towards the far path. “You may pass.”

Thorne felt sick, violated. He had been carved. A part of his interior world now existed here, in this external nightmare. He stumbled past the antler tree, not looking at the man on the stool. As he reached the far side of the clearing, the Rememberer’s dry voice followed him.

“A word of grace, for your payment. The rules change further down. Memory is the currency here, at the threshold. Deeper, where your brother ran, the currency is… sharper. They will not ask for a story. They will take the teller.”

Thorne did not turn back. He plunged into the mist beyond the clearing, his heart a cold stone in his chest. The dead wood fell away. The ground became rocky again, but now it was carved into shallow, regular steps—a staircase made by no human hand, descending into the belly of the tear.

The mist grew warm. The ozone smell intensified, now underlaid with a sulphurous, organic stench, like a bog breathing. The light dimmed from grey to a deep, bruised purple. The staircase wound down between walls of wet, black rock that gleamed as if sweating.

He heard water. Not the cheerful chatter of the Allt Coire na Saobhaidh, but a slow, thick, rhythmic sound. A drip. A plash. A swallow.

The staircase opened onto a ledge overlooking a cavern so vast its ceiling and far walls were lost in a haze of warm, vapourous darkness. Below him, perhaps another hundred feet down a rough slope of stalagmite teeth, was the source of the light and the sound.

A loch. It filled the cavern floor, its waters utterly black and perfectly still. It was not water, Thorne realized. It was too viscous, too reflective—a sheet of polished obsidian. And from the cavern ceiling high above, things fell into it. They were slow, gelatinous drops of a faint, phosphorescent green liquid. They fell with that same thick *plash*, and where they struck the black surface, they did not ripple. They spread in a momentary, sickly bloom of light before being absorbed. Each bloom illuminated, for a heartbeat, the far shore.

There was a beach there. Of black sand. And on the beach, things moved.

They were humanoid, but elongated, stooped. They moved with a slow, shambling gait. They were building. Piling up the black sand into mounds, into low walls, into shapes that hinted at arches and columns, only to have them collapse, or be kicked apart by other figures. It was a futile, endless labour under the slow, dripping rain of green light. A civilization of ants in a poisoned jar.

And there, on the near shore, directly below Thorne’s ledge, was a figure that was not shambling. It stood tall and still, facing the black water. It was draped in a cloak of what looked like woven shadows. Its head was raised, not towards the futile city, but up, towards the ledge where Thorne stood.

It had been waiting for him.

Thorne froze. This was the guardian. Not a passive rememberer, but an active sentinel. The way forward was across that loch, or along its shore. The sentinel blocked it.

As he watched, the figure below turned. It had no face. Where a face should be, there was a smooth, dark concavity, like the inside of a mask. It raised an arm, a long, pale limb that seemed too jointed, and pointed directly at him.

No voice sounded, but words formed in Thorne’s mind, cold and clear as ice-water trickling into his ear.

*You are not the lost one. You are the follower. You carry his scent. You carry the iron. You may not pass.*

Thorne’s hand went to the obsidian knife. Iron. The old stories whispered in his mind. The Good Folk. The aversion to iron. This thing knew what he carried.

*The knife is a key,* the thoughts pressed in. *It is a tooth of the world above. It may not enter the heart. Cast it into the deep water. Then you may descend. You may join the search in the dark.*

The command was absolute. The figure did not move, but Thorne felt its will like a pressure on his skin. It was offering him a choice: relinquish his only weapon, his only piece of the familiar world, or be barred. This was the trade. Not memory, but power. Not a story, but the means to shape one.

He looked at the black, silent loch. He looked at the futile, labouring figures on the far shore. He thought of Alasdair, “bleeding light,” running ahead into this. His brother had no such knife to give up. What had he done?

The sentinel’s will intensified, a silent shove against his mind. *Cast it.*

Thorne’s fingers tightened on the haft. The knife was cool, solid. A truth in a world of lies. He saw the dewdrop heather, grey and captive. He felt the hollow where his memory had been. This place took. It consumed. It would not stop until he was empty, until he was another shambling builder on the dark shore.

He would not give it the knife.

He took a step back from the ledge’s lip.

The reaction was instantaneous. The sentinel’s form seemed to blur. It did not climb the slope; it *flowed* up it, a smear of darkness against the wet rock, terrifyingly fast. A soundless roar of negation filled Thorne’s skull.

He turned and ran.

He fled back up the carved staircase, his boots slipping on the slick stone. The purple gloom closed around him. He could hear no pursuit, but he could feel it—a chilling absence at his back, drinking the sound of his flight. The sentinel was not chasing him. It was *erasing* the space behind him.

He burst back into the clearing of the antler tree. The Rememberer was gone. The stool of stone sat empty. The tiny, grey heather shoot was already withering, its captive dewdrop extinguished.

Thorne did not stop. He pelted through the dead forest, the bone-white trees a blur. The mist welcomed him back, cold and thick. He found the low wall, leaped over it, and scrambled back up the endless scree slope, his lungs burning. The mist thinned. The wind returned, not as a blade, but as a wail of protest.

He climbed. He did not know how. His hands were raw, his muscles screamed. He found the hairline fractures in the jet cliff and hauled himself upward, an animal fleeing a trap. The luminous mist receded below him. The wind’s shriek grew louder, cleaner.

He collapsed over the rim of the world, onto the solid scree of the upper slope, gasping the thin, sharp air of the moor. He rolled onto his back, staring up at the featureless grey sky. He was shaking, not from cold, but from the unravelling of his soul.

He had reached the edge. He had descended into the tear. He had paid a toll in a memory. He had seen the sentinel of the deep. And he had run.

He had not found Alasdair. He had found only the machinery of the place, a civilization of consumption built from the bones of nature and memory. And he understood now, lying broken on the lip of the abyss, that to go back down would require more than courage. It would require becoming something less than himself, piece by piece.

The obsidian knife was still in his belt. He drew it. It looked like a child’s toy, a sliver of night against the grey. It was all he had.

From below, through the tear in the world, no sound rose. But the sense of attention returned, not hungry now, but satisfied. It had tasted him. It knew what he was made of. And it knew he would be back.

The hook was still set. It was just buried deeper, in a darker, more vital place. Thorne closed his eyes, and in the hollow where the memory of fire and dew had been, he felt the first cold, hard seed of a new resolve begin to form. It had no light. It was all edges. It was the only thing he had left.

He would need a different currency.



---

# Chapter 6

## **Chapter 6**

The moor was a liar. It offered the sky, a vast and empty mercy after the choked mist of the tear, but its solidity was a thin crust over a deeper hunger. Thorne walked. The wind, which had been a blade on the ascent, was now a constant, probing finger, seeking the cracks in him. It whistled through the gaps in his ribs where the memory had been torn out. He felt lighter, hollowed, a bell with no clapper. The cold seed of his new resolve sat in that hollow, a knot of black frost.

He followed no path. Paths were for places that remembered purpose. Here, the land was a rumpled grey blanket, stitched with dead heather and outcrops of shattered granite that thrust up like rotten teeth. The only constant was the distant, saw-toothed rim of mountains, purple against the grey, holding up the sky. They were not his mountains. They were the bones of this place, bare and sharp, and they watched him with a passive, geologic contempt.

He walked for hours, or days. Time had unspooled in the tear. The sun, a pale coin behind the cloud-scrim, did not move so much as fade and re-emerge, bleached of all warmth. His body moved with a mechanical certainty he did not feel. His hands, raw from the climb, had stiffened into claws. He kept one on the hilt of the obsidian knife. It was the only truth he carried.

The first change was the smell. Not peat, not pine. A mineral sharpness, like struck flint and cold ash. Then the sound: a low, grinding hum, so deep it was felt in the back teeth before it was heard. It was not the burn’s complaint. It was the sound of stone being persuaded.

He crested a rise of slick, grassless earth and saw the valley.

It was not a glen. It was a wound. Where the hills should have curved in a maternal cup, they had been sheared away, quarried into terraced steps of赤裸的, pale stone. The terraces were geometric, precise, an obscene geometry imposed upon the land’s flesh. Upon them stood the civilization.

It was not a town of wood and thatch. It was a city of shaped rock and captured light. Towers, not grown from the earth but extruded from it, rose in slender, spiralling needles of a substance like smoked quartz. They caught the feeble light and held it, glinting dully, like the eyes of a dead fish. Between them, low buildings hunched, their walls made of interlocking blocks of the same pale stone as the quarries, fitted without mortar. Roads, straight and grey, cut the valley floor into a grid. No smoke rose from hearths. No movement stirred the streets that he could see. Only the deep, grinding hum, emanating from somewhere deep in the valley’s heart.

Civilization. But it was not a gathering. It was a consumption, rendered permanent. It had not built with the land; it had built *from* it, and the land was left as a scar, a negative space. Thorne stood on the lip of the wound, the wind plucking at his clothes. The satisfied attention from the tear was gone. In its place was a different pressure: a vast, indifferent focus, like the gaze of a spider at the centre of a mineral web. This was the source. This was the machine that fed on the tear’s strange harvest.
