And they fled into the singing, fractured dark.



---

# Chapter 10

## **Chapter 10**

The water was not water. It was the Citadel’s confession, screamed into the void.

Vail led, his boots finding purchase on the spray-slick ledge by touch and by memory of older, darker places. The alloy gate roared on their left, a mouth vomiting its luminous, poisoned history. The air was a cold slurry of mist and sound. Whispers condensed on the rockface, beading and running like sweat. A woman’s sigh became the hiss of spray; a fragment of a lullaby twisted into the gurgle of a whirlpool forming and dying in the blackness below. They were not escaping a place. They were fleeing a becoming.

The pursuit’s tread, a crisp, metallic punctuation to the water’s chaos, did not fade. It found the rhythm of the tunnel, using it as a drum. It was the sound of order insisting upon itself, even here, in the gut where order had curdled.

Kael was a shadow at Vail’s shoulder, his breath ragged but his movements fluid, a man shaped by vertical drops and uncertain stone. Torvin brought up the rear, his bulk a wall between them and the tunnel’s mouth. The old man did not hurry. He moved with the grim, tectonic certainty of a slope deciding to slide.

The ledge narrowed. The alloy lip of the gate gave way to naked, drilled rock, weeping mineral tears. The roar of the outflow was joined by another sound—the wind of the outside world, funnelled through a deep cleft. It was a cleaner sound, but it carried the water’s ghosts now, scattering them into the open air.

Ahead, the promised maintenance platform emerged from the gloom, a rusted iron web bolted to the cliff face. Beyond it, the escarpment fell away into the gloaming, a sheer sweep of basalt and stubborn, wind-gnawed pine. Freedom was a geography of verticals and cuts.

They scrambled onto the platform, its grating shivering under their weight. For a moment, they were suspended between the Citadel’s weeping throat and the vast, indifferent throat of the glen. The sky was the colour of a fresh bruise, the first hard stars pricking through.

Vail looked back. In the arched mouth of the outflow tunnel, shadows coalesced. Not the vague shapes of pursuit, but defined, angular forms backlit by a cold, blue-white light. Curators. They carried no torches. The light came from slender rods in their hands, casting long, sharp shadows that seemed to cut the rock itself. They moved onto the ledge without hesitation, their boots sure on the slime. They were not hunters tracking prey. They were surgeons, entering to remove a flaw.

“The descent,” Kael hissed, pointing to a series of rusted iron rungs bolted into the rock, leading down from the platform’s edge into a chimney of stone. “It goes. For a while.”

“They will follow,” Torvin rumbled, his eyes on the advancing light. “They are not men who understand ‘too far’. The Citadel’s reach is a law they carry in their bones.”

“Then we break the ladder,” Vail said.

But as he moved toward the edge, the world shifted. Not the world of rock and air, but the world within the grain. A pressure, a silent, harmonic shriek. The shard in his pack, the heart of the flawed crystal, did not vibrate. It *pulled*. It was a toothache in the substance of things. The Curators’ rods flared brighter in response. They were not just tracking footprints. They were tracking the flaw’s resonance, the dissonant song in the stone.

They could not outrun a song.

Kael saw his understanding. “The stone. They’re tuned to it.”

Vail’s hand went to the pack. The shard was cold, then hot, a cycle like a sick heartbeat. It was a beacon. To escape, he would have to silence it. Or use it.

The first Curator reached the end of the ledge, ten yards from the platform. The man’s face was obscured by a close-fitting helm, his eyes two pools of reflected rod-light. He did not speak. He raised his free hand, palm out. A gesture of arrest. Of recall. The air between them thickened, shimmering like heat haze off summer stone. The roar of the water muted, became distant. The world was being simplified, edited down to a command: *Be still.*

Vail felt the pressure on his skin, a phantom hand pressing him to the grating. Kael grunted, his knee buckling. Torvin planted his feet, a low growl building in his chest, a sound that belonged to bears and landslides.

The flaw-shard flared in answer. Not with light, but with a *wrongness*. A memory that was not a memory: the taste of copper, the smell of frost on heather, the sight of a stag’s eye going dark—Iain’s memory, bleeding through. The Citadel’s ordered extraction, the clean separation of memory from stone, was undone here. This shard wept its stolen life raw.

The shimmering air rippled. The Curator’s head tilted, a minute gesture of recalibration. The pressure stuttered.

“Now!” Vail snarled, and threw himself toward the ladder.

***

The descent was a fall with pauses. The iron rungs were rust-scaled, treacherous. Some spun in their sockets. Others were mere stubs. Below, the chimney opened onto a steep scree slope that vanished into the dark of the forest floor, hundreds of feet down. The wind here was a knife, sharp with the promise of winter and distance.

Above, the blue-white light bloomed on the platform. A Curator leaned out, sighting down. He did not shout a warning. He simply lifted his rod.

Vail felt it before he saw it—a thread of resonant force, a plucked string tuned to the shard’s dissonance. It was not aimed at him. It was aimed at the rockface beside him.

The basalt, ancient and solid, *sang*. A single, piercing note. And where it sang, it cracked. A spider-web of fractures exploded across the cliff face with a sound like snapping bones. The rung under Vail’s left foot sheared clean away.

He dropped, his stomach lurching into his throat. His right hand held, the jolt tearing through his shoulder. He swung, dangling over the gulf. The pack with the shard slammed into the rock, a dull *thud* that echoed the stone’s song.

Kael, two rungs below, reached up a desperate hand. “Here!”

Another note. This one lower, a cello’s groan of stress. A section of the cliff the size of a croft house bulged, groaned, and began to slide. Not a collapse, but a deliberate, terrible shedding. The Curators weren’t trying to kill them. They were surgically removing their path.

“Jump!” Torvin roared from above. He was not on the ladder. He stood on the platform’s edge, facing the light.

Vail looked down. The next secure rung was five feet below and to the left. A leap of faith over a void that ended in shattered stone. He looked at Kael’s outstretched hand, at the terror in the younger man’s eyes. It was not terror of the fall. It was terror of the sound, the unmaking of the mountain’s voice.

He let go.

The world was air and roar. His hand slapped against cold, wet iron. His fingers closed. He held. His body slammed into the rock, driving the breath from him. Above, the sliding section of cliff face met the scree slope with a thunder that was not sound but feeling, a deep shockwave in the earth. A cloud of dust and debris bloomed upward, swallowing the platform, the Curators, and Torvin.

“Torvin!” Kael screamed into the boiling grey.

No answer came but the settling rumble of stone.

The ladder below was gone, buried. They were stranded on a vertical face, the cliff above sheared smooth, the drop below now a shorter, but still fatal, leap onto a fresh tumble of jagged rock.

The dust began to clear. The blue-white light was gone. The platform was a mangled twist of iron, half-buried under rubble. Of the Curators, there was no sign. And of Torvin, there was only silence.

Vail pressed his forehead against the cold stone. The old man’s sacrifice was not a dramatic leap. It was a stand. A refusal to be chased. He had become the obstacle, and the mountain had answered in kind.

“He cleared the path,” Vail said, his voice raw with dust and something else.

“He *is* the path,” Kael whispered, the truth a cold stone in his mouth.

They hung there, two insects on a wall. The shard in Vail’s pack was quiet now, as if shocked by the violence. The direct pursuit was buried. But the Citadel’s reach was long. They had to move.

To the east, the cliff face was fractured, a series of cracks and ledges leading diagonally down toward a ribbon of black that was the Allt Coire na Saobhaidh, swollen with the Citadel’s weeping outflow. It was not a route. It was a suggestion.

“We climb,” Vail said.

***

The stone accepted them, but it did not welcome them. This was the raw flesh of the mountain, unmediated by drill or charge. It was treacherous. Handholds crumbled. Ledges proved to be dust on slanting shelves. They moved like blood moving through a wound, slow, persistent, leaving a trace of their passage in scrapes and smears of blood on grey rock.

Hours bled together. The bruise of the sky deepened to black, the stars hardening into icy points. The cold became a living thing, gnawing at fingers and toes. The roar of the burn grew louder, a constant, chiding presence below.

Vail’s mind, honed by the Citadel’s geometries, began to read the cliff differently. He did not see just rock. He saw tension and compression, the memory of ancient fires and slow, patient ice. The flaw-shard’s resonance was quiet, but his perception was now tuned to a different frequency—the frequency of fracture, of flaw. He found the hidden lines, the hairline cracks that spoke of deeper weakness, of a path through the seeming solidity. He was not climbing a wall. He was navigating the mountain’s silent thoughts of coming apart.

“Left,” he would grunt, his voice a dry rustle. “The dark seam. It goes.”

Kael, the nimble climber, followed not the obvious holds, but Vail’s intuitions. He saw the older man’s transformation. Vail’s eyes were no longer those of a man looking *at* stone, but of a man listening to it. It was unsettling. It was the only thing that kept them alive.

They reached a broad, sloping ledge carpeted in brittle moss just as the moon rose, a sliver of bone behind scudding cloud. The burn was a silver thread thirty feet below, crashing through a narrow chasm. Across the water, the pine forest began, a wall of impenetrable shadow.

They collapsed on the ledge, their bodies trembling with exhaustion and spent adrenaline. The silence here was profound, broken only by the water and the wind. It was the silence of the outside. It should have felt like safety.

Kael broke it. “They’ll send others. They’ll come from below, now. Not behind.”

Vail nodded, sucking air that tasted of pine and cold water. He shrugged off his pack, wincing. He drew out the flaw-shard. In the moonlight, it was a dull, dead thing. A piece of junk. But when he held it, the world subtly bent. The roar of the burn contained, for a fleeting second, a child’s laughter. The wind in the pines sighed a phrase in a language he almost knew. The stone under his palm hummed with a forgotten warmth.

This was the reward. Not an object of power, but a key to a door he could now never close. The world was not stable. It was a palimpsest, bleeding through.

“We can’t take it with us,” he said.

Kael stared. “After all that? Torvin…”

“Torvin died for the flaw, not the shard. The Citadel wants this piece back because it’s proof. Proof their process is a lie. That you cannot cut memory from the world without the world remembering the cut.” He looked across the chasm at the waiting forest. “If we keep it, they will never stop. They will tune their rods to its song and hunt us to the ends of the earth. It’s a compass needle pointing straight to us.”

“So we destroy it.”

“You can’t destroy a memory. You can only scatter it.” Vail stood, walked to the edge of the ledge overlooking the roaring chasm. “The water is already full of them. The ghosts are already loose.”

He understood now the final, desperate requirement. The ally’s sacrifice had cleared the physical path. But the shard’s sacrifice would clear the metaphysical one. To be free of the hunt, they had to give up the prize. They had to return the stolen memory to the stream, to let the wild, chaotic water take it and break it into a thousand indecipherable fragments, to seed it into the mud and the roots and the stones of the riverbed. To re-wild it.

It felt like surrender. It felt like the only true victory.

He held the shard out over the void. It felt heavier now, dense with the weight of a stolen life. Iain MacAnndra’s last moments, the steam rising in spirals, the colour of the pines. A private death turned into public property, and now to be turned into folklore, into the whisper in the reeds.

“Is this not what you wanted?” he asked the stone, or the ghost within it. “To go back to the hill?”

He opened his hand.

The shard fell. It did not glitter. It was a swallowed piece of dark. It struck the silver tumult of the burn and was gone without a splash, absorbed. For a moment, nothing changed.

Then, the water’s roar deepened. Not in volume, but in texture. A new note entered the chorus—a clear, mournful, bell-like tone that was the essence of the high glen, of frost and blood and solemn gratitude. It rose through the chaos, pure and undeniable, holding for three heartbeats before the water shattered it, carrying its pieces away downstream, into the lochs, into the sea.

The beacon was extinguished. The song was scattered. They were, for now, just men.

***

The rest of the descent was a blur of pain and automatic motion. They forded the burn where it shallowed, the water knifing through their clothes, carrying the ghost of that bell-tone around their knees. They stumbled into the tree line, the sudden hush of the pine forest a physical pressure on their ears after the roar of water and wind.

Dawn found them deep in the Glen of Shadows, miles downstream from the Citadel’s hidden vents. They moved through a world they had left behind, but it did not know them anymore.

Vail saw the forest with new eyes. Before, it had been a fact. A dense, passive thing of wood and shadow. Now, it was a conversation. The gnarled root across the path was not an obstacle; it was the land’s memory of a long-ago rockfall, a tendon of old stress. The pattern of lichen on a north-facing stone was a slow, patient record of centuries of wind. The whisper of the canopy was not just air; it was the exhalation of the mountain, a breath carrying the dust of crystals and the echoes of the burn’s new song.

He had gone into the Citadel to learn the grammar of stone. He had learned, instead, that the stone had a voice. And the voice was fractured, and beautiful, and full of grief.

They climbed a rise, breaking from the pines onto a high, open moor of heather and granite outcrop. The sky was the colour of wet slate, the air sharp and clean. Below them, the glen unfolded, a deep-cut wound of green and grey. And there, across the valley, perched on its arrogant shoulder of rock, was the Citadel. From this distance, in the pure morning light, it looked like a natural formation. A glacial erratic of impossible geometry. It did not gleam. It sat. It was a fact.

But Vail could see the flaw in its grain. Not a physical crack, but a metaphysical one. A stillness that was not peace, but extraction. A silence that was not quiet, but emptiness. It was a scar. And from this scar, he knew, the coloured crystals still wept their stolen lives into the dark channels, and the water still carried the ghosts out into the world, and men in helms still moved with crisp, synchronized tread, enforcing a order that was a kind of death.

He was outside it now. He was in the world that was receiving the seepage. He was no longer a Curator, preserving a curated past. He was a man standing in the present, listening to the past as it bled, unstopped, into the future.

Kael stood beside him, shivering in his damp clothes. “What now?”

Vail took a long, slow breath. The air of the glen filled him. It tasted of peat, of decaying pine needles, of cold stone. And underneath it, faint as a dream, of copper and frost.

“Now,” he said, his voice the dry rustle of the heather at their feet, “we learn to listen.”

He turned his back on the Citadel. Before him, the moor rolled away, pathless, towards the higher, wilder mountains. The ordinary world. But it was not ordinary. It was singing, softly, in a thousand fractured, forgotten tongues. The veil was thin here. And he knew, with a certainty that was colder than the dawn, that the Curators would not be the only ones who had heard the shard’s final, bell-like cry. Something else in this old, remembering land had pricked up its ears. The hunt from the Citadel might be blinded. But other hunters, attuned to older laws, might just have been awakened.

The path was gone. The way forward was not a line. It was a field of resonance. He took the first step into it.



---

# Chapter 11

## **Chapter 11**

The burn was talking, but the words were not for him.

Vail knelt on the bank of the Allt Coire na Saobhaidh, his hands submerged to the wrists. The water was a blade of cold, carving the feeling from his flesh. He did not pull them out. He let the current speak against his skin, its thousand tiny pressures a language of friction and flow. It told of deep stones, of peat-stained pools, of the long, slow seep from the high corries where the snow clung in grey, stubborn teeth. And underneath, a thinner, sharper song: the metallic whisper of the Citadel’s weeping, carried league upon league to poison the roots of the moor.

Kael watched from a few yards upstream, chewing on a piece of dried venison. The boy’s eyes were no longer on the horizon, hunting for threats. They were on the water, on the way the light fractured over the stones. He was learning to listen with his eyes. It was a start.

“It’s not a path you find,” Vail said, his voice joining the murmur of the burn. “It’s a path you become. The land lays a thread against your soul. You either feel it, or you are deaf.”

“And if you’re deaf?” Kael asked.

“Then you walk in circles until the weather takes you. Or something else does.”

Vail stood, water sheeting from his hands. He did not shake it off. He let the air take it, a slow surrender. The cold had scoured him clean of the Citadel’s climate, the perpetually tempered, scentless air. Here, the wind had a texture. It carried the memory of rain, the promise of frost, the sigh of a billion pine needles. It was not controlled. It was an exchange.

He looked at his palms. The lines there were no longer the neat, purposeful maps of a Curator’s duties. They were cracked with cold, stained with peat, calloused from stone and rope and the haft of an axe. They were becoming a record of the world, not a manipulation of it.

“We follow the water?” Kael asked.

“We follow the silence in the water,” Vail corrected. “Where the Citadel’s song is loudest, the land is most wounded. The silence will be a scar. We find the scar. We listen to it.”

They moved north and west, away from the arrogant shoulder of rock, into the belly of the glen. The pines gave way to open moor, a vast, hummocky sea of dead heather and bronze grass, stippled with dark, secretive lochans. The sky was a sheet of hammered lead, pressing down. Their progress was not a march. It was a kind of drift, punctuated by long, motionless pauses where Vail would simply stand, eyes closed, head tilted as if catching a distant strain of music. Kael learned to stand still too. He learned that the land’s voice was not in the grand sweep of the wind, but in the gaps between gusts. Not in the cry of the raven, but in the silence that pooled in its wake.

It was during one such pause, in the lee of a weathered tor that rose from the moor like the spine of a buried beast, that the ordinary world presented its test.

The smell reached them first: not the clean rot of the forest floor, but the thick, cloying, iron-sweet stench of fresh slaughter. It lay over the moor like a blanket. A few hundred yards ahead, a rough circle of crofters’ cottages, little more than stone huts with turf roofs, huddled around a byre. The scene was not one of chaos, but of a terrible, precise stillness. No sheep dotted the pasture. No smoke rose from the chimneys. The only movement was the frantic flapping of a torn hide, nailed to the byre door, in the rising wind.

Vail stopped. The resonance here was not a scar. It was a wound, raw and screaming.

“Stay here,” he said, his voice low.

“But—”

“This is not your lesson. This is mine. To see, and not to curate. To witness, and not to annotate. Stay. Be silent.”

He approached alone. The wind, now at his back, pushed the stench into his mouth, his lungs. It was a taste from the oldest part of the brain. Death, and fear, and the end of things.

The first body was by the gate. An old man, his face a mask of bewildered agony, his throat opened not with the neat slit of a knife but with the ragged tear of teeth. His hand still clutched a hay-fork, its tines clean. He had not landed a blow. Around him, in the churned mud, were prints. They were not the shod hooves of stolen livestock. They were pads, large as a man’s spread hand, with the deep, brutal gouges of claws. They led in a deliberate, circling pattern from cottage to cottage.

Vail did not enter the homes. He did not need to. The story was written in the mud, in the spatters on the low stone walls, in the terrible silence that was not peace, but emptiness. The predator had not come for food. It had come for terror. It had killed everything—the sheep in the byre, the dog by the hearth, the crofter and his wife, their two grown sons—and taken almost nothing. A ritual of waste. A statement.

He found the centre of the pattern. In the middle of the small yard, a single stone, flat and moss-covered, had been cleared. Upon it, arranged with a grotesque care, were the hearts of the victims. Seven of them, laid in a spiral. They were not eaten. They were offered. And at the spiral’s centre, pressed into the moss, was another print. This one was different. It was almost human, but the arch was too high, the toes too long, and at the forefront was the deep, unmistakable puncture of a talon.

The silence here was not the Citadel’s extraction. It was a different kind of vacuum. It was a silence that had been *listened to*. Something had heard the shard’s cry, had tasted the Citadel’s poisoned seepage in the water, and had answered. Not with order, but with a older, wilder chaos. A hunter, attuned to older laws.

Vail did not touch the stone. He did not pray. He simply looked, letting the image etch itself onto him. This was the world receiving the seepage. This was the past, bleeding unstopped into the future. It was not a theory in a crystal-lit archive. It was the blood on the moss, the cold little hearts on the stone.

He returned to Kael. The boy took one look at his face and asked no questions. His own had gone the colour of the sky.

“What… what was it?”

“A consequence,” Vail said, the word as dry as stone. “The Citadel thinks it quarantines the past. It does not. It pressurizes it. It creates a fault line. And something always comes up through the fault.” He turned his gaze from the croft to the looming mountains beyond. “It is awake now. And it knows we are here. Our scent is in the stream. Our silence is different from the land’s.”

“We run?”

Vail almost smiled, a crack in a cliff face. “There is no running on a field of resonance. You move with the note, or you break against it.” He looked at his hands again, the Citadel-softness now fully scoured away. “We go to the community. The real one. Not a curated exhibit. The one we left behind.”

***

The village of Achnasaul was not a collection of buildings. It was an outcropping of life, stubborn as lichen on the mountain’s flank. Stone and thatch huddled together for warmth against the wind that funnelled down from the high passes. The air here smelled of woodsmoke, of baking oatbread, of the tangy, alive scent of a midden. It was the smell of persistence.

They saw the fear long before they reached the first house. It hung over the village like the low cloud, a palpable chill. A boy herding geese too close to the houses, his eyes wide. The swift, furtive drawing of a shutter as they passed. The absence of men in the fields.

The conflict came to its head at the smithy. It was the heart of the village, the place where the mountain’s ore was tamed into nail and ploughshare. The great bellows wheezed, but the hammer was still. A dozen men stood in a loose half-circle before the open forge. They were not the Citadel’s synchronized helms. They were farmers and herdsmen, their faces hardened by weather and worry, tools in their hands—axes, hammers, a rusty old sword. And in their centre, facing them, was a man Vail recognized.

Iain MacAnndra stood with his back to the anvil. He was not a large man, but the mountains had carved him, and in the forge-light he looked like a figure from a older tale, his face all planes and shadows, his eyes reflecting the banked fire. He held no weapon. His hands, red and powerful, hung loose at his sides.

“…not a question of courage, Tam,” Iain was saying, his voice the low rumble of stone settling. “It’s a question of sense. You take twenty men into the Coire na Creige with pikes and hayforks, you are not going hunting. You are going to feed it.”

Tam, a broad-shouldered man with a furious red beard, shook his axe. “And we wait here? Like sheep in a pen? It’s taken the MacLennans’ whole stock! It killed old Donnal and his family at the high croft! It’s coming down the glen, Iain! It’s tasting us!”

A murmur of agreement ran through the men. It was the sound of a community fraying at the edges, its shared logic unravelling into blind, collective terror.

“It is not coming down the glen,” Iain said, his voice cutting through the murmur. “It is *herding* us. Can you not see the pattern? It drives the game from the high corries. It strikes the outermost crofts. It leaves its… marks.” He paused, and the memory of the stone with its spiral of hearts passed over his face like a shadow. “It wants us bunched, terrified, ripe. It is not a wolf. It is a thinker.”

“Then what?” another man cried. “Pray? Wait for the Citadel’s men? They’ve never answered a call from the likes of us.”

“The Citadel,” said a new, dry voice from the edge of the crowd, “is the reason it is here.”

All heads turned. Vail stepped into the circle of light. He was a stranger, lean and travel-worn, his clothes not of the village, but the look in his eyes was not that of a outsider. It was the look of a man who has seen the same darkness they had. Kael hovered behind him, a shadow.

Tam brandished his axe. “Who’s this? Another voice for doing nothing?”

“This,” Iain said, his eyes never leaving Vail’s, “is the man who heard the hill sing. Or so my wife’s cousin’s boy claimed before he ran back to his mother’s hearth.” There was no mockery in his tone. It was a statement of fact, awaiting verification.

Vail ignored the axe. He looked at Iain, then at the ring of frightened faces. “The creature is a *cù-sìth*,” he said. The old name fell into the silence like a stone into a pool. “A faery hound. Or what the world now, in its forgetting, might call one. It is a predator of resonance. It feeds on fear, on disorder, on the rupture between the world and itself. The Citadel, with its crystals and its channels, has created the greatest rupture of all. It has torn a scar in the veil. The shard I carried was a piece of that scar. When it broke, it was a bell. The *cù-sìth* heard it. It has come to the wound.”

Tam spat. “Faery tales. We need steel, not stories.”

“You have steel,” Vail said, nodding at the axe. “It did not help the MacLennans. Their steel was clean.” He took a step forward, into the very centre of their fear. “The *cù-sìth* is not of this world, not fully. It walks in the thin places, the tears. Your anger, your blunt courage—that is meat and drink to it. It will pull you into its terrain, where your strength is a phantom, and your fear is a solid thing it can use to break your neck.”

The men shifted, uncertain. The logic of the forge was failing them.

“You have a plan, stranger?” Iain asked quietly.

Vail met his gaze. “To beat a hunter of chaos, you must become utterly, perfectly still. To defeat a consumer of fear, you must feel none. You must not go to it as a pack of snarling dogs. You must go as a single, silent stone. And you must offer it not the meal it wants, but a taste of its own medicine.”

“How?” Tam demanded, but the bluster was leaking from his voice.

“You will stay here,” Vail said to the men. “You will guard your homes. You will light your fires, and you will feel, in your hearts, not terror for yourselves, but a cold focus for the land. You will think of the quiet of the deep stone, the patience of the root. You become the still centre. You deny it the feast.”

“And you?” Iain asked.

Vail looked towards the dark mouth of the glen, where the Coire na Creige lay hidden in cloud. “I will go to the scar. I will listen to the silence it has made. And I will close it.”

“Alone?”

“With him,” Vail said, glancing at Iain. “If he will come. He knows the stone. And the stone is what we must become.”

A long silence stretched, filled only by the sigh of the bellows and the crackle of the forge. Iain looked at his neighbours, at their fear-haunted eyes. He looked at his own hands, the hands that could gentle a calf or strip a stag, the hands that knew the grain of things. He nodded, once.

“Aye,” he said. “I’ll come.”

***

The Coire na Creige was not a place. It was an absence.

They climbed as the light failed, leaving the last stubborn patches of grass for a world of shattered granite and scree that hissed underfoot like broken glass. The air grew thin and sharp. Clouds, cold and personal, wrapped around them, blurring the world to a radius of grey stone and colder mist. Sound died here. The wind’s moan was muffled, sucked into the vast, bowl-shaped hollow of the corrie.

This was the scar. Vail could feel it in his teeth, a high, thin vibration that was the opposite of sound. It was the resonance of the Citadel’s wound, miles away but connected through the bones of the earth like a tuning fork. And here, where the rock was raw and the world thin, the *cù-sìth* had made its lair.

Iain moved like a part of the mountain, each footfall deliberate, each breath measured. He carried a long, old spear, its ash shaft darkened with smoke and oil, its iron head a simple, lethal leaf. He did not speak. He had shed the last of his role as the village’s mediator. He was a hunter now, in the oldest sense.

Vail, too, had shed his final skin. The Curator was gone, buried under peat and blood and the memory of small hearts on a stone. The man who remained was not a hero, not a sage. He was a listener. And he heard the flaw in the world here, the dissonance that the creature was gorging upon. His flaw had always been a desire to preserve, to catalogue, to keep the past in a sterile box. That was the Citadel’s sin in miniature. To be cleansed of it, he had to do the opposite. He had to engage. He had to touch the bleeding edge of the now, and *change* it.

They found the lair where the back wall of the corrie sheared up into a black, dripping cliff. A cave mouth, a ragged tear in the rock, exhaled a breath that stank of old blood and a sweet, rotten perfume that was neither plant nor animal. Before it, the ground was littered with bones, picked clean and splintered. Not just sheep. Fox. Eagle. A shadow that might have been a pine marten. The *cù-sìth* was not just killing for hunger or terror. It was erasing the song of the corrie, note by note, creating a vacuum of silence where only its own resonance could live.

From the darkness within, two points of light ignited. Not the reflected glow of animal eyes. These were a sour, phosphorescent green, like corrupted moss. They held no emotion. Only a vast, attentive hunger.

Iain lowered his spear, planting the butt against the stone. A rock. An unmoving thing.

Vail stepped past him. He held out his empty hands. He did not think of the archives, of the ordered rows of crystals. He thought of the stag in Glen of Shadows, its last breath rising in spirals. He thought of the burn’s constant song. He thought of the stubborn, smoky smell of Achnasaul. He filled himself with the ordinary, persistent truth of the world the Citadel ignored.

The creature flowed out of the cave.

It was larger than a wolf, built on a nightmare’s logic. Its fur was the colour of a bruise, shading to black at its massive paws. Its limbs were too long, its joints bending in ways that whispered of a different geometry. Its head was lean, almost elegant, but its mouth was a gash filled with teeth like shards of smoky quartz. And its eyes were pure, intelligent vacancy. It did not snarl. It assessed.

Vail felt the fear rise, a cold tide in his gut. It was the old fear, the Curator’s fear of the uncatalogued, the unpredictable, the *real*. He let it rise. And then he let it go. He imagined it flowing out of him, down through the soles of his feet, into the patient granite of the mountain. He offered it nothing. He became a vessel of stillness.

The *cù-sìth* paused. Its planned meal—a terrified, screaming mind—was not presenting itself. It tilted its head. The gesture was almost human.

It spoke. Not in words, but in a direct injection of sensation into Vail’s mind: a cacophony of screaming rabbits, the crunch of bone, the suffocating silence after a avalanche, the metallic tang of the Citadel’s poisoned streams. It was offering him a taste of its own reality, the world as pure, consuming disorder.

Vail did not flinch. He reached into his own memory, not the curated ones, but the true ones. He gathered the *shush* of Iain’s knife through the stag’s hide. The spiral of steam in the cold air. The drip of blood on bronze moss. The complaint of the burn over its stones. The feel of cold water on his wrists. The taste of oatbread and woodsmoke. He did not hurl it at the creature. He simply let it exist, a solid, quiet core in the centre of the psychic storm.

The *cù-sìth* recoiled as if struck. The ordinary, the persistent, the *real* was an acid to its nature. It shrieked, a sound that was all in the mind, a needle of pure negation.

It sprang.

Not at Vail, who was now an unappetizing stone, but at Iain, the more familiar profile of a fighting, fearing man.

Iain did not move. He stood behind his spear, his body a continuation of the ash shaft, rooted to the stone. He made no sound. He showed no fear. He was a man performing a single, necessary task: being present.

The creature’s leap, calculated for a dodging, screaming prey, was a fraction off. It did not knock the spear aside. Its own momentum drove the iron leaf deep into the hollow of its shoulder, where the bruise-coloured fur met the unnatural joint.

There was no roar of pain. There was a sound like a rent sheet of velvet, and a burst of that same sweet-rotten scent, overwhelming now. The green eyes flared, not with anger, but with a kind of startled recognition. It twisted in mid-air, a motion of liquid agony, and landed ten feet away, the spear torn from Iain’s grip, the shaft snapping, the head buried in its flesh.

It looked at them, the two silent men, one empty-handed, one now weaponless. It looked at the quiet, solid reality they had become in this place of screamed silence. The intelligence in its eyes flickered, not with defeat, but with a kind of cold, final understanding. It was not defeated by strength, but by irrelevance.

It turned and flowed back into the cave, a shadow bleeding into a greater shadow. The green phosphorescence of its eyes winked out.

The silence that followed was different. It was not the *cù-sìth*’s vacuum. It was the natural, weary silence of the high mountains after a storm. The flaw in the air was gone. The scar, for now, had stopped weeping.

Iain let out a long, shuddering breath. He walked to the broken spear shaft, picked it up, and looked at the splintered end. “It will die in there,” he said, not a question.

“Yes,” Vail said. His own body was trembling with released tension, a vessel that had held too much stillness. “But it understood. This terrain is no longer fertile for it. The feast is over.”

“We should burn the lair.”

“No,” Vail said. “Let the mountain have it back. Let the water seep in, and the roots find the bones. Let it become ordinary again. That is the only victory that lasts.”

They turned and began the long, cold descent from the corrie. Behind them, the cave was just a dark mouth in a cliff, saying nothing.

***

The victory in Achnasaul was quiet, and it cost.

They returned to find the village still standing, still shrouded in fear, but unbreached. The men had held their focus, a fragile, collective act of will that had left them hollow-eyed with exhaustion. The news that the creature was broken, that the corrie was silent, was met not with cheers, but with a profound, weary relief that seeped into the stones of the houses and the packed earth of the yard.

The cost was seen in Iain’s wife, Mairi, when she saw the broken spear in his hand. She said nothing. She pressed her face to his shoulder for one long moment, then turned to stir the broth over the fire. Her silence was the loudest sound in the room. The village’s new order was this: a knowledge that the world beyond their hearths was stranger and wilder than they had allowed themselves to believe, and that their safety was not a right, but a fragile, continuous act of listening and becoming.

For Vail, the cost was a final severance. He stood at the edge of the village at dawn, looking east. Somewhere beyond the horizon, the Citadel sat on its rock, a fact. He felt no pull towards it. It was a museum of ghosts, and he was a man of the whispering, bleeding present. His old self, the Curator, was as dead as the *cù-sìth* in the cave. The man who remained had a purpose, but it was not preservation. It was integration. To listen to the land’s song, and to help mend the tears where the song leaked out.

Kael approached, a pack on his back. “They say the weather will hold,” he said. “For a few days.”

Vail nodded. “You are going back?”

“To my mother’s hearth? No.” The boy looked out at the moor, his eyes no longer those of a frightened fugitive, but of an apprentice. “The burn is still talking. I’m not done learning to listen.”

A ghost of a smile touched Vail’s lips. It was answer enough.

The new order was established. Not a rule, not a system. A practice. A way of moving through a world that was both stone and veil. They would walk the resonance, tend the quiet, and stand as still, solid things against the chaos that fed on noise.

As they left Achnasaul, the first true snow of the season began to fall. It came down in large, soft flakes, muting the world, covering the blood on the moor, gentling the harsh lines of the granite. It was a blanket, a silence of a different, kinder kind.

Vail stopped on a rise and looked back once. The village was a smudge of smoke in the white. Ahead, the mountains vanished into a cloud of falling snow. The path was gone. The way forward was not a line.

He took the first step into it. And as he did, a new sound reached him, faint but clear beneath the hush of the snow. It was not the cry of a shard, nor the psychic shriek of a dying myth. It was the crisp, distant, rhythmic *crunch-crunch-crunch* of marching feet on frozen ground. Not from the Citadel’s direction.

From the north.

A different geometry of sound. A colder, drier kind of order. The Citadel’s hunt might have been blinded, but someone else had been counting, and calculating, and their tally had now come due.

The snow fell, erasing the world behind them. But ahead, the new silence was already broken.



---

# Chapter 12

## **THE STONE AND THE VEIL**

### **Chapter 27**

The stag was dead, but the forest was not done with it.

Vail stood over the carcass, his breath pluming in the iron-cold dawn. It lay at the edge of the Rowan Wood, just where the ancient trees gave way to the scar of the new northern road. The animal was a young hart, its throat torn open not by wolf or wildcat, but by a jagged, rusted snare-wire, half-buried in the leaf-litter. It had thrashed itself to death. The steam rose from the wound, not in a cloud, but in slow, spiralling threads that seemed to hesitate in the still air. It was not the heat of life escaping; it was a question, hanging.

He knelt. His knife, the same grey sliver of old steel Iain MacAnndra had once used to part hide in the Glen of Shadows, was in his hand. The memory was not a ghost; it was a layer in the stone of him. He did not cut. He laid his palm on the stag’s flank, still warm, and listened.

The forest’s song here was a dissonant chord. Beneath the chatter of a wren and the sigh of wind in bare branches, he felt the low, grinding hum of the road’s crushed stone—a dead, geometric frequency. And beneath that, fainter but colder, the rhythmic, metallic pulse he had first heard in the snow a season ago: *crunch-crunch-crunch*. The Northern Collective’s surveyors were methodical, their presence a slow frost, not a fire.

The stag’s death was a punctuation mark. Not an end, but a comma in a sentence spoken between stone and veil.

He worked then, not with the silent efficiency of Iain the hunter, but with a ritual slowness. He cut the wire, coiling its vicious geometry into a pocket. He field-dressed the carcass, his hands sure and red, the only sounds the soft *shush* of blade through tissue, the drip of dark blood on frozen earth. He took the heart and liver, wrapping them in burdock leaves. The rest he left at the tree line, a gift for the forest’s own. A return, not a taking.

This was the practice now. The mending.

***

**Scene 1: The Ordinary World, Changed**

The cottage at Achnasaul was no longer a hermit’s retreat. Smoke, rich with the scent of peat and drying herbs, curled from its chimney. The small byre sheltered a goat and three chickens. A neatly tended plot lay sleeping under a mulch of bracken, waiting for spring.

Inside, the space had been transformed. The Citadel’s sterile maps were gone. In their place, on the rough stone walls, were maps of a different kind: charcoal rubbings of the cup-and-ring marks from the high moor; a chart of local streams annotated not with volume, but with words like “melancholy” or “chattering”; a calendar of phases of the moon alongside notes on where the veil had felt “thin” or “resistant.” It was a lexicon of resonance.

Vail hung the haunch of venison from a rafter. He placed the stag’s heart in a clay bowl on the central table—an altar to a different kind of knowledge. He moved through these actions, these translations of death into continuity, and felt the truth of it in his bones. The Curator was dead. The man who remained was a scribe of the living world, a keeper of the quiet. His ordinary world was this conscious participation. There was no going back to mere observation.

Kael arrived as the short day began to fail, his cheeks ruddy with cold, his eyes bright. He carried a brace of grouse and a small, worried frown.

“The new survey post,” he said, without preamble. “They’ve cleared the hawthorn copse at the Three Sisters. Just… flattened it. They call it ‘line-of-sight clearance.’”

Vail nodded, stirring a broth of roots and the stag’s liver. The rich, iron smell filled the room. “Hawthorn guards the threshold there. The Sisters will be… louder now.”

“I heard them,” Kael said, his voice low. “Like a hum you feel in your teeth. It’s not good.”

“No. It is a wound. A small one.” Vail ladled broth into two bowls. “We will tend it tomorrow. Show it how to scar over.”

This was their work. Not to fight the Collective’s advance with force—that was the Citadel’s old game, noise against noise—but to strengthen the land’s own song around their incisions, to make the veil here resilient, dissonant to their machines. To be a subtle arthritis in the joints of their progress.

As they ate, Vail taught. Not doctrines, but methods. How to listen for the psychic grain of a place, to find the note that would steady it. How a pattern of stones, or a specific planting, or even a repeated melody hummed at a certain hour, could act as a suture. Kael was a quick study. His fear had been refined into a profound attentiveness.

Later, by the fire, Kael spoke of his mother. He would visit her soon, he said. Not to stay, but to show her he was whole. To tell her, in terms she might understand, about the quiet work. “She thinks I’m learning botany,” he said, a faint smile on his lips.

“You are,” Vail replied. “The botany of being.”

The boy—now a young man—slept on a pallet by the hearth. Vail sat at the table, the stag’s heart before him. He placed his hands on the cool clay of the bowl and closed his eyes. He did not seek visions. He sought the echo. The moment of the stag’s terror, its final struggle—he let it wash against him, then gently smoothed it away, like calming water. He offered back to the forest, through the vessel of the heart, a pattern of peace, of acceptance. A tiny, deliberate act of healing.

This was his ordinary world now. A continuous, conscious conversation.

***

**Scene 2: The Elixir Shared**

The community was not a village, not anymore. Achnasaul was still a smudge of smoke, but its people were different. Some had left for the lowland towns, lured by the Collective’s promises of powered light and machine warmth. Those who remained were the stubborn, the old, the quietly strange.

They gathered in Murdo’s barn for the Martinmas feast, not because the old calendar held sway, but because the thinning of the year made the work necessary. Vail and Kael brought the venison. Others brought ale, oatcakes, the last of the autumn apples.

The air was thick with woodsmoke and the smell of roasting meat. But beneath it, Vail sensed the new tensions. Old Fergus muttered about survey markers pulling up his turnips. Elspeth, who could still sing the *orain* *làidh* of her grandmother, spoke of dreams filled with the sound of grinding gears.

When the eating was done, Vail did not stand to give a speech. He simply took out his *feadag*, the old wooden flute, and began to play. It was not a merry jig. It was the melody he had woven at the Three Sisters that afternoon—a simple, repeating pattern of notes, grounded and slow, with a curious hollow resonance in the middle phrase. He had played it for hours as Kael replanted hawthorn shoots and arranged river stones in a specific, non-geometric pattern.

As the notes filled the barn, a subtle change occurred. The anxious chatter died down. Fergus stopped grumbling and stared into his cup. Elspeth’s pinched expression softened. They didn’t know what they were hearing, but they *felt* it. It was a sound that spoke to the land beneath the barn’s stone floor, and the land, in turn, quieted the fear in their bones.

After, old Murdo approached, his shrewd eyes on Vail. “That was a different tune. It… sits in the ear.”

“It sits in the soil, Murdo,” Vail said softly. “It’s a reminder. For the land. For us.”

“Aye,” Murdo grunted, looking around at his neighbors. Some were nodding off, peaceful for the first time in weeks. “Well. It’s a better fence than wire, I’ll give you that.”

The elixir was not a potion or a proclamation. It was a shared resonance. It was the demonstration that the old ways were not about superstition, but about a sophisticated ecology of spirit and matter. It was the knowledge that they could, in small ways, armor their world against the crushing, sterilizing frequency of the new civilization. Vail shared it through music, through action, through the simple, potent fact of the venison that had nourished them—a gift from a forest they were learning to defend not with weapons, but with understanding.

Elspeth came to him as the party broke up. She took his hands in her gnarled ones. Her eyes were wet. “You gave me back my quiet,” she whispered. “The dreams… they’re just dreams again. Not invasions.”

He squeezed her hands. “The song is always there, Elspeth. We just have to remember how to sing along.”

***

**Scene 3: Relationships Redefined**

The reconciliation came on a day of hard, clean cold. The sky was a porcelain blue, and the snow squeaked underfoot.

A figure appeared on the track from the south: a woman, wrapped in a good wool cloak, moving with the hesitant determination of someone in unfamiliar territory. Rhona.

Vail saw her from the cottage door. He felt a complex chord sound within him—old grief, residual guilt, a fragile hope. He did not go out. He waited.

She stopped a dozen paces from the door, taking in the smoke, the byre, the evidence of life. Her gaze finally settled on him. He saw the Citadel’s sharpness still in her eyes, but it was blunted by a deep, weary sorrow.

“They’ve officially listed you as ‘Decommissioned,’” she said. Her voice was hoarse from the cold, or from disuse. “Asset forfeit. The Achnasaul file is closed.”

Vail said nothing. The Citadel’s verdict was as meaningful to him now as the chirping of sparrows.

“I resigned,” she continued, the words coming out in a rush of steam. “After the report was buried. After they called the *cù-sìth* a ‘mass hallucination induced by geothermal fumes.’” She looked away, towards the mountains. “I couldn’t curate lies anymore.”

He gestured to the door. “Come in. Get warm.”

Inside, she was visibly shocked by the cottage’s interior. Her eyes scanned the resonance maps, the herbal bundles, the simple, vital clutter. It was the antithesis of the Citadel’s sterile order. Kael, tactfully, took himself off to check the snares.

Rhona sat by the fire, accepting a cup of mint tea. The silence between them was not hostile, but vast.

“Why did you come here, Rhona?” he asked at last.

“To see,” she said. “To understand what you chose instead.” She paused. “And to apologize. I was your anchor to a world that was… suffocating you. I thought I was protecting you. I was just making you a better prisoner.”

He shook his head slowly. “You were a tether to a self I needed to remember before I could shed it. I needed the Curator to die cleanly. You helped with that, even if you didn’t mean to.”

It was forgiveness, but not absolution. It was an acknowledgment of necessary pain.

“What is this?” she asked, gesturing to the walls, to the world outside.

“It’s listening,” he said. “And answering.”

He told her, then. Not everything, but enough. About the veil, not as a mystical curtain, but as the active, resonant interface between the deep, slow consciousness of the land (the Stone) and the fleeting, creative, destructive chaos of life (the Veil). He explained the Collective’s threat: not that they were evil, but that their frequency of pure, abstract order was an anesthetic. It didn’t tear the veil; it starved it, silencing the song until the land became inert, a resource, a blank slate.

She listened, not as a scholar, but as a refugee. When he finished, she said, “So you’re not stopping them.”

“We can’t. The tide of that civilization is too vast. But we can teach the land here to… digest it. To incorporate the road without being poisoned by it. To make the silence they create into a different kind of silence—a fertile one, not a dead one.”

He saw the spark in her eyes then, the one he’d loved—not the spark of cataloguing, but of *seeing*. “It’s applied ecology,” she whispered. “Psycho-acoustic ecology.”

He smiled, the ghost of his old smile. “Call it what you need to.”

Before she left, as the afternoon light slanted gold, he took her to the Rowan Wood. He showed her the place of the stag, now marked only by a scatter of bones, clean and white. He had her place her hands on the trunk of the oldest rowan.

“Just listen,” he said.

She closed her eyes. He watched the concentration, then the confusion, then the dawning, awe-struck wonder on her face. She felt it. The faint, vibrant hum, the intricate, living pattern beneath the bark.

When she opened her eyes, they were full of tears. “It’s… a library,” she breathed.

“And we are its librarians now,” he said. “A small, quiet library. But persistent.”

She did not ask to stay. Their relationship was reconciled into something new: she would return to the lowlands, not as a Citadel agent, but as a sleeper, a defender of quiet in the heart of the noise. She would be his connection to that other, rushing world, and he would be her anchor to this deeper one. They were no longer partners in preservation, but allies in a long, subtle war of resonance.

***

**Scene 4: The New Beginning**

Spring came late, a hesitant green unfurling against the brown moor. The Northern Collective’s road had reached the head of the glen, a grey ribbon stopping abruptly at the edge of the peat bog. Their maps, Vail knew, would show it as a “natural obstacle,” a “delay.” He and Kael had spent the winter making that bog sing with a particularly disorienting, mist-hung resonance. Machines stalled there. Men got lost within sight of their camp. The Collective’s engineers were baffled, writing reports on “unique magnetic anomalies.”

It was a holding action. Vail knew they would find a way around, or through. Time was not on the side of the stone. But time was not the point. Integrity was.

On the morning of the equinox, Vail climbed to the high cave, the one where the *cù-sìth* had died. He went alone. The entrance was now woven over with living willow, a living veil he and Kael had planted. He parted it and entered.

The silence was profound, but it was no longer the silence of death. It was a silence like a deep pool, holding memory. He sat on the stone where he had fought his inner chaos, and he listened to the cave’s new song. It was a lament, but a finished one. A story that had reached its end and was now at rest.

From his pack, he took the stag’s heart. It was desiccated now, light as a stone. He placed it in a niche in the cave wall, beside a smooth water-worn pebble from the burn and a feather from a raven. A tiny cairn of meanings.

He did not look toward the future with a plan, but with a posture. He was a man rooted in a practice, a listener, a mender. Kael was his successor, already dreaming of ways to weave resonance into the very wool of the local sheep, into the patterns of drystone dykes. Rhona was his agent in the world of straight lines. The community at Achnasaul was his chorus.

The war between nature and civilization was not a war to be won. It was a tension to be held, a dissonance to be managed. His life’s work was to be a tuning fork for this one, precious piece of the world, to help it hold its note against the gathering drone.

He left the cave as the sun balanced on the horizon, day and night equal. He stood on the mountainside, looking down. The Glen of Shadows was filled with a soft, golden light. The new road was a faint scar on its far edge. The smoke from his cottage rose in a straight, thin column. He could hear, far below, the clear sound of Kael’s hammer on metal—he was forging a new knife, learning the song of iron and fire.

And from the north, if he strained, he could still sense it: the cold, dry pulse of the Collective, the *crunch-crunch-crunch* of an order that knew only itself. It was coming. The snow that had erased the world had melted, revealing the true, enduring landscape of conflict.

But here, now, there was also the whisper of the rowans waking, the chuckle of the snowmelt burn, the steady heartbeat of the land beneath his feet. He had integrated the chaos. He had become a part of the song, not its analyst. The path forward was not a line. It was a pattern, a resonance, a practice.

Vail MacAnndra turned his face into the spring wind, feeling it carry the scent of peat and stone and distant, unknown futures. He took the first step down the hill, not as an ending, but as the next, necessary note in an endless, beautiful, and defiant song.

The stag was dead. But the forest was not done with it. It was part of the forest now, and the forest was just beginning to sing.



---

# Chapter 13

## **THE STONE AND THE VEIL**

### **Chapter 13**

The song of the forge was a different music to the hill’s.

Kael MacAnndra’s hammer fell in a steady, three-beat rhythm: a chime, a thud, a sigh of escaping heat. *Ting. Thump. Hssss.* The iron, a rough tongue of metal scavenged from an old gatepost, glowed a sullen orange on the anvil. He was not shaping a tool, not yet. He was learning the language of the material, listening for the note within the ore. His hands, already losing their scholar’s softness, were mapped with small, silver burns. The air in the old byre they’d converted smelled of coal, scorched horn, and his own sharp sweat.

Vail stood in the doorway, a silhouette against the bright, greening world outside. He watched for a long moment, his head tilted as if parsing a complex peat-stain on water. The rhythm was good. The boy had patience. But there was a tension in Kael’s shoulders, a fierceness in the strike that spoke of something more than learning.

“You’re arguing with it,” Vail said, his voice quiet but cutting through the metallic echoes.

The hammer paused mid-arc. Kael looked up, his face smudged with soot, eyes bright in the dim forge-light. “It’s stubborn.”

“It’s iron. It remembers being stone. You must persuade it, not command it.” Vail moved into the byre, the packed-earth floor silent under his boots. He picked up the cooling metal with a pair of tongs, turning it in the light from the open hearth. “See here. You struck against the grain. You’re trying to make it into a blade, but it’s telling you it wants to be a hook, or a bracket. A mending piece.”

Kael set the hammer down, the frustration leaking from him like steam. “A blade is what’s needed.”

“Is it?” Vail placed the iron back on the anvil. “A blade cuts. It severs. Is that the first note you wish to sound in this place?”

The question hung in the hot air. Kael wiped his brow with his forearm. From outside came the distant, contented bleat of a ewe, the skylark’s endless chain of song over the high pasture. The forge, for all its fire, felt like a cave of intent.

“The Collective’s surveys,” Kael said, the words coming out in a rush. “Rhona’s reports. They’re not just mapping rock. They’re cataloguing *potential*. Hydrostatic pressure for dams. Mineral seams for extraction. Slope gradients for… for something. It’s a blade. They’re forging a blade against the glen.”

Vail nodded slowly. He walked to the water barrel, submerged his hands, and brought a palmful to his lips. “And your answer is to meet a blade with a blade.”

“What else is there?”

“A shield.” Vail’s grey eyes held him. “A vessel. A bell. A thing that turns, or holds, or sings. Violence is a language they understand perfectly. It is their native tongue. To speak it is to concede the conversation.”

He took up the hammer. It was lighter in his hand, an extension of his arm. He positioned the iron on the anvil, not as a thing to be dominated, but as a partner in a dance. His first strike was not a blow, but a tap—a question. *Tink.* He waited, as if listening. Then another, slightly firmer, on a different plane. *Tonk.* He rotated the metal, his movements fluid, a slow river of motion around the fixed point of the anvil. The rhythm that emerged was not a marching beat, but a lament, a coaxing. The iron began to glow again, not with protest, but with a kind of awakened willingness. It began to curve, not because it was forced, but because it was shown the possibility of curve.

Kael watched, his anger cooling into awe. This was not metallurgy. It was courtship.

“The land has its own will,” Vail said, not looking up, his voice merging with the ring of steel. “The stone, the water, the root. The Collective sees will as a flaw to be corrected. We must see it as a thread to be woven. Their way is the single, deafening note. Ours must be the chord.”

He quenched the metal. The scream it made was short, decisive, full of purpose. He held up the result. It was not a knife. It was a graceful, open spiral, like a fiddlehead fern or a frozen eddy of water. A bracket, yes, but one that seemed to still be in motion.

“It will hold a shelf,” Vail said. “A shelf that will hold a stone, or a cup, or a seedling in a pot. It is a small act of holding. That is our work now. Not to fight the blade, but to make the idea of the blade seem… crude. Unnecessary.”

A shadow fell across the doorway. Rhona stood there, her tablet in hand, her city-made jacket looking stark and angular against the organic curves of the byre. Her face was pale, the professional composure cracked at the edges.

“The conversation,” she said, her voice tight, “may have been conceded already.”

She stepped inside, the glow of her tablet screen a cold moon in the forge’s sunset. On it was a map, but not like the old Ordnance Survey maps Vail kept in the cottage. This was a layering of ghostly colours, pulsing grids, and sharp, angular notations. It was the glen seen through a lidless, mathematical eye.

“They’ve moved from passive spectral analysis to active sub-surface resonance,” she said, pointing to a series of concentric circles overlaying the northern foothills of Ben na Saighead. “Sonic pulses. They’re not just looking at the geology now. They’re *interrogating* it. Measuring fracture points, density variances.” She zoomed in on a cluster of symbols near the Allt Coire na Saobhaidh. “And they’ve flagged anomalies.”

“Anomalies,” Vail repeated, the word flat and foreign.

“Areas where the sub-surface resonance returns… chaotic data. Inconsistent with the surrounding matrix. One is here.” She tapped a point on the map. It pulsed a soft, warning red. It was at the head of the Glen of Shadows, near the old shieling ruins. Near the cave.

“They’ve categorized it as a ‘sub-terrainian void of unknown configuration.’ A curiosity. For now.” She looked from Vail to Kael. “But their protocol is to resolve curiosities. The next phase is physical investigation. A team. Drills. Probes.”

The sounds of the forge—the settling coals, the drip of condensation—seemed to grow louder in the silence. Vail placed the spiral of iron on the anvil with a soft, final *clink*. The note it made was pure, and sad.

“They’ve found the throat,” he said quietly. “And they wish to clear it.”

*

The rowans by the cottage were in full, creamy bloom, their scent a honeyed veil over the sharper smells of earth and stone. But Vail did not smell them. He stood at the edge of the kitchen garden, his back to the warm stone of the house, and faced north. He had closed his eyes, not to block out the world, but to listen to a deeper layer of it.

He felt it now, as a throbbing in his teeth, a faint, metallic taste at the back of his tongue. The Collective’s sonic pulses. They were not sound as the hill understood sound. They were sterile, repeating shrieks of pure information, cutting through the soil and rock like scalpels through skin. To the land, it was a form of violence. A shouting in a sacred space.

He could feel the glen’s distress as a low, discordant hum in the soles of his feet. The water in the burn ran slightly agitated, its song frayed. The birds were quieter. It was a subtle thing, a dimming of the background resonance he had spent a lifetime tuning himself to. To anyone else, it would be nothing. A change in the weather. A passing mood.

But to Vail, it was an assault in progress.

Rhona’s plan was logistical, political. She was drafting appeals to heritage boards, compiling ecological impact assessments, leveraging obscure EU protections for ‘unique acoustic landscapes.’ It was a shield of paper and procedure. It might slow the blade. It would not stop it.

Kael’s anger was a forge-fire, burning for a weapon. A direct, resonant sabotage of their equipment, a way to turn their own sonic pulses against them, to flood their data with noise. It was a blade of a different kind.

Both were necessary. Both were insufficient.

The glen needed a different response. It needed to *answer*. Not in the language of law, or of violence, but in its own native tongue. It needed to become, for those who were probing it, inexplicable. It needed to be a story they could not write down.

He opened his eyes. The late afternoon sun slanted across the glen, painting the new leaves of the birches a luminous green. The new road was a pale, careless scratch on the far ridge. He thought of the stag, of the forest not being done with it. The glen had integrated the death, had made it part of its song. Could it integrate this probing? Could it swallow the scalpel and transform it?

An idea, fragile and cold as the first ice on a puddle, began to form. It was not a plan. It was a posture. A practice.

He found Kael at the burn, scrubbing the forge soot from his hands in the cold, peat-brown water.

“We must go to the cave,” Vail said.

“To hide the entrance? Camouflage it?”

“No.” Vail’s gaze was on the tumbling water, watching how it flowed around a stone, how it sang a different note when obstructed. “To listen. And then, to amplify.”

*

The climb was familiar, the path a muscle memory in their legs. But the glen felt different. The usual chorus of evening sounds—the pipit’s call, the wind in the heather—was muted, as if holding its breath. The intrusive *thrum* of the sonic pulses was a physical pressure here, a headache behind the eyes. The land felt raw, exposed.

They reached the cave as the last of the sun gilded the high crags. The entrance was a darker shadow in the shadow of the cliff, the rowan sapling at its mouth trembling in a wind that wasn’t there. Vail placed a hand on its slender trunk. It was vibrating, a faint, distressed buzz.

Inside, the darkness was absolute, a velvet weight. The silence was not empty; it was a held tension, like the moment before a thunderclap. The cave was listening to the violation.

Vail did not light a lamp. He moved forward by memory and feel, his hand brushing the cool, wet wall. Kael followed, his breath loud in the confined space. They reached the chamber where the resonance had once woven its perfect, terrifying chord. Now, the air felt jagged. The sonic pulses from the north penetrated here, not as sound, but as a series of sharp, psychic jabs, disrupting the natural harmonics of the space. It was like hearing a beautiful, complex piece of music being played while someone rhythmically slammed a door.

Vail sat on the stone floor, his back against the wall. He gestured for Kael to do the same. “Do not fight it,” he whispered. “Listen *through* it. Find the glen’s note beneath the noise.”

It was agony at first. The invasive pulses were like needles in the mind. But slowly, as his breathing slowed and matched the slow, deep exhalation of the hill itself, Vail began to separate the threads. The pulse was a sterile, repeating *A*. But beneath it, stubborn, was the glen’s foundational hum—a complex, shifting tone closer to a *D*, rich with overtones of flowing water and root-growth. The cave, distressed, was producing a discordant harmonic, an angry *F-sharp*.

It was a cacophony. A wound.

Vail began to hum. Not a melody, but a single, pure note. An *E*. It was not the glen’s note, nor the cave’s distress signal. It was a third thing. A mediator. He hummed it softly, steadily, feeling the vibration in his chest, in his skull, letting it bleed into the stone at his back.

For a long time, nothing happened. The invasive *A* continued its assault. The discord held.

Then, Kael joined him. His voice was younger, clearer. He found the *E* and held it, matching Vail’s pitch exactly. Two human voices, small in the vastness of the stone, offering a single, steady note.

The cave seemed to lean in. The oppressive quality of the discord shifted, just slightly. The angry *F-sharp* wavered, as if listening.

Vail changed his note. He slid from the *E* down to a *C-sharp*, a fifth below the invasive *A*. A foundational support. Kael, his ear sharp, instinctively moved to a *G-sharp*, creating a chord. A minor, haunting triad: C-sharp, E, G-sharp.

They were not singing *at* the cave. They were singing *with* it, offering a new harmonic possibility.

The air in the chamber changed. The sharp, jabbing quality of the invasive pulses began to blur, to smear. It was as if the stone itself was absorbing the sound, transforming it. The cave’s distressed *F-sharp* softened, melted, and drifted toward the *E*. The resonance of the chamber, so long disrupted, began to stir. It did not build to the overwhelming, visionary chord of before. That was a song for a different purpose. This was a song of integration, of healing.

A low, sub-audible thrumming rose from the stone beneath them. It was the hill’s own voice, deep and slow, joining the chord. The human notes were the treble, the hill’s hum the bass. Together, they created a complex, shifting resonance that wrapped around the invasive sonic pulse. They did not block it. They *contextualized* it. They made it a single, clumsy note in a far richer, more ancient harmony.

The psychic pressure lifted. The headache faded. The cave was no longer a raw nerve, but a deep, resonant chamber again, processing the insult, weaving it into the fabric of its own long story.

Vail stopped humming. Kael’s voice trailed off a moment later. The silence that returned was not the tense silence of before. It was a deep, peaceful quiet, like the moment after a storm has passed. The only sound was the distant, eternal drip of water and the low, contented hum of the stone.

In the darkness, Vail could feel Kael’s stunned awe.

“We didn’t stop them,” Kael whispered.

“No,” Vail said, his own voice rough with use. “We taught the glen how to answer. We gave it a new pattern. The blade cannot cut a chord. The drill cannot extract a harmony.”

“Will it… will it last?”

“Nothing lasts. But it establishes a pattern. A practice. When they send their probes, the glen will not scream. It will… sing back. A song they cannot interpret. A data stream of beautiful, impossible noise.” He pushed himself to his feet, his bones aching. “Now, we must do the same for the people.”

*

The meeting at Achnasaul Hall was not like the others. The fear was a tangible smell in the room—sour, sharp. It was the smell of futures being foreclosed. Donald the farmer had a notice about compulsory land access for ‘geophysical survey.’ Morag, who ran the holiday lets, had received a blandly threatening query from a Collective subsidiary about ‘planning conformity’ for her off-grid cabins. The threads of the net were tightening, not with a dramatic yank, but with a slow, administrative cinch.

Rhona stood at the front, her tablet displaying flowcharts of appeals processes. Her words were clear, logical, a seawall against the fear. But the people’s eyes were glazed. They heard ‘judicial review’ and ‘submission window’ and they saw the bulldozer on the horizon anyway.

Vail waited until she finished. He did not stand. He remained in his chair by the cold fireplace, a still point in the anxious room.

“They are speaking to the land in a language of force,” he said, his voice not loud, but somehow filling the spaces between Rhona’s fading echoes. “And the land is learning to answer in a tongue they do not understand. Our work is the same.”

All eyes turned to him. He saw the desperation in them, the need for more than a procedure.

“They believe reality is a thing to be measured, extracted, and simplified. Our task is to make our reality here… richer. More complex. More *inconveniently* alive.” He looked at Donald. “Your field where they wish to survey. The one with the old well. Plant it. Not with barley. With a meadow. A hundred different flowers, herbs, grasses. Make it a riot of life their sensors cannot categorize. A biodiversity event that requires… observation.”

He turned to Morag. “Your cabins. Offer ‘acoustic retreats.’ Market the silence, the specific song of this glen. Make it a feature. A valued commodity. A ‘heritage soundscape.’ Give it a name, a price. They understand value. Give them a new column for their spreadsheet.”

He looked around the room, at the weathered, worried faces. “We cannot be a problem they solve. We must become a story they cannot finish reading. A tune they cannot get out of their head, and cannot quite write down. Kael is learning the song of iron. Rhona is weaving a shield of words. You, each of you, must find your note. The note of your land, your craft, your home. And we must sound them together. Not in unison. In harmony. A chord so dense and living that their single, deafening note of progress seems… poverty-stricken.”

It was not a battle plan. It was a choir practice. And he saw, slowly, the fear in the room begin to change. It did not vanish, but it morphed into a kind of fierce determination. Donald’s shoulders straightened. Morag’s eyes lost their glaze, sharpening with cunning. They began to talk, not about legal clauses, but about heirloom seeds, about beehives, about reviving the old names for the fields, about recording their grandparents’ stories of the place. They began to weave their own net, not of resistance, but of profound, intricate *being*.

Later, as the twilight deepened and the hall emptied, Rhona approached Vail. Her professional armour was back in place, but her eyes were thoughtful.

“A meadow is not a zoning law,” she said.

“No,” he agreed. “It is more permanent.”

“They’ll still come. With their probes.”

“Let them,” Vail said, looking out the window at the first stars pricking the violet sky over Ben na Saighead. “Let them measure the unmeasurable. Let them try to silence a song that is now being sung by every root, every stone, every voice in this glen. We are no longer defending a position. We are becoming a phenomenon.”

He left her then, walking out into the cool evening. The sonic pulses were still there, a faint, irritating buzz at the edge of perception. But beneath it, stronger now, he could hear the glen finding its new voice. The chuckle of the burn seemed to carry a hidden harmony. The wind in the pines had a purposeful rhythm. The land was learning the chord they had taught it in the cave, and was beginning to hum it back.

He walked up the track towards his cottage, the smell of peat smoke guiding him home. The work was endless. It was not a war to be won, but a music to be sustained, note by difficult note.

As he reached his door, he paused. From the north, on the very edge of hearing, carried on a stray gust of wind, came a new sound. Not the *crunch-crunch-crunch* of marching order. This was a mechanical, grinding shriek, followed by a deep, percussive *thud*.

Then another.

And another.

It was the sound of rock meeting drill. The first physical incision. The blade had finally touched the skin of the world.

Vail MacAnndra stood in his open doorway, the warm light of his home at his back, the vast, singing darkness of the glen before him. He listened to the brutal, rhythmic *thud-thud-thud* from the north. It was a crude, simple beat.

He smiled then, a thin, hard curve in his beard. He nodded, as if to a pupil who had just played their first, clumsy note.

“Good,” he whispered to the night. “Now play it again. And listen for what plays back.”

He stepped inside, and closed the door on the darkness. But he did not close out the sound. He let it in. Let it become part of the house’s song. The kettle on the stove began a low, rising whistle, its own pure note rising to meet the distant, discordant drum.



---

# Chapter 14

## **THE STONE AND THE VEIL**

### **Chapter 14**

The drills were a toothache in the bone of the world.

For three days and three nights, the *thud-thud-thud* had pulsed from the northern corrie, a dull, arrhythmic heartbeat that did not sync with the glen’s own. It was not a constant sound. It came in fits—a screaming grind of diamond-tipped teeth on Lewisian gneiss, a silence thick with hydraulic tension, then the deep, concussive *thud* of a core sample being torn free. Each impact sent a shiver through the bedrock, a tremor so faint only the oldest pines and the deepest springs could feel it. But Vail MacAnndra felt it. He felt it in the soles of his feet as he split peat behind the cottage. He felt it in the handle of his teacup, a minute vibration that made the surface of the tea shiver. The glen was being prodded, its ribs counted by a cold, metallic finger.

He worked, as he always worked. He checked the snares on the high moor, finding only one hare, thin and taut with winter. He repaired a section of drystone dyke where the frost had shouldered it loose. He sat with Catriona in the silent library of the big house, maps of the estate spread between them like patient, sleeping beasts. They did not speak of the drills. They spoke of water tables, of deer paths, of the old names for the places now marked with surveyor’s pins. *Coire na h-Innsig*—the Corrie of the Warning. *Allt a’ Chlaidheimh*—the Burn of the Sword. The language was a map in itself, a deeper geology. The drilling, for all its violence, was a superficial thing. A scratch. But a scratch could go septic.

On the fourth morning, a different sound arrived.

It began as a hum, a low-frequency drone that seemed to emanate from the ground itself, vibrating up through the floor flags of the cottage. Vail paused, a spoonful of oatmeal halfway to his mouth. It was not the drill. This was a wider, more diffuse sound, the audible shadow of a great machine waking. He set the spoon down, the sound stealing his appetite. He knew what it was before he saw it. He had heard it before, in other glens, on other hillsides where the veins of the earth had been deemed ripe for bleeding.

He took his jacket from the peg and walked out, not towards the north, but east, up the steep shoulder of Creag nan Gabhar. The morning was still, the sky a sheet of polished lead. The hum grew as he climbed, thickening the air, pressing against his eardrums. It was the sound of generators. Not the portable, sputtering things that powered the drills, but the deep, industrial purr of containerised diesel units, enough to birth a small, temporary town of light and noise. They were building their base camp.

From the summit of the craig, he could see it. The mouth of the northern corrie, once a quiet bowl of heather and broken rock where eagles nested, was now a wound of raw activity. A spider-web of temporary tracks, pale as scars, had been etched across the peat. At their centre, a cluster of modular cabins and Portakabins gleamed a sickly, corporate yellow under banks of LED lights that burned against the gloom. Among them, hulking and insectile, stood the drill rig itself, a lattice of steel and ambition, its mast a stark finger pointing at the sky as if accusing it. The hum was the sound of its life-support. The *thud-thud-thrump* was its pulse.

And around it, moving with the meticulous, disconnected purpose of ants, were men. Tiny at this distance, their hi-vis jackets were points of chemical orange against the muted land. They moved between vehicles, unspooled cables, consulted tablets. They did not look at the corrie walls. They did not pause to feel the wind change direction. They interfaced with the machinery, and the machinery interfaced with the rock. The land itself was merely a condition, a problem of density and fracture points.
