Vail watched, his breath steaming in the cold air. He did not feel rage. Rage was a hot, simple thing, and this was cold and complex. What he felt was a profound, weary recognition. This was the true face of the thing they fought. Not malice, but a vast, efficient indifference. A process. The glen was not a being to these men; it was a site. Its song was data-point noise. Its veins were mineralogical schematics. They would not hate it. They would simply unmake it, piece by quantified piece, and file the reports.

He turned from the sight, his eyes stinging from the wind or the distant, unnatural light. As he did, his gaze swept across the lower slopes of the glen, across the silver thread of the Allt Coire na Saobhaidh, across the dark smudge of the pinewood that held the cave. And there, he saw the glen’s answer.

It was subtle. To any other eye, it would be nothing. A trick of the light. A weather shift.

But Vail MacAnndra knew weather. He knew light.

The mist was rising. Not the great, rolling blankets that poured down from the high tops, but tendrils. Slender, purposeful fingers of vapour were uncurling from the surface of the burn. They were seeping from the peat hags, breathing out from the mossy banks of the Allt a’ Chlaidheimh. They were emerging between the roots of the pines, silent and grey. They did not drift aimlessly. They gathered. They coalesced into a thin, persistent veil that began to flow, with the slow, gravitational certainty of water, up the glen. Towards the northern corrie.

It was flowing *against* the wind.

A faint, cold thrill traced Vail’s spine. He had asked the land to learn the chord. He had not taught it tactics.

He descended the hill, not by the path, but straight down the heathery face, his boots sliding on the wet slope. He did not go home. He went to the source.

*

The cave was breathing.

Vail stood at its entrance, the familiar damp chill wrapping around him. But the silence inside was different. It was not the deep, absorbing quiet of stone. It was a *held* silence. A listening silence. The trickle of water from the seep in the rear wall was the only sound, but its rhythm was odd—not a random drip, but a deliberate, spaced *plink… plink… plink*, like a clock ticking in an empty room.

He stepped inside, his hand finding the wall for guidance. The darkness was absolute, but it felt charged, like the air before lightning. He did not need light. He moved by memory, by the pressure of the space on his skin. He reached the inner chamber, the place of the three stones and the hidden spring.

He stopped.

The veil of mist he had seen outside was here, inside. It was not thick, but it was present, a luminescent greyness that clung to the floor, pooling in the hollows, obscuring the bases of the standing stones. It swirled gently, as if stirred by a breath he could not feel. And from the small, black pool in the centre of the chamber, more vapour was rising in a steady, gentle exhalation. It was cold, colder than the air, and it carried the scent of deep rock and old water.

Vail knelt by the pool, his knees pressing into the cold, wet earth. He placed his palms flat on the ground, fingers spread. He closed his eyes.

He did not hum. He did not try to sing the chord. He simply opened himself. He let the vibration of the distant drills come up through the rock, into his bones. *Thud-thud-thrump*. A crude, invasive rhythm. He felt it as a sickness, a discordant knot in the glen’s quiet body.

Then he listened deeper.

Beneath the industrial throb, he felt the answering tremor. It was not an echo. It was a correction. A counterpoint. It came from the water—not just the pool, but from all the hidden waterways that laced the mountain like a nervous system. A faint, harmonic shiver, travelling through the aquifer. He felt it in the stone at his fingertips—a low, almost sub-audible hum, the bedrock itself vibrating at a frequency that made his teeth ache. The standing stones around him were not inert. They were conduits, their ancient, crystalline structures humming like tuning forks struck by a distant, dissonant bell.

The land was not just singing back. It was diagnosing. It was finding the frequency of the invasion and generating its own interference pattern.

He stayed there for a long time, his consciousness suspended between the two pulses: the brutal, mechanical *thud* from the north, and the complex, ancient resonance rising from the glen’s very heart. They were not fighting yet. They were measuring one another. Taking each other’s scent.

When he finally rose, his joints stiff with cold and concentration, the mist in the cave had thickened. It curled around his legs as he walked back to the entrance. Outside, the glen was vanishing. The tendrils had become a tide. A solid wall of grey-white fog now filled the strath, swallowing the pinewoods, blurring the outlines of the hills. It was silent and utterly still. And it was moving, with a glacial patience, up towards the yellow lights and the industrial hum.

Vail allowed himself a single, slow breath. The first move in the game had not been his. It had been the glen’s.

*

Catriona found him at the boundary wall, where the estate track met the new, temporary road the surveyors had bulldozed. He was a silhouette against the advancing fog, a figure carved from peat and granite.

“They’ve called,” she said, her voice tight. She held out a satellite phone, an ugly, black lump of plastic. “The site manager. A man named Forrester. He says the weather’s closed in. Unprecedented localised fog. It’s a safety issue. All non-essential work is halted.”

Vail took the phone, his hand dwarfing it. He said nothing, looking past her to where the fog was now lapping at the first of the contractor’s “No Unauthorised Access” signs, making the stark red letters bleed into the grey.

“He wasn’t asking, Vail,” Catriona continued. “He was informing. He also said their initial ground-penetrating radar readings are… anomalous. Inconsistent. They’re getting interference. Ghost echoes. He implied we might be responsible. That someone could be planting equipment to sabotage their readings.”

“Is that what we’re doing?” Vail asked, his voice a low rumble.

“You tell me.”

He handed the phone back. “We’re planting nothing. We’re asking the land to remember what it is. Sometimes, remembering looks like resistance.” He nodded towards the fog. “This is the land remembering it can be unseen.”

“Forrester isn’t a fool. He’s ex-military. Engineering corps. He’s used to obstacles. He’ll have lidar, thermal imaging. Fog won’t stop him for long.”

“No,” Vail agreed. “It won’t. It’s a pause. A breath. It tells us they can be inconvenienced. That their machines have limits the weather does not.” He turned to her. “The interference on their scans… that is the glen’s own voice. A song their machines are not built to hear, but cannot ignore. It is static on their line to the deep.”

Catriona wrapped her arms around herself, not from the cold, but from a sudden, profound unease. She was a strategist. She dealt in laws, in documents, in human systems. This was something else. “What happens when they push through the fog? When they get a clean reading and start drilling in earnest? A bit of static won’t stop a diamond bit.”

Vail looked north, though the corrie was now utterly lost in the murk. The hum of the generators was muffled, distant, as if heard through wool. “The first note has been played. The second must answer. A drill seeks a weakness. It finds the fracture in the rock and exploits it.” He met her gaze, his eyes the colour of the fog itself. “What if the rock… changes its mind?”

*

The fog held for thirty-six hours.

It was a profound, disorienting blanket. It deadened sound and erased distance. The world shrank to the circle of gravel at one’s feet, the damp trunk of the nearest tree. At the MacAnndra cottage, the world ended at the garden gate. The constant *thud-thud-thrump* was gone, smothered. Only the deep, generator hum remained, a directionless drone that seemed to come from everywhere and nowhere.

On the second afternoon, a vehicle emerged from the whiteness. It was a dark green Land Rover Defender, new and clean, its sides streaked with moisture. It crunched to a halt outside Vail’s gate. The man who stepped out was in his late forties, built like a rugby player gone to seed but still solid. He wore a practical, expensive jacket, clean boots, and an expression of controlled neutrality. This was Forrester.

Vail was waiting for him, not at the door, but at the woodpile, splitting logs with a steady, rhythmic *thwack* of the axe.

Forrester approached, not venturing into the yard. “Mr. MacAnndra. I’m John Forrester, site lead for Caledonian Mineral Surveys.”

Vail set another log on the block. *Thwack*. It cleaved cleanly. “I know who you are.”

“I’d like to speak with you.”

*Thwack.* “You are.”

Forrester’s jaw tightened slightly. “This weather. It’s unusual. Localised to this glen. Forecast shows clear ten miles in every direction.”

“The glen has its own weather. Always has.”

“It’s causing significant delays. Safety issues. My men can’t see three feet in front of them.” He paused, watching Vail’s back as he bent to pick up another log. “Our subsurface scans are also experiencing unprecedented interference. It’s costing us time. Money.”

Vail straightened, the axe resting on his shoulder. He turned, finally looking at Forrester. His gaze was flat, assessing. “The land is not a blank page. It has a texture. A history. Your machines are reading that history. They just don’t understand the language.”

Forrester took a step forward, his neutrality hardening into something more direct. “Let’s not play games, MacAnndra. I’ve done this from Chile to Kazakhstan. There’s always local… colour. Superstition. Resistance. People try to scare us off. They bury metal, run wires, try to spoof the instruments. It never works. We have filters. We have protocols. And we have the law on our side.”

“I am not running wires,” Vail said, his voice quiet. “I am listening to the hill. And the hill, it seems, does not care for your drills.”

A faint, cold smile touched Forrester’s lips. It was not a friendly expression. It was the smile of a man who has identified a problem and is already categorising the solution. “The hill will care even less when it’s a hole in the ground feeding the batteries in your phone. This is happening. The fog will lift. My scanners will be recalibrated. And we will drill. You can be a footnote of local difficulty, or you can be sensible. My company is prepared to offer a revised… goodwill package. For the inconvenience.”

He was not offering a bribe. He was stating a transaction. The glen’s silence, its song, its very being, had a price in his ledger, and he was ready to enter it.

Vail looked past him, into the swirling grey. “Your offer is dust. Take it back to your yellow boxes and count it again.”

Forrester’s smile vanished. He gave a short, sharp nod. “Right. I’ve made the effort. The next time we speak, it will be with security personnel and a court injunction.” He turned on his heel and walked back to the Defender. The engine growled, and the vehicle was swallowed by the fog, the sound of its engine fading until there was only the drip of water from the eaves and the soft, pervasive sigh of the mist.

Vail did not move for a long time. He stood by his woodpile, the axe heavy on his shoulder, feeling the glen around him. The fog was not just water vapour. It was a presence. A held breath. Forrester had seen an obstacle. He had not seen the will behind it.

That evening, the fog began to change.

It started with a sound. A low, melodic groan, like a great door opening on stone hinges. It came from the depths of the pinewood. Catriona heard it in the library and froze, a sheaf of legal papers falling from her hand. Vail, sitting by his fire, heard it and closed his eyes, listening not with his ears, but with the memory in his bones.

The groan was followed by a shift in pressure. The fog, which had been so still, began to stir. It began to flow with purpose, streaming through the trees, pouring down the burnsides, funnelling itself into the narrow mouth of the northern corrie. It was no longer a blanket. It was a river. A silent, grey river flowing uphill.

And as it flowed, it began to carry echoes.

Not echoes of the present, but of the past. The porous stone of the glen, saturated with memory and the new, strange resonance, began to breathe them out into the vapour.

At the periphery of the camp, a security guard sitting in his heated pod heard the clear, desperate shout of a man’s voice: *“Fàilte! Fàilte!”* He jerked upright, shining his torch into the wall of grey. Nothing. A few minutes later, the sound of iron-shod hooves on rock, a whole troop of them, passing close by. He radioed it in, his voice tense. Another guard, on the opposite side, reported the distinct smell of woodsmoke and roasting meat, and the fragment of a song in a language he did not know. The thermal cameras showed fleeting, human-shaped blobs of cold that appeared for a second and then dissipated, like breaths on the air. The lidar painted phantoms—shapes that had mass one moment and were gone the next.

Inside his Portakabin office, Forrester stared at the bank of monitors, his face lit by their cool glow. The data was nonsense. The interference wasn’t just static now; it was forming patterns. Shapes that looked like architectural outlines. Sound waves that resolved into harmonic frequencies no natural geological formation should produce. He had a team on the phone from Aberdeen, voices crackling with tired confusion. *“It’s like the bedrock is… singing, John. And the fog is acting as a sonic lens.”*

Forrester slammed his hand on the desk. “It’s not singing! It’s a trick! It’s guerrilla theatre with a fog machine and some buried speakers!” But his voice lacked conviction. He had seen tricks. This was different. The fog was cold and wet on his skin when he stepped outside. The sounds seemed to come from inside his own head. The sense of being watched was palpable, a pressure from the countless, unseen stones in the circling dark.

He gave the order. “Pull the perimeter guards in. Double them up. No one goes out alone. And get the meteorologist on the line. I want to know when this damn soup is going to burn off.”

*

Vail walked into the river of fog.

He left the cottage and moved through the pines, a shadow in the greater grey. He did not need to see. The path was in his feet. The fog parted for him, curling around his body like a living thing. The echoes swirled around him too—a snatch of piping, the clash of old steel, a woman’s laughter from a fire a thousand years cold. He did not fear them. They were the glen’s memories, stirred from their sleep by the discordant drum. The land was telling its stories, reminding itself who it was.

He walked for an hour, climbing high above the camp, until he stood on a rocky outcrop overlooking the corrie. The yellow lights below were smeared into fuzzy halos, the shapes of the cabins mere suggestions. The great drill rig was a ghost of steel, its outline blurred. The hum of the generators was strained, labouring against the damp.

He sat on the cold rock, his back against stone that had felt the weight of glaciers. He opened his mouth and began to hum.

It was not the full, complex chord of the cave. That was the glen’s own work now, reverberating through its subterranean chambers. This was a single, pure note. The foundational tone. The note of deep stillness. The note of patient, immutable stone.

He hummed it into the fog. He fed it into the stream of vapour and memory flowing downhill. He was not directing. He was reinforcing. Adding his own small, human certainty to the glen’s vast, ancient one.

Below, in the camp, the phenomena intensified.

A guard watching a monitor saw the thermal image of the entire hillside above the camp suddenly bloom with a network of fine, glowing lines, like a circuit board or a root system made of light. It lasted for three seconds and vanished.

Two engineers, trying to recalibrate a seismic sensor, watched in disbelief as the readout, instead of showing the simple *thump* of their test charge, displayed a complex, rhythmic waveform that matched no known geological signature. It looked, one would say later in the canteen, his hands shaking slightly, like a *heartbeat*.

And John Forrester, standing at the window of his cabin, staring into the impenetrable grey, heard it. Clear as a bell, right beside his ear. A voice, speaking in a calm, Gaelic lilt. It said only two words.

*“Thig a-mach.”*

*Come out.*

He spun around, his heart hammering against his ribs. The cabin was empty. The door was closed. The voice had been inside the room. Inside his head.

He was a rational man. He built his life on measurable forces, on tensile strength and profit margins. But in that moment, wrapped in the silent, watching fog, with the very data on his screens defying physics, a crack appeared in his certainty. It was a small crack, but it was deep. It was the crack through which fear seeps.

He reached for his radio, his hand unsteady. “All teams,” he said, his voice tighter than he intended. “Full lockdown. No one moves. We wait for daylight.”

*

The fog lifted with the dawn, not by drifting away, but by dissolving. One moment it was there, a solid world of grey. The next, it was thinning, retreating into the ground, into the burns, into the pores of the rock, until only a diamond-bright morning remained. The sky was a hard, clean blue. The hills were sharp-edged, every detail cruelly exposed. The camp, with its yellow boxes and its skeletal rig, looked suddenly small and fragile, a child’s toy abandoned on the vast, ancient floor of the corrie.

Vail stood from his rock perch, his limbs stiff with cold. He looked down at the camp. Men were emerging, blinking in the light, their faces pale. They moved slowly, looking over their shoulders, speaking in low tones. The night had left its mark. It was not in anything they could report, but in the set of their shoulders, in the way they glanced at the surrounding hills not as a landscape, but as a presence.

He had done what he could. The glen had spoken its first, unmistakable word. It was a word of disorientation, of memory, of subtle, psychological siege.

But as he turned to go, his eye caught a new movement. At the far end of the camp, a different kind of vehicle had arrived. A large, windowless van. Men in dark, unmarked uniforms were unloading equipment. Not drilling equipment. Technical gear. Antenna arrays. Dish-like sensors on tripods. They began setting it up in a precise circle around the main drill site, their movements efficient, professional, devoid of the confusion of the other workers.

Forrester was there, talking to a tall, thin man in a civilian coat. The man nodded, his face impassive, and pointed to the rig. The new equipment was not for extraction. It was for listening. Deeper, and with a different intent.

The company was no longer just probing for minerals. They had felt the anomaly. The singing rock. The intelligent fog. The ghost echoes on their scans. They had categorised it not as superstition, but as an *unknown variable*. And unknown variables, to a certain kind of mind, were not just obstacles. They were potentially valuable. A new kind of resource. A phenomenon to be isolated, studied, and understood. Controlled.

Vail felt the cold of the dawn seep into his marrow. The game had just changed. The blunt instrument of the drill was one thing. A focused, intelligent curiosity was another. It was a sharper, more dangerous blade.

He descended the hill, the brilliant morning light feeling like an exposure. The glen’s first move had been a masterstroke of mist and memory. But the answer had not been retreat. It had been the deployment of a finer, more analytical net.

He reached his cottage as the sun cleared the eastern ridge. The kettle began to whistle as he stepped inside, its note clean and simple. He did not smile. He stood in the doorway, looking back up the glen towards the now-visible wound of the camp.

The crude, discordant drum was silent for now. But in its place, he could almost hear the new sound—the high, thin whine of sensitive machines listening, listening, listening for the source of the song.

They were no longer just trying to cut the glen open. They were trying to eavesdrop on its soul.

He closed the door, but the sound was already inside, a needle-thin vibration in the quiet of his home.



---

# Chapter 15

## **THE STONE AND THE VEIL**

### **Chapter 15**

The high, thin whine was not a sound of the glen. It was a foreign frequency, a vibration that lived in the wires and the silicon, in the polished casings of machines that had never known peat-smoke or the taste of rain. It did not fade with the day’s turning, as the birdsong faded or the wind shifted. It was a constant, a pressure behind the ear, a metallic taste at the back of the tongue. It was the sound of civilisation listening, and its listening was a form of violence.

Vail spent the day in a state of attenuated quiet. He walked the high moor above the cottage, his movements deliberate, a man measuring the borders of a newly drawn cage. From the bald summit of Creag na h-Iolaire, he could see it all. The camp was a geometric scar, the rig a black exclamation mark. Around it, the new equipment formed a pale, precise circle—a chalk ring around an insect, a laboratory seal. Men in clean jackets moved between the units, checking readouts, adjusting dials. There was no shouting, no wasted motion. It was a clinic. The patient was the earth, and the diagnosis was pending.

He watched a Land Rover disgorge two more figures. They carried cases shaped for delicate instruments. One of them paused, lifted his head, and scanned the encircling hills with binoculars. The lenses swept across the heather, over the outcrops, and for a moment, they fixed on the dark speck that was Vail against the skyline. The gaze lingered, analytical, unblinking, before moving on. It was not the look of a man who saw a shepherd or a local curiosity. It was the look of a technician noting an environmental variable. Vail felt a cold clarity settle in his gut. He was no longer merely an obstacle. He was a data point.

The glen, in response, had grown preternaturally still. It was not the stillness of peace, but the stillness of a held breath. The birds were sparse, their calls clipped and distant. The wind had died to a whisper, as if wary of carrying any trace of itself to those listening ears. Even the Allt Coire na Saobhaidh seemed to run more quietly, its waters hushed over the stones. It was conserving itself. Withdrawing. The brilliant mist of the dawn had been a shout. This was a retreat into a deeper silence, a gathering in the marrow of the rock.

As dusk began to pool in the corries, Vail descended not to his cottage, but to the old shieling by the lochan. The bothy was a low, humped ruin, its roof long gone, its walls holding only memory and the slow creep of sphagnum moss. He needed stone that had not forgotten. He sat on the broken lintel, his back to the modern world, and let the twilight seep into him.

The change came not as a sound, but as a texture in the air.

The temperature dropped, not with the gradual cool of evening, but in a single, breath-stealing plunge. The lochan’s surface, a moment before stirred by a faint breeze, became a sheet of polished lead. From the water, and from the saturated peat around it, a fog began to rise. It did not billow or drift. It *coalesced*. It pulled itself up from the ground in thick, vertical ropes that twisted slowly upon themselves, braiding the failing light into strange, bruised colours: the purple of a fresh bruise, the grey of a week-old corpse, the deep, arterial green of deep pool water.

Vail sat motionless. This was not the blanket mist of before. This was architecture.

The braids of fog wove together, forming shapes that hovered at the edge of recognition. A suggestion of antlers, vast and branching, that dissolved into a tangle of thorn. The outline of a long, low body that flowed like a current before breaking into a school of smaller, darting forms. A tall, slender pillar that might have been a figure, until a swirl of vapour became an arm, a hand, fingers that stretched and then frayed into trailing moss. There were no faces, only focal points—gatherings of darker vapour where something like attention might reside.

It was a council. A gathering of the glen’s oldest voices, using the only medium left to them that the listening machines might not yet fully comprehend: form and symbol, written in water and cold air.

A single braid, thicker and darker than the rest, detached from the loom of mist. It slid across the still surface of the lochan, leaving no ripple, and came to rest a yard from Vail’s boots. It condensed, solidified, until it was no longer fog, but a sculpture of hoar frost and shadow. It was the stag. The one from the story. The one from the knife-cut in the first light of the world. It stood, head high, its frost-antlers catching the last cobalt gleam of the sky, a creature of absolute, silent sovereignty.

Then it turned its head, and the hollows where eyes would be were filled with the reflected, dying gleam of the rig’s security lights, miles away but piercing the gloom. A tiny, orange pinprick in each socket. The message was not gentle. It was a mirror. *This is what they bring. This is the light they give us.*

The frost-stag dissolved, not into mist, but into a shower of crystalline dust that fell without a sound onto the peat. The other forms began to unravel, sinking back into the earth and water from which they had come. The cold lessened by a degree. The audience was over.

The meaning was clear. The glen’s first, grand defensive gesture had been met not with fear, but with instrumentation. The conversation had moved to a different plane. It was no longer a battle of wills, but of wavelengths. The entity in the rock, the intelligence in the water and the mist, could hide from drills and dynamite. But could it hide from a focused, scientific search for anomalous energy? From spectral analysis and ground-penetrating resonance scans?

Vail walked back in the dark, the image of those orange pinpricks in the frost-stag’s eyes burning in his mind. He understood the new danger. Forrester and the man in the coat were not trying to conquer a superstition. They were trying to isolate a signal. And once a signal was isolated, it could be mapped. It could be traced to its source. It could, theoretically, be *replicated*. Or silenced.

His cottage was a pocket of stale warmth. He lit no lamp. He stood at the window, looking at the constellation of artificial lights in the distance. The high whine was in the walls here, too, a subliminal itch.

He knew what he had to do. It was a thing Iain MacAnndra would never have contemplated, an act that crossed from guardianship into something more active, more dangerous. The old man had been a warden, a keeper of the balance. But the balance was now tilting on a scale calibrated in gigahertz and milliteslas. Passive witness was no longer enough.

He went to the old sea-chest at the foot of his bed. Beneath blankets and old journals, his hands found cold, heavy metal. He lifted out the rifle. It was a Lee-Enfield .303, a relic from a different war, its stock dark with the oil of long-gone hands. His father’s. It had been used for deer, for the occasional rogue fox. It was a tool of the glen. Now he cleaned it methodically, the rituals of disassembly and oiling a meditation in the dark. The parts came together with solid, oiled clicks.

But this was not the true weapon. The rifle was a statement, a last resort. The real work was subtler. He needed to speak the glen’s language back to it, to give it a new way to hide. He needed to become not just a warden, but a saboteur of the invisible.

He slept fitfully, the rifle leaning against the chair by the door, a stark, vertical line in the gloom. He dreamed of Iain MacAnndra, but the old man was not cleaning a stag. He was at the drill site, his grey knife in his hand. He was not cutting flesh. He was cutting cables, cleanly, one after another, the severed ends writhing like copper worms. And from each cut, not blood, but a spill of dark, silent mist poured forth, filling the compound, swallowing the listening machines whole.

He woke in the pre-dawn, the dream’s cold logic lodged in his chest. The first step was observation. He needed to see their new eyes.

An hour before true light, he was on the hillside above the camp, a grey shape in the greyer heather. The air was biting, the stars still sharp chips of ice. Below, the camp was a pool of electric daylight, stark and ugly. The listening array was powered up, a ring of soft, green LED glow. He focused on it through his old brass telescope.

The equipment was sleek, alien. Panels displayed scrolling waveforms—spikes and sine waves dancing in real time. One of the technicians emerged from a Portakabin, holding a tablet. He walked to a central unit, tapped the screen, and a low, audible hum pulsed through the quiet—a deep, probing *thrum* that Vail felt in his teeth. It lasted five seconds, then ceased.

Immediately, the waveforms on all the other units spiked chaotically. The technician studied his tablet, frowning. He made an adjustment. The thrum came again. Another spike, different this time, a complex cascade of energy that scrolled across the screens before dampening into a faint, rhythmic pulse. A heartbeat. They had found a residual rhythm. They were pinging the glen, and measuring its echo.

Vail tracked the technician’s gaze. He was not looking at the rig. He was looking east, towards the deep fold of land where the Allt Coire na Saobhaidh emerged from its darkest, most overhung ravine—the place the old maps called *Sloc na Sìth*, the Hollow of Peace. The place where Iain MacAnndra had found his stag. The technician pointed. The man in the civilian coat, who had emerged silently from another cabin, followed the gesture and nodded.

They had triangulated. They had a locus. The source of the “unknown variable” was not diffuse. It was localised. It was in the Hollow.

A cold fury, clean and sharp, rose in Vail. It was the fury of a man seeing a sacred tomb being measured for its door. He lowered the telescope. The dawn was beginning, a pale smear over the eastern hills. In another hour, they would likely send a team. To take samples. To place more sensors. To lay their clinical hands on the heart of it.

He moved quickly, silently, back down the slope, keeping to the folds of the land. He did not go home. He went to the byre behind his cottage and began to load a pack. Not with tools of destruction, but with tools of redirection. A small, powerful magnet wrapped in sheep’s wool. A hand-cranked radio, an old thing that could squeal across frequencies. A bottle of iron-rich silt from the lochan bed, thick and colloidal. These were his answers to their machines. Nonsense signals. Magnetic static. A scatter of particulate to confuse their sensors. He would be a ghost in their wires, a will-o’-the-wisp in their data.

He slung the pack and the rifle. As he turned to leave the byre, he saw her.

Catriona stood in the doorway, backlit by the weak dawn. She wore a heavy coat, her hair loose and wild from the wind of her walk. Her face was pale, set in lines of grim understanding. She had seen the pack, the rifle.

“They’ve found it, haven’t they?” she said. Her voice was flat, drained of its usual music.

“The Hollow,” Vail confirmed.

She stepped inside, closing the door behind her. The intimacy of the dim, dusty space was sudden and profound. “I heard the new machines. Felt them. It’s like a headache in the ground.” She looked at his preparations. “You can’t just shoot them, Euan.”

“I know that. This isn’t for shooting. It’s for… talking in a language they might notice.”

“They’ll arrest you. Or worse, they’ll section you. The man in the coat is from the government. Some quiet branch. Forrester answers to him now.”

“What would you have me do?” The question was not a challenge, but a genuine ask. He was tired of the solitude of this knowing.

Catriona was silent for a long moment. She reached out and touched the stock of the rifle, not with fear, but with recognition. “My great-uncle carried one like this. At Loos.” She withdrew her hand. “He said the worst thing wasn’t the noise. It was the silence just before the barrage. The knowing what was coming.” She met Vail’s eyes. “This is our silence. The barrage is their science. You can’t fight a barrage head-on. You have to undermine it. You need a diversion.”

“A diversion.”

“They’re listening for the song of the stone,” she said, her voice dropping, taking on the cadence of the old stories. “So give them a different song. A louder one. From the wrong place.”

Vail understood. Misdirection. Use their technology against them. Create a phantom signal, an anomaly so compelling it would draw their focus away from the Hollow while he worked to obscure the true source.

“The old lead mine,” he said. “On the west face. Seams of quartz and metal. It echoes like a bell in the right conditions.”

“Aye. It’s a wound, but a shallow one. It can bear the attention.” She reached into her pocket and pulled out a small, cloth-wrapped bundle. She unfolded it. On her palm lay a piece of the singing rock, the size of a hen’s egg. It hummed faintly, a vibration felt more than heard. “Take this. Put it deep in the mine adit. Their machines will scream when they sense it. It will be the brightest light on their map. It will take them days to drill and probe it, to find it’s just a fragment.”

Vail took the stone. Its song resonated up his arm, a familiar, sorrowful chord. “And the Hollow?”

“While they’re fascinated with their new toy,” Catriona said, a fierce, sharp light in her eyes, “you do what you must. And I’ll do what I can. There are still people here. We can be a noise they can’t filter out. Complaints. Obstructions. Folk seeing strange lights. Bury them in human static.”

It was a plan. Fragile, desperate, but a plan. A fusion of the old ways and a new, cunning resistance. For the first time since the whine began, Vail did not feel entirely alone.

“Thank you,” he said, the words inadequate.

Catriona shook her head. “Don’t thank me. Just be effective. And be careful. That man in the coat… he doesn’t look like he believes in ghosts. But he looks like he’d very much like to catch one.”

She left as silently as she had come, melting back into the dawn. Vail stood for a moment, holding the singing rock in one hand, the strap of the rifle in the other. He was a bridge between worlds, armed with a fossil and a firearm, tasked with defending a secret with a lie.

He set out, not towards the Hollow, but west, towards the scar of the old mine. The sky lightened to a hard, porcelain grey. As he climbed, the high whine from the camp seemed to sharpen, focusing, as if it had caught a new scent on the air. His scent. Or the scent of the stone in his pack.

He reached the mine by mid-morning. The adit was a black mouth in the hillside, weeping rust-coloured water, fringed with broken ferns. The silence here was different—empty, dead, a silence of abandonment. He entered, the cool damp closing around him. Fifty yards in, by the light of his headlamp, he chose a spot: a crack in a quartz seam, glittering dully. He wedged the fragment of singing rock deep into the fracture, packing it with wet clay and gravel. It was a seed. A lure.

As he worked, he felt a change. The dead silence of the mine began to *stir*. A faint, almost imperceptible vibration trembled in the air. The water dripping from the ceiling altered its rhythm. The rock fragment, in contact with the quartz vein, was singing. Not its own lonely song, but a song amplified and distorted by the geometry of the mine, reflected and refracted by the crystal faces. It was no longer a hum. It was a rising, resonant chord, deep and strange, that seemed to come from the very bones of the mountain.

He retreated, quickly. As he emerged, blinking, into the daylight, he heard it—a palpable vibration thrumming through the soles of his boots. The mine was singing a false, magnificent bass note to the sky.

He moved to a vantage point and raised his telescope towards the camp.

For a moment, nothing. Then, chaos.

The orderly green LEDs of the listening array flashed into frantic red. Technicians spilled from the cabins, staring at screens, pointing west, towards the mine. The man in the coat appeared, followed by Forrester. The technician with the tablet was speaking rapidly, jabbing his finger at his display, showing a massive, stunning spike on his waveform. The source was no longer a faint pulse from the east. It was a blazing, undeniable beacon from the west. The man in the coat took the tablet, studied it, his face a mask of intense, focused interest. He gave a short, sharp order.

Forrester began shouting. Men scrambled. A team was mobilised—not geologists this time, but the technical crew, loading their most sensitive mobile scanners into a tracked Argocat. They were going to investigate. Immediately. The Hollow was forgotten. The new signal was too powerful, too provocative to ignore.

Vail allowed himself a single, tight breath. The diversion had taken.

He turned and began to run, not walk, along the high, hidden deer trails that led back towards the Hollow of Peace. He had a window, perhaps only hours. He needed to use the tools in his pack, to weave a cloak of interference around the true heart of the glen before their curiosity was satisfied with the mine’s false song and they turned their hungry, intelligent eyes back to the east.

The sun climbed, a cold coin in a hard sky. Below him, the Argocat, a clumsy insect, began its slow crawl up the opposite slope towards the singing mine. The high whine from the camp shifted, modulating, focusing its attention on the new target.

But as Vail ran, the cold air burning in his lungs, a new thought crystallised, cold and sharp as the quartz in the mine. The man in the coat had not looked surprised by the massive new signal. He had looked… satisfied. As if a hypothesis had been confirmed. As if he had been waiting for the glen to make a move.

What if the diversion was not a diversion he had created, but one he had been meant to find? What if the listening array was not just listening, but *herding*?

The hook for the next chapter pulled taut in his mind, sharper than any blade. He ran faster, the weight of the rifle on his back a reminder that some silences, once broken, could only be answered with a sound that was final.



---

# Chapter 16

## **THE STONE AND THE VEIL**

### **Chapter 16**

The deer trail was a memory in the heather, a ghost-line known only to the red deer and the men who had become like them. Vail followed it, his breath a ragged plume in air that tasted of ice and peat-smoke. The weight of the pack, of the rifle, was nothing against the new weight in his chest: the cold, quartz-sharp suspicion that he had not out-thought the man in the coat, but had been out-played.

The glen unfolded below him, a bowl of muted colour—ochre, umber, the bruised purple of dying bell heather. The Allt Coire na Saobhaidh was a silver suture in its floor. To the west, the tracked Argocat was a black beetle crawling up the shoulder of Sgùrr nan Clach, towards the old mine. Its progress was slow, deliberate. The high, insectile whine from the camp had changed pitch; it was a focused, probing hum now, a needle seeking a groove. They were scanning the mine with a precision that felt surgical. Testing. Interrogating the stone.

Vail’s window was closing with every methodical turn of the Argocat’s tracks. He ran not with panic, but with the grim economy of a man crossing a narrowing ledge. The landscape offered no comfort, only clarity. The pines were stark, their branches like cracked bones against the sky. A hoodie crow lifted from a lone rowan, its call a rusty hinge swinging in the wind. It was a land that showed its teeth.

He reached the high rim of the Hollow of Peace as the sun cleared the eastern peak, flooding the glen with a light that was bright but carried no warmth. It was the light that showed every fissure, every scar. He dropped to his knees behind a tumble of lichen-crusted boulders, shrugging the pack from his shoulders. His hands, cold-stiff, worked the buckles with practised haste.

The tools were not tools of his world. They were artefacts of the new one, the one that listened to stones and spoke in data. A compact signal generator, its casing matte black and pitted from use. A spool of hair-thin superconducting wire. A palm-sized quantum battery, cold to the touch even through his gloves. He had taken them, over months, from forgotten corners of research labs, from obsolete projects, the way a magpie takes shiny things. They were pieces of a shield he had never hoped to fully assemble.

His plan had been simple: to weave a net of interference around the Hollow, a white-noise cloak in the electromagnetic spectrum that would blur the glen’s true, soft song into the background hiss of the earth. To make it invisible to their hungry arrays. But the plan had been predicated on a simple adversary—a curious, technological beast snuffling for a scent. Not a hunter who laid trails of his own.

He unspooled the wire, beginning to lay it in a wide, irregular arc along the rim, pegging it down with titanium stakes. The generator hummed to life, a sub-audible vibration he felt in his teeth. He set its parameters: not a blanket of noise, but something subtler. A mimic. He programmed it to echo, with a slight delay and a corrupted harmonic, the strong, clean signal now pouring from the mine. To make the Hollow’s energy signature appear as a faint, flawed reflection of that western source. A ghost in the machine’s own data, easily dismissed as an echo, a phantom.

It was a risk. It tied the Hollow’s concealment to the very diversion he now doubted. If they silenced the mine’s song, his cloak would be a dead circuit. He worked faster, the cold granite biting through his trousers. As he worked, his eyes kept scanning the western slope. The Argocat had reached the mine adit. Figures disembarked, small and dark as ants. They unloaded scanners on tripods, began to sweep the entrance. There was no urgency. They were not investigating an anomaly; they were cataloguing a specimen.

The thought crystallised, hard and final. *Herding*. The array was not passive. Its pulses, its focused beams, were a form of pressure. A cattle-prod for the earth. They had been poking the glen, and the glen, in its deep, slow way, had flinched. The mine’s song was not a random surge. It was a reaction. A pain response.

And he had led them right to the tender spot.

A sound cut the air, not from the west, but from the north-east. A low, resonant *thrum*, felt more than heard, a vibration that passed up through the rock into the marrow of his bones. It was the sound a great cable makes when plucked. It lasted three heartbeats, then faded into the wind.

The glen had spoken again. Not from the mine. From the direction of the Standing Stone in the high corrie.

In the camp below, the insectile whine stuttered, shifted, and split into two distinct tones. One remained locked on the mine. The other began to sweep, searching, towards the north-east.

Vail froze, a stake half-driven in his hand. They had heard it. Of course they had heard it. Their ears were everywhere. The stone’s call was a clearer beacon than any he could fabricate. His mimicry cloak was useless before it. He was a man trying to hide a bonfire by lighting a candle beside it.

He looked down at the half-laid wire, the humming generator. A child’s dam against a spring tide. A profound and weary understanding settled over him. He could not hide this place. The conversation between the glen and the array had begun. He was not a guardian here. He was a witness. Or perhaps merely an intercept.

He pulled the stakes from the ground, coiled the wire with slow, deliberate movements. The generator he silenced. The tools of civilisation went back into the pack, inert, defeated. He kept out only the rifle. Its oiled wood and blued steel were an older answer, a simpler grammar. He checked the bolt, the magazine. The cartridges were long, heavy, their brass cold against his fingers.

He rose. The decision was made not in his mind, but in his body, in the set of his shoulders and the line of his sight. He would go to the Stone. If the glen was going to answer, he would hear its voice directly. And if the men in the camp came for it, he would be there. The final sound.

He moved off the rim, descending into the Hollow proper. Here, the air changed. The wind died. The cold was not the dry cold of the heights, but a damp, deep chill that rose from the moss and the dark, peaty water of the lochan at the Hollow’s heart. The silence was a presence. It was not an absence of sound, but a fullness, a density that absorbed the crunch of his boots on frost, the rustle of his coat. It was the silence of a held breath.

He circled the lochan, its water black and perfect as a slab of obsidian. On the far side, the ground began to rise again towards the high corrie. The path was a seam of pale quartzite that gleamed dully in the flat light. As he climbed, the *thrum* came again. Stronger now. It was not a sound in the air, but a pressure in the blood. It stirred a memory, not his own, but one that lived in the muscle and bone of his lineage—a memory of fires on hills, of voices chanting not words, but tones that matched the pitch of the land.

The corrie opened before him, a steep-walled amphitheatre carved by a glacier long dead. At its head, a scree slope of shattered grey rock lay beneath dark cliffs. And before the scree, standing alone in a basin of crushed grass, was the Stone.

It was taller than a man, but not by much. Its shape was irregular, a rough blade of bluish schist shot through with veins of milky quartz. It was not a monument. It was a fragment, a tooth left in the gum of the earth. It looked unworked, merely placed. But the placing was everything. It stood at the exact confluence of three visible streams that trickled from the cliffs, and it cast a shadow that, at midsummer sunset, pointed like a finger to the notch of the highest peak.

Vail approached slowly. The air around the Stone hummed, a faint, tactile resonance. The moss at its base was a vibrant, impossible green, a shock of life in the season’s decay. He stopped a few yards away, the rifle slung across his back, his hands empty.

He did not know what to do. He was a man of two worlds, and in this moment, both failed him. The science could measure the piezoelectric discharge, the subsonic resonance. The old stories spoke of gods and gateways. Neither language fit the simple, overwhelming *thereness* of the thing. It was a knot in the fabric of the place. A scar that had healed with power.

He reached out a hand, not to touch, but to feel the space between his skin and the stone.

A voice spoke behind him. Not the glen’s. A human voice.

“I rather thought you’d come here.”

Vail turned, his movement fluid, unstartled. The man in the grey coat stood at the edge of the corrie, twenty yards away. He had not approached up the quartz path. He seemed to have materialised from the shadow of a boulder. He held no weapon. His hands were in his coat pockets. His face was calm, inquisitive, like a scholar who has found an interesting text.

“Forrester’s pet geologist,” the man said. His voice was educated, precise, carrying easily in the still air. “Or perhaps his stalking horse. I admit, the rifle gave me pause. But the rest of your performance was compelling. The nervous energy. The clever, clumsy sabotage of the core sample. The panicked run to set up your little jamming array.” He nodded back towards the Hollow’s rim. “I watched you dismantle it. Wise. It would have been like throwing feathers at a tank.”

Vail said nothing. He let his weight settle, his breathing slow. His eyes scanned the corrie edges. The man was alone. For now.

“My name is Locke,” the man said, as if introducing himself at a seminar. “And you have been remarkably helpful, Mr….?”

“Vail.”

“Vail. Good. You led us to the secondary resonance. The mine. A fascinating reaction. The array’s focused beams trigger a sympathetic vibration in the quartz lattice. It’s like tapping a wine glass. The primary emitter, however,” he gestured with his chin towards the Stone, “seems to operate on a different principle. It is not merely reactive. It is… conversant.”

Locke took a step forward. His boots made no sound on the turf. “You feel it, don’t you? The pressure. The infrasound. It’s modulating. It responded to our scan of the mine. It’s learning the frequencies we use.”

“It’s not a machine,” Vail said, his voice rough from disuse.

“No,” Locke agreed, a spark of genuine enthusiasm in his eyes. “It’s better. It’s a self-regulating, naturally occurring piezoelectric oscillator, integrated into a geological network we are only beginning to map. It’s a transmitter. And a receiver.” He took another step. “The stories your people told, Vail. Of stones that sang on certain nights. Of hills that listened. They weren’t myths. They were user reports. Primitive, but accurate.”

“They were warnings,” Vail said.

“All new technologies are met with warnings,” Locke replied dismissively. He was closer now, ten yards away. His gaze was fixed on the Stone. “Do you know what this is? It’s a naturally formed quantum processor. The quartz veins are its circuitry. The stress of the earth’s plates provides the power. It doesn’t *think*, not as we do. But it processes information. Environmental data on a scale we can’t conceive. Tectonic shifts. Atmospheric pressure. The pull of the moon. It’s been compiling a model of this planet for millennia. And it broadcasts that model. A song of the earth.”

Vail’s mind recoiled from the explanation even as it understood it. It was a translation of mystery into data, and the translation felt like a violation.

“Why are you here?” Vail asked.

“To listen,” Locke said. “And to learn the language. To download the dataset. Imagine it, Vail. A complete, real-time planetary operating system. Climate modelling of perfect accuracy. Seismic prediction. Resource mapping beyond our wildest dreams. The end of uncertainty.”

“And then?”

Locke’s smile was thin, professional. “Then we learn to speak back. To make requests. To optimise.”

*To herd*, Vail thought. The final herd. Not of deer, or men, but of continents. Of weather. Of the deep, slow blood of the world.

“It won’t let you,” Vail said.

“It’s a system,” Locke corrected. “It has no *let*. It has parameters. Inputs and outputs. We are simply becoming one of its inputs. A significant one.” He finally looked directly at Vail, the scholar’s warmth gone, replaced by a flat, operational clarity. “You’ve served your purpose. The local guide. The canary in the coal mine. You led us to the key nodes. Now, I need you to step aside. My technical team is on its way. We’re going to place a non-invasive resonator against the Stone. To have a proper dialogue.”

Vail did not move. He stood between Locke and the Stone. “This isn’t your coal mine.”

“It’s not yours either,” Locke said, a note of impatience entering his voice. “It belongs to no one. It simply *is*. And the future belongs to those who understand what *is*.”

From below, in the glen, came the distinct, grinding sound of another vehicle. The second team. Coming for the Stone.

Locke heard it too. “Last chance, Vail. Walk away. Go back to Forrester. Tell him you found nothing but rocks and wind.”

Vail thought of Iain MacAnndra, carving the stag in the Glen of Shadows. The steam rising in slow, spiralling threads. The last breath of the hill, being called back. This was the same. This was taking the liver of the world, holding it not as a priest would, but as a butcher.

He unslung the rifle. The movement was not aggressive, but declarative. He did not raise it. He simply held it, one hand on the stock, the other by the trigger guard. A fact.

Locke’s eyes hardened. He sighed, a faint exhalation of disappointment. “Sentiment. The last refuge of the obsolete.” He raised a hand, a small device clenched in his fingers. A remote. “I anticipated resistance. Not from the glen. From its self-appointed priest.”

He pressed a button.

The world erupted in sound. Not from the Stone, but from the cliffs above them. A focused, agonising shriek of pure ultrasound, emitted from a dozen small, grey discs that Vail now saw fixed to the rock faces. They had been there all along. It was a sound that bypassed the ears and attacked the nerves, the bones, the fluid in the inner ear. Vail cried out, staggering, his hands flying to his head. The rifle fell to the grass. The world tilted, dissolved into nausea and blinding pain. He dropped to his knees, retching.

Locke watched, impassive. “A simple deterrent. Sonic territory marking. It will pass. Once you’re removed.”

Vail fought the urge to curl into a ball. He forced his eyes open, his vision swimming. He saw Locke walk past him, towards the Stone. He saw the man take a small, cylindrical device from his coat—the resonator. He saw him reach out to place it against the schist.

And he saw the Stone react.

The veins of quartz within it flared, not with light, but with a vibration so intense the air around the Stone seemed to liquefy, to shimmer like a heat haze. The *thrum* returned, but this time it was not a single note. It was a chord, deep and complex and furious. It hit the ultrasonic shriek from the cliffs and shattered it. The sound discs popped, one after another, in small puffs of smoke and ceramic dust.

Locke staggered back, the resonator falling from his hand. He clutched at his ears, blood trickling between his fingers. His face was a mask of shock, not at pain, but at contradiction. “Impossible… the frequency was calibrated…”

The ground trembled. Not an earthquake, but a localized shudder, as if the earth were shrugging a great weight from its shoulders. From the three streams at the head of the corrie, the water flow doubled, then trebled, not with runoff, but as if some deep spring had been tapped. Water poured over the rocks, clear and cold and fast.

And the Stone sang.

It was a sound beyond sound. It was the grind of tectonic plates, the sigh of roots in deep soil, the crack of frost-split rock, the whisper of a billion needles of pine in a high wind. It was the voice of the glen, and it was not a song of data. It was a song of identity. A declaration of self.

It hit Vail like a wave. He did not hear it with his ears. He felt it in the pattern of his heartbeat, in the synaptic firings of his brain, in the double helix that coiled in every cell of his body. It was a pattern that recognised his own. It was not welcoming. It was not hostile. It was *acknowledging*. A single, vast, indifferent, specific note: *I am here*.

Locke was on his hands and knees, vomiting, his body rejecting the frequency. His eyes were wide with a terror that was entirely human. He had come to talk to a machine. He had woken something else.

The song peaked, held, and then began to change. It turned outward, down the glen. It was no longer a declaration. It was a command.

In the camp, every screen went black. Every light died. The great listening array emitted a final, feedback-screech of protest, and then fell silent, its delicate electronics fused into uselessness. The high whine was cut off, leaving a vacuum of silence that was instantly filled by the wind, the burn, the call of a distant bird.

In the mine on Sgùrr nan Clach, the quartz vein that had blazed with false song went dark and dead. It would never sing again. The price for its betrayal was permanent silence.

The song faded. The vibration in the air stilled. The Stone was just a stone again, blue-grey and streaked with white, standing in a corrie filling with the music of rushing water.

The silence that followed was different. It was not the dense, holding silence of before. It was a clean, sharp, aftermath silence. A line drawn.

Vail climbed to his feet. The nausea was gone. His head was clear, scoured clean. He picked up his rifle. He walked over to where Locke lay, gasping, bleeding from his ears and nose.

The man looked up at him, his intellectual certainty shattered, replaced by primal confusion. “What… what was that?”

Vail looked down at him, then past him, at the Stone. At the glen unfolding below, now free of the insect hum, alive only with its own ancient voices.

“That,” Vail said, his voice quiet, “was the end of the conversation.”

He slung the rifle over his shoulder. He did not look at Locke again. He turned and began to walk down from the corrie, following the new, vigorous streams. The diversion was over. The herding was done. The glen had spoken its final word to civilisation.

But as he walked, the hook for the next chapter pulled taut, cold and sure. Locke’s team was still down there, blind and deaf. Forrester was still there, with his contracts and his greed. And the man in the grey coat had heard the song, not as a user report, but as a threat. They would not understand. They would only want to control what had controlled them. They would be coming back. Not with listeners. With silencers.

The glen had declared itself. And now, it would have to be defended. Not with jamming arrays, but with the final sound. Vail’s hand rested on the rifle’s stock. The stone had sung. Now came the veil of blood.



---

# Chapter 17

## **THE STONE AND THE VEIL**

### **Chapter 17**

The glen, in the wake of the song, was a patient convalescent. The air was rinsed. The light had a brittle, clarified quality, as if the world had been scoured with cold water and left to dry in a high wind. The false vibration, the insect hum of the listening machines, was gone. What remained was the old, familiar grammar of the place: the wind’s long vowels in the pines, the consonant clatter of the burn over rock, the soft punctuation of a raven’s call. It was a language Vail understood in his marrow. It was the sound of a thing returned to itself.

He walked down from the high corrie with the steady, ground-eating pace of a man leaving a shrine. His boots sank into sodden peat, skidded on wet scree. The new streams, born of the Stone’s release, chattered beside him, clear and urgent, cutting fresh channels through the heather. He did not look back. The Stone was a fact behind him, its work done. The man, Locke, was a broken instrument left among the rocks. The aftermath was not a place for lingering.

His mind was a clean, cold chamber. The nausea, the psychic pressure, had been burned away in the song’s final, clarifying note. In its place was a tactical clarity as sharp as frost. The diversion was over. The herding was done. The glen had declared its sovereignty in a language of pure force. It had drawn a line in the peat and the rock.

Civilisation would not read it.

That was the next fact. The only fact that mattered now. Locke’s team, blind and deaf below, would be stumbling, frightened, their systems slag. Forrester would be in his Portakabin, a map of profit suddenly gone silent, his calculations unspooling into panic. And the man in the grey coat—the one who listened not for profit but for power—would have heard the song’s echo on some deep, encrypted channel. He would not hear a natural phenomenon. He would hear a weapon. A declaration of war from the territory itself.

They would come back. Not with listeners. With silencers.

The thought settled in Vail’s chest, cold and dense. He adjusted the sling of the rifle on his shoulder, his fingers checking the safety by touch. The stone had sung. Now came the veil. And a veil was not a wall. It was a thing that obscured, that separated, that could be drawn across something precious. It could be made of mist, or shadow, or blood.

He reached the treeline where the pines began their climb, a ragged army of sentinels. The light here was fractured, pooled in deep amber on the needle-strewn floor. He moved into the shadows, his shape dissolving into the vertical lines of the trunks. This was his ground. The high corrie had been the glen’s pulpit. This was its nave.

A sound, not of the glen, reached him. A human sound. Muffled, frantic.

He stopped. Became a part of a tree’s shadow. His breath stilled.

Through a lattice of pine boughs, he saw a clearing fifty yards downhill. Three figures. Two of Locke’s technicians, their expensive jackets torn by branches, their faces pale smears of fear. They were supporting a third man between them, his legs dragging. It was the one who had operated the main array, the one with the headphones. Blood, dark and glossy, had run from his ears down his neck, staining his collar. His eyes were open, but they saw nothing of the pine or the light; they were fixed on some interior horror, wide and unblinking.

“—just keep moving, just get to the track,” one of the supporting men was gasping, his voice a ragged thread. “The Land Rover, we just need to—”

“He’s not hearing us, Rob. He’s not *seeing* us.”

“Doesn’t matter. Forrester will have a medic. Just move.”

They stumbled onward, a clumsy, three-legged creature of shock, heading roughly east, toward the old forestry track that led to the mine compound. They were fleeing the mountain’s rebuke. They were not a threat. They were evidence.

Vail let them pass. Their terror was a better weapon than any bullet; it would carry the story down the hill, a contagion of failure. He waited until the sounds of their crashing progress faded into the sigh of the wind. Then he moved again, angling south, taking a higher route that would bring him to the ridge above the mine compound. He needed to see. He needed to count.

The climb was steep, over outcrops of gneiss slick with moss. The physical effort was a kind of thinking. His body remembered the paths, the handholds, the way the land folded. His mind plotted vectors. Forrester first. The man was a node. Cut the node, the network would flail. Then the grey coat’s men, if they were here. They would be different. Quieter. Harder.

He reached the ridge. The wind here was a physical presence, shoving at his back, carrying the peat-smell of the high moor. Below, in the shallow bowl of the coire, the Sgùrr nan Clach mine compound lay like a scab.

The Portakabins were dark. No generator hum. The satellite dishes on the roofs were tilted at useless angles. A lone Land Rover was parked haphazardly, its driver’s door hanging open. He saw movement—Forrester, a bulky shape in a waxed jacket, pacing outside the main cabin, a satellite phone pressed to his ear. Even from this distance, Vail could read the man’s posture: the hunched shoulders, the jerky gestures of the free hand. The posture of a man shouting into a void.

No other movement. No sign of the grey coat’s team. Not yet.

Vail’s gaze travelled past the compound, down the glen to where the Allt Coire na Saobhaidh burn met the darker, slower waters of the main river. There, nestled in a fold of land, was the cottage. A thin tendril of peat-smoke rose from its chimney, straight up in the windless shelter of the hollow. Iain was there. The old man would have felt the song pass through the bedrock, through the roots of the heather. He would be waiting. Not with questions. With a kettle on the hob, and a deep, grim understanding.

That was his other flank. The fixed point.

A flicker of light from the compound. Not electric. The flare of a match. Forrester had lit a cigarette, the brief orange glow illuminating a face clenched tight with frustration. He threw the match down, resumed his pacing, the phone still at his ear.

Decision, cold and final, crystallised in Vail.

He began his descent, not toward the cottage, but directly toward the compound. He did not rush. He used the land’s contours as a blind, moving from the cover of a granite outcrop to the deep shadow of a ruined drystone dyke. He was a shadow lengthening, a stain of dusk spreading downhill.

He reached the edge of the cleared ground around the Portakabins. Twenty yards of open, churned mud and gravel to Forrester’s back. The man had turned, facing down the glen, his voice now audible in bursts as the wind snatched it.

“—complete blackout, do you understand? Total system failure… No, not just ours! Locke’s entire array is fried… The man himself is missing! I have three of his team back here looking like they’ve seen a… a ghost, or a… I don’t know what!”

A pause. Forrester’s head bowed. The voice that came next was lower, tighter, the voice of a man pleading with a bank manager.

“Listen to me. The acoustic anomaly is gone. *Gone*. The asset is silent. We have no product. We have a catastrophic failure on site, injuries… This is not a delay. This is a terminal event. The cost of containment alone…”

Another pause. Then, a sharp, bitter bark that was not a laugh. “Oh, I’ll secure it. There’s nothing *to* secure but a hole in a hill and a bunch of broken toys. What you need to send isn’t more auditors. It’s a clean-up crew. And a lawyer.”

He ended the call. Stood perfectly still for a long moment, the phone hanging from his hand. Then, with a sudden, violent motion, he hurled the phone against the side of the Portakabin. It shattered with a dull plastic crack.

Vail stepped out of the shadow of the dyke.

The sound of his boot on gravel was deliberate. A announcement.

Forrester spun around. His face, already pale, went the colour of curdled milk. His eyes darted past Vail, searching for others, finding only the empty, watching glen. He took an involuntary step back, his shoulders hitting the cold metal of the cabin wall.

“You.” The word was a puff of vapour.

Vail stopped ten feet away. He did not raise the rifle. It remained slung, his hands loose at his sides. His silence was more unnerving than any shout.

“What did you do?” Forrester’s voice climbed, fraying at the edges. “What in Christ’s name was that… that *noise*? Locke’s men are catatonic! My seismographs are fused! What did you *do*?”

“I listened,” Vail said, his voice flat, a stone dropped in a still pool. “You tried to make a tool of it. It doesn’t like tools.”

Forrester stared, his businessman’s mind struggling to parse the statement. The fear in his eyes was being crowded out by a hotter, more familiar emotion: rage at a spoiled deal. “You’ve destroyed everything. Years of work. Millions. Do you have any concept—”

“I have a concept of a glen,” Vail interrupted, the words cutting like a blade of ice. “It’s not a concept to you. It’s a column in a spreadsheet. Now the column is zero. Your work here is finished.”

“Finished?” Forrester pushed himself off the wall, a flush of anger returning some colour to his face. “You think this is over? This is a setback. A data point. The resource is still here. The mineral rights are still mine. That… that *phenomenon* just proved the value! We’ll come back with hardened systems, with dampeners, with—”

“You won’t.” Vail took a single step forward. It was not a threat of violence, but a imposition of truth. “You’re going to pack your broken toys. You’re going to take your shell-shocked men. You’re going to drive out of this glen. And you are never going to file another report, or sign another contract, for this piece of earth.”

Forrester’s laugh was a hollow thing. “Or what? You’ll shoot me? You’re one man. A hermit with a rifle. The world doesn’t work like that anymore. There are contracts. There are laws.”

“The glen just rewrote the law,” Vail said. “You felt it. Your machines recorded it. That was the only judgment that matters here. The sentence is exile.”

He saw the calculation flicker behind Forrester’s eyes. The assessment of risk, of leverage. The man was cornered, but he was a creature of paper and precedent. He believed in systems that moved slowly, that could be gamed. He did not believe in the finality of a mountain’s voice.

“You need to understand something,” Forrester said, forcing a veneer of calm over his panic. “I am not the decision-maker. I am a project manager. The people I answer to… they are not like me. They are not like Locke. They don’t get frightened by loud noises. They get interested. You haven’t ended this. You’ve just escalated it to a pay-grade you can’t possibly comprehend.”

The man in the grey coat. The listener for power.

“I comprehend it,” Vail said. “Let them come. Let them bring their silencers. They’ll find the silence here is already complete. And it’s hungry.”

For a long moment, they stood in the gathering dusk, the two poles of the conflict: the man of contracts, and the man of the corrie. The wind moaned through the guy-wires of the dead satellite dishes. Down in the compound’s other cabin, a low, keening sound began—one of the technicians, weeping.

Forrester’s shoulders slumped, not in defeat, but in a tactical retreat. The fight had gone out of him, replaced by a cold, professional desire to be elsewhere, to file the report, to transfer the blame. “We’ll leave. At first light. The vehicles need checking. The men need… sedating.”

Vail nodded once. It was not agreement. It was acknowledgment of an inevitable trajectory. “First light. Not a minute later. If you’re still here when the sun clears Sgùrr nan Clach, you will learn what the silence eats.”

He turned and walked away, back toward the treeline. He did not look back. He knew Forrester was watching him, knew the man’s mind was already racing down logistical paths, drafting the language of mitigation. It didn’t matter. The man was a husk, already emptied by the glen. The real threat was still coming.

As Vail melted back into the pines, a new sound threaded into the evening. Not the wind. Not the burn.

The distant, mechanical thrum of a helicopter, still miles away but approaching fast from the south-east. It was not the clattering, utilitarian sound of a search-and-rescue bird. This was a deeper, more purposeful beat, the sound of something sleek and expensive moving with intent.

Forrester heard it too. Vail saw him turn, head cocked, a confused hope dawning on his face. His clean-up crew? His lawyers?

But Vail knew. The tone was wrong. This was not a vehicle of salvage.

This was a vehicle of acquisition.

The hook pulled taut, cold and sure. The grey coat’s men were not waiting for a report. They were already in the air. They had heard the song, and they had not waited for the echo to fade. They were coming to secure the asset in its new, potent state of silence. To wrap it in a different kind of veil—one of concrete and keycards and controlled access.

The glen had declared itself. And civilisation, in its most ruthless, efficient form, was coming to accept the declaration as a challenge.

Vail broke into a run, not away from the sound, but perpendicular to it, heading for the high ground west of the corrie. He needed sight. He needed to see what was coming, and how many. The rifle bounced against his back. The clean, scoured feeling in his head was gone, replaced by the old, familiar pressure—the pressure of a storm front building, of a trap being set.

The stone had sung its warning.

Now the veil had to be drawn. And he was the only one who knew how to hold the thread.



---

# Chapter 18

## **THE STONE AND THE VEIL**

### **Chapter 18**

The helicopter’s thrum was a needle stitching the sky, drawing a seam of civilisation over the raw weave of the glen. Vail moved through the pines like a shadow dislodged from its tree, his boots finding the peat and stone without sound. The high ground west of the corrie was a broken spine of granite, a vantage that looked down the throat of the glen toward the distant, unseen road. He did not think of Forrester, that emptied man by the silent burn. He thought of vectors, of approach patterns, of the arrogance required to send such a machine into a place that had just finished swallowing a man whole.

He broke from the treeline onto a lichen-crusted shoulder of rock. The wind here was cleaner, sharper, carrying the scent of cold earth and the metallic promise of snow. He dropped to a crouch behind a cairn of tumbled stones, their edges black with old moss, and shrugged the rifle from his shoulder. The scope was cold against his eye.

The machine came into view not as a shape, but as a violation of the air. It was a sleek, matte-black Eurocopter, its lines predatory, its rotor-wash a visible distortion ploughing through the stable evening. It was not circling, not searching. It flew with the grim purpose of a surgeon’s blade, straight for the heart of the corrie where the stone stood and the burn ran silent. It was not Forrester’s people. It was the grey coat’s blade, unsheathed.

Vail tracked it, the crosshairs resting just below the cockpit. A breath held, a finger resting on the curve of the trigger guard. Not yet. The shot was possible. A fuel line, the tail rotor. He knew the angles. But a downed bird in the corrie was a beacon, a carcass of steel that would draw more of its kind, an infection of scrutiny. This required a cleaner cut.

The helicopter slowed, its nose tilting as it surveyed the ground. It hovered, a black dragonfly over a pond, then descended behind the screen of pines near the bothy. The thrumming deepened, became a physical pressure that vibrated in Vail’s teeth, then ceased as the engines wound down. An abrupt, ringing silence followed, more profound than before, as if the glen itself was holding its breath.

They were on the ground.

He counted them as they disembarked, small, efficient figures in dark tactical gear, not military but something smoother, more corporate. Four. They moved with the synchronized grace of a hive. One carried a case that was not for weapons. Another held a device—a tablet or a sensor array—and immediately began scanning the area, his head turning slowly like a radar dish. They ignored the bothy. They converged, instead, on the bank of the Allt Coire na Saobhaidh.

On the place where the water did not run.

Vail watched, the cold of the stone seeping up through his knees. This was the acquisition. They were not here for witnesses or explanations. They were here to sample the silence, to bottle the void. To take the glen’s declaration and file it under a project code.

He saw the one with the case kneel. He opened it, revealing not tools, but vessels: vials of thick, opaque glass, and a pump apparatus of brushed steel. He inserted a tube into the motionless water. The pump whirred, a sound like an insect’s wings, and the dead water climbed the tube, filling a vial. It looked wrong, that water in its glass prison—not fluid, but inert, a slug of lead-coloured gel. The man sealed it, labelled it, placed it in a cushioned slot. He took a second sample from the burn’s bed, a scoop of the grey, granular silt. A third from the air just above the water, using a different device that hissed softly.

They were cataloguing the pathology.

The man with the sensor swept it toward the standing stone. The device emitted a low, urgent ping. He spoke into his throat mic, words snatched away by the distance but their tone clear: clinical excitement. They had found the epicentre. Two of them broke away and approached the stone, but they did not touch it. They circled it at a respectful distance, sensors extended, recording every facet of its silence. One produced a camera and took pictures, not of the stone itself, but of the ground around it, of the specific pattern of the frost-heaved earth.

They understood, on some level, what they were handling. They did not see a monument. They saw a mechanism. A natural weapon that had been triggered.

A false victory, cold and complete, settled over Vail. He had thought the battle was between the glen and the men who would carve it up. He had thought his role was to be the grit in the machine, the witness who refused to be cleared away. He had forced Forrester to see, to hear the silence, and he had watched the man break against it. He had believed, for a scoured-clean moment, that the truth of the place was its own defence.

He was wrong.

The glen had not defended itself. It had performed. It had shown its capability. And these men, these quiet, efficient men in their tactical gear, were not afraid. They were *interested*. They were the ultimate expression of civilisation: a system that did not fear the wild, but sought to disassemble it, to isolate its principles, to replicate its effects in a sterile lab. They did not want to preserve the glen. They wanted to harvest its function. The silence wasn’t a warning to them; it was a data point. The stone wasn’t a sacred object; it was a natural phenomenon of immense applied potential.

Everything he had built—his understanding, his strategy of revelation—collapsed. It was not a shield. It was a blueprint, handed to the very enemy most capable of using it.

The men finished their work at the stone. They regrouped by the helicopter. One spoke into his mic again. Then, to Vail’s cold surprise, they did not leave. Two of them remained, taking up positions with the relaxed alertness of professional sentries. They were staying. Securing the site. The other two boarded the helicopter. The engines whined, the rotors began to turn, slicing the gathered quiet into ribbons. The black machine lifted, pivoted, and climbed away to the south-east, carrying its precious, stolen silence in a padded case.

The hook was not just taut; it had been set, and the line was now in someone else’s hands.

Vail retreated from the cairn, back into the pines. The pressure in his head was no longer that of a coming storm. It was the pressure of a sealed chamber. He had drawn the veil aside to show Forrester the abyss, and now a different kind of eye had looked in, had measured its depth, and had found it useful.

He moved north and east, keeping to the high ground, circling back toward the bothy from the unseen side. He needed to see, to understand the perimeter they had established. The two sentries were professionals. They would have night vision, comms, motion sensors. They were a tripwire. The real force, the analysis, was now elsewhere, in some windowless facility.

As dusk bled the last colour from the land, he found a perch on a heather-clad bluff overlooking the bothy from three hundred yards. He glassed the area. One sentry was visible, a dark shape leaning against the stone wall of the ruin, a silhouette against the pale grey of the dead burn. The other would be mobile, patrolling a circuit. They were not hiding. Their presence was a statement: *This is now a controlled site.*

A flicker of movement near the treeline across the corrie. Not the sentry. Something lower, four-legged. A red deer hind, moving tentatively, her head high, nostrils flaring. She took a few steps toward the burn, toward her usual watering place, then stopped. Her whole body stiffened. She did not sniff the water. She stared at it, ears flat back. Then she turned, not with the graceful leap of alarm, but with a slow, stiff-legged dread, and melted back into the pines.

Even the animals knew. The silence had a footprint now, a territory. And the men in their tactical gear were standing in its heart, unaffected.
