The thought was a cold stone in his gut. They had protection. Counter-measures. Something derived from the samples they’d already taken, or from prior knowledge. They were not operating blindly. This was not their first veil.

Darkness fell, absolute and starless, a blanket of cloud drawn over the glen. The cold deepened, a crystalline cold that spoke of the high mountains. Vail watched the sentry’s position. A pinprick of green light flared once—the glow of a night-vision monocle being adjusted. Time stretched, measured only by the slow creep of frost over the heather.

Then, a new sound.

Not the helicopter returning. Not the wind.

A voice.

It came from the direction of the bothy, carried on the still, cold air with unnatural clarity. It was a man’s voice, calm, conversational, amplified just enough by the strange acoustics of the silent corrie.

“Mr. Vail.”

The voice did not shout. It simply spoke, knowing it would be heard.

“We know you’re out there. We have thermal imaging from the bird. We’ve had a lock since you left the treeline.”

Vail did not move. He let his breath slow, his body become part of the rock and the frost. It could be a bluff. The thermal signature on cold stone, through dense heather, was not a sure thing.

“My name is Locke,” the voice continued. It was educated, smooth, devoid of Forrester’s brittle anxiety or the grey coat’s bureaucratic chill. This was a different caste. “We’d prefer to talk. You have a unique… contextual understanding of this location. One we value.”

Vail remained still. The hook was not just set; it was being played with a gentle, insistent tension.

“The individual you spoke with earlier, Mr. Forrester,” Locke said, as if discussing a mildly disappointing colleague. “He is currently experiencing a profound non-localised traumatic stress response. He’s of no further use. You, however, saw the event. You witnessed the transition. And you remain… functional. That is data.”

So, Forrester was catatonic. Emptied. A husk. They had found him, assessed him, and discarded him.

“Come to the bothy, Mr. Vail. No weapons. We are not your enemies. We are simply the next phase. The phase that understands what this place truly is. You can help us contain it. Or you can watch from the outside as we learn to use it.”

The words hung in the frozen air. *Learn to use it.* There it was. The deepest truth, laid bare. Civilisation’s answer to nature’s power was not reverence, nor was it fear. It was appropriation. The glen was not a temple to be preserved or a monster to be slain. It was a resource to be mined. Its silence, a tool. Its stone, a component.

A false victory shattered. He had believed the glen’s truth was a weapon against the men who would spoil it. But the glen’s truth was merely a new kind of spoil, more valuable than timber or water or landscape. He had not protected it by revealing it; he had advertised it to the only buyers sophisticated enough to bid.

He saw a green light wink on across the corrie, then another. Not lights, but the glowing indicators of equipment. They were activating something. A low hum, barely at the threshold of hearing, vibrated through the ground. It was not the living hum of the earth. It was a manufactured frequency, a synthetic counter-pulse.

They were not just studying the silence.

They were trying to *tune* it.

Vail slowly, silently, slid backwards off the bluff, into the deeper blackness of a peat hag. The cold mud swallowed his ankles. The decision was not whether to answer Locke’s call. The decision was what to do now that the war had changed its shape. He could not fight these men directly. They were too well-equipped, too prepared. They represented a will that was impervious to the old ways, to the terror of the unseen.

But the glen was not a passive resource. It was an active, hungry thing. It had declared itself. And Locke’s men, for all their technology and their confidence, were standing in its mouth. Their synthetic hum was a provocation. A tapping on the glass of a cage.

He thought of the stone. Not as a monument, but as a keystone. A plug. He thought of the silence not as a field, but as a flow, diverted, held back by that ancient, carved sentinel. What if the event with Forrester was not the stone singing? What if it was the stone *straining*?

Locke wanted to use the power. But what if the power was not the silence itself, but what the silence was holding in?

A new hook pulled, deep in the fabric of his understanding, cold and terrifying. He had been trying to protect the veil from being torn. But what if the real danger was not the tearing, but the careful, scientific *unpicking* of its stitches by men who did not believe in what lay beneath?

He moved through the peat hag, away from the bothy, away from the sentries and their humming machines. He needed to get to the high sanctuary, to the bothan of the old man. He needed the journal, the maps, the fragments of lore that spoke not of what the stone did, but of why it had been placed. He had looked for answers in the glen’s present. He now needed its past.

The glen had declared itself to Forrester as an end.

To Locke, it was a beginning.

Vail now feared it was neither. It was a threshold. And the men in their tactical gear, with their vials and their sensors, were preparing to cross it, thinking they controlled the gate.

He broke into a low, loping run, the rifle a dead weight on his back. The false victory was ashes. The deeper truth was a door, and Locke had the key in his hand. Vail’s only remaining purpose was not to guard the door.

It was to ensure that when it opened, it consumed the men who thought themselves its masters.

The mechanical hum from the corrie faded behind him, swallowed by the older, deeper quiet of the mountains. But the vibration lingered in his bones. It was the sound of the veil being tested, not by a shout, but by a calculated, scientific whisper.

And from the direction of the standing stone, felt more than heard, came the faintest, coldest tremor of reply.



---

# Chapter 19

## **THE STONE AND THE VEIL**

### **Chapter 19**

The high sanctuary was not a place one found. It was a place one was permitted to approach.

Vail moved through the gloaming not as a man crossing land, but as a needle drawing a thread through worn cloth. He did not follow the deer trails or the old peat-cutters’ paths. He navigated by the memory in his muscles, by the subtle pressure changes in the air against his cheek, by the way the lichen grew thicker on the north faces of certain boulders that were not entirely boulder. The land here was a palimpsest, older stories written beneath the skin of heather and stone. The mechanical hum from Locke’s corrie was gone, erased by distance and the dense, absorbing silence of the upper glen. But its echo was inside him now, a tuning fork struck against his spine.

He had run until his lungs burned with cold fire, then slowed to this predatory walk. The rifle was a blasphemy here, its synthetic stock and polymer grip an affront to the peat and the primordial schist. He carried it anyway. The men who tested the veil with whispers would carry worse.

The bothan of the old man, Ruaridh, was not a building. It was a geological afterthought. A slab of basalt had slumped against the shoulder of Sgùrr nan Ceannaichean millennia ago, and under its lee, time and peat and human need had conspired to create a pocket of stillness. From twenty paces, it was a shadow. From ten, a tumble of rocks furred with moss. You saw it only when you were meant to, when the angle of light fell just so, or when the wind shifted and carried the faint, peat-smoke scent of a life lived deliberately small.

Vail paused at the edge of the small, stony clearing before the overhang. No light showed. No dog barked. The silence was total, but it was a different quality than the manufactured quiet down in the corrie. This was a silence of absorption, of deep listening. It did not reject sound; it digested it.

“*A Ruaridh*,” Vail said, his voice low, not a shout but a release of breath shaped into a name. It vanished into the quiet without an echo.

A long moment passed. A dipper, slate-grey and white, bobbed on a stone in the burn that trickled from under the basalt slab, then flew with a sharp *tzic* into the gathering dark. The curtain of moss hanging across the bothan’s entrance stirred, not from the wind, for there was none.

The old man emerged like a root from the earth. He was thin to the point of transparency, his frame a collection of weathered sticks wrapped in faded wool and oiled canvas. His hair was the colour of old frost, long and tangled, and his eyes were the shocking, clear blue of a winter sky over ice. They held no surprise, only a vast, weary knowing.

“Iain MacAnndra’s boy,” Ruaridh said. His voice was the sound of dry bracken rubbing together. “You have the stink of fear on you. And of iron that is not honest iron.”

Vail inclined his head. The old man had never used his adopted name. To him, Vail would always be the son of the man who had read the stag’s liver and known the weather of the soul. “The glen is sick, Ruaridh. And the medicine men have come with poison.”

Ruaridh turned and ducked back inside. An invitation. Vail followed, stooping beneath the low lintel.

The interior was a cave of shadows and gentle, radiant heat from a small, perfect fire of peat and birchwood on a stone hearth. The air smelled of woodsmoke, dried herbs, old paper, and the deep, mineral damp of the earth. Shelves carved into the rock held a few tin mugs, a chipped enamel pot, bundles of roots and feathers. In a niche, a small, ancient stone cross sat, its carving softened by centuries of touch. Against one wall lay a bed of heather and sheepskins. Against the other, under a piece of greased hide, was a stack of books and a disorder of papers.

The old man settled onto a stool by the fire, gesturing for Vail to take another. “You have been fighting the symptom. The cough. Not the rot in the lung.”

“I tried to guard the veil,” Vail said, the admission rough. “To keep it from being torn.”

“And you saw them not tearing, but probing. Picking at the stitches.” Ruaridh’s blue gaze was unblinking. “You saw the stone not as a singer, but as a lock.”

Vail felt a chill that had nothing to do with the outside air. “How do you know this?”

A skeletal hand waved towards the entrance. “The glen tells me. It is restless. The quiet has a new flavour. A metallic taste. You felt it. You are of the glen enough to taste it, but not of it enough to know its name.” He leaned forward, the firelight carving valleys in his face. “You think the silence is the power. It is not. The silence is the bandage. The wound is what lies beneath.”

“The Thin Places,” Vail whispered.

“A pretty name for a terrible truth,” Ruaridh spat softly. “Places where the world is not finished. Where the weave is thin, and what was before the weave… presses close. The old ones, the real old ones, they knew. They could not mend the weave. So they made patches. Stones. Songs. Sacrifices. They placed them over the thin spots, and the pressure from the other side… it made the patches hum. It made them powerful. Men felt that power, and they forgot it was a symptom. They started to worship the bandage, and forgot the wound.”

Vail saw it then, with a clarity that was like a knife to the gut. The standing stone in the corrie. Its song, its power to warp reality, to grant visions or madness—it wasn’t magic. It was stress. It was the groan of a dam under unimaginable pressure. Locke and his ilk saw a battery to be tapped. A science to be understood.

“They are going to try to channel it. To use it,” Vail said.

“They will try to siphon the pressure,” Ruaridh corrected. “They think they can bleed it off, control the flow. They are fools. You do not relieve the pressure on a deep-sea trench by poking a hole in the hull. You invite the abyss in.”

“What happens if they succeed? If they ‘unpick’ the stitch?”

Ruaridh was silent for a long time, his eyes on the peat glow. “The stories call it *An Dubh-Chlàbar*,” he said finally. “The Black Mire. Not a thing, but an un-thing. A forgetting. It does not destroy; it unmakes. It turns substance to mist, memory to echo, law to whim. A place where *An Dubh-Chlàbar* leaks through… time folds. Light forgets how to travel straight. A man might walk in and never have been.” He looked at Vail. “Your Forrester. He did not die. He… un-became. The glen reclaimed him not into death, but into potential. He is raw material now, waiting for the shape of the silence to tell him what to be.”

The horror of it settled in Vail’s marrow, cold and heavy. It was worse than any monster, any malevolent spirit. It was anti-creation. “The stone is holding it back.”

“The stone is focusing it,” Ruaridh said. “Like a lens focuses sunlight. It takes the diffuse pressure of *An Dubh-Chlàbar* and concentrates it into a single, bearable point of power—the silence, the field. Remove the lens, or crack it… the focus is lost. The pressure floods out. Not as a beam, but as a wave.”

“How do I stop them?”

“You cannot stop men who are already halfway across a threshold they do not see,” the old man said, a profound sadness in his voice. “Their machines, their certainty… they are a weight already tipping the balance. You can only choose what happens when they fall.”

He rose, moving to the covered niche. He pulled aside the greased hide. The books were leather-bound, water-stained, their spines cracked. Among them were older things: vellum scrolls, slate tablets incised with Ogham, bundles of birch bark tied with gut. He did not touch the books. Instead, he drew out a single, folded sheet of heavy, handmade paper, gone brown with age. He brought it to Vail.

“Your father’s father’s father was a keeper of the word. He mapped not the land, but the wound in it.”

Vail unfolded the paper. It was a map of the glen, but not as any Ordnance Survey would draw it. The contours were of energy, of pressure. The burns were marked not as blue lines, but as silver threads of “carrying-song”. The woods were shaded in gradients of “listening”. And in the heart of the corrie, where the standing stone was, the paper was darkest, a core of dense, inked spirals labelled in a tight, Gaelic hand: *Fòcas an Dubh-Chlàbar*—Focus of the Black Mire.

But it was the lines radiating from that focus that seized Vail’s breath. They were like cracks in porcelain, fine hairline fractures labelled *Srathan Tana*—Thin Traces. One ran up to the high sanctuary. Another snaked down the glen towards the lodge. A third, the faintest, bled off towards the eastern ridge.

“The stone focuses the pressure,” Ruaridh murmured, his finger hovering over the map. “But the strain is taken up by the land itself. These traces… they are capillaries. They bleed off the excess, disperse it into the living rock, the deep water, the old roots. The glen has been bearing this weight for millennia. It is tired, Iain’s boy. And now men drill into the heart of it, and their machines vibrate with a frequency that is… abrasive. It is not healing the capillaries. It is scouring them raw.”

Vail understood. “The event with Forrester. The anomalies. They’re not the stone failing. They’re the land’s dispersal system overloading. The pressure is seeking new outlets.”

Ruaridh nodded. “The stone strains. The land aches. And the men with their vials listen to the ache and call it data.” He tapped the faint trace leading to the eastern ridge. “This one. It has been quiet for centuries. But two nights ago, the ptarmigan fled from there. The heather on its slope is grey, not with frost, but with a kind of… dust. A forgetting of colour.”

A new, more immediate fear crystallised. “If they proceed. If they try to tap the focus…”

“They will not just crack the lens,” Ruaridh said, his voice a dry, final leaf falling. “They will shatter the entire network of capillaries. The pressure will not flood out in a wave. It will back up, then rupture in a dozen places at once. *An Dubh-Chlàbar* will not just leak. It will bloom. This glen, and everything in it, will become a patch of un-being. A stain on reality. Your Locke thinks he can build a dam to control the sea. He will only give the abyss a shape to fill.”

Vail stared at the map, the old paper rough under his calloused fingers. His mission had inverted once more. It was not about protecting a secret, or even preventing a catastrophe. It was about triage. Containment.

“I need to know their timeline. Their method.”

Ruaridh’s eyes gleamed in the firelight. “Then you must listen to the glen itself. It knows the irritation of their presence better than any spy. Go to the eastern trace. See the dust. Feel the silence there. It will be… hungry. Then you will know what you are truly facing.”

He folded the map back into Vail’s hands. “Take this. It will not guide your feet, but it may guide your understanding. And take this also.” He turned to the hearth, picked up a small, worn leather pouch from the mantel. It was tied with a thong. He pressed it into Vail’s palm. It was heavy for its size, and cold. “Salt from the tears of the western sea. Iron from a fallen star. Rowan ash. It is not a charm. It is an anchor. A reminder of what *is*. In a place that whispers of what is *not*, you will need to remember your own substance.”

Vail tucked the pouch and the map inside his jacket. The weight of them was negligible. The weight of their meaning was colossal. He stood. “Thank you, Ruaridh.”

The old man did not rise. “Do not thank me. I have given you a burden, not a gift. I have shown you the tumour and handed you a scalpel, knowing the surgery may kill the patient.” He looked into the peat fire, his ancient face bleak. “The glen is dying by inches. Perhaps a quick end is a mercy. Perhaps your men of science will simply be the instrument of a fate long written. Go now. And do not come back here. This place… it is on a trace. The silence is growing teeth here. I am old. I am part of the quiet. But you still have sound in you. Do not let it be the last thing you make.”

The dismissal was absolute. Vail ducked out of the bothan, leaving the peat-scented warmth for the vast, cold throat of the night.

The moon was a sliver of bone high above the peaks, casting a weak, phosphorescent light. Vail oriented himself by the feel of the mountain against his back, by the memory of the map now burning in his mind. He turned east, away from the sanctuary, and began to climb.

The journey was a descent into a different kind of darkness. The familiar nocturnal sounds—the scuttle of a vole, the sigh of wind in a grass tussock—faded away. The air grew still and thick, not with moisture, but with a profound auditory absence. It was the silence of the corrie, but untamed, undirected. It did not calm; it watched.

After an hour of steep, scrambling ascent, he reached the ridge the old man had indicated. The heather here was indeed grey. Not the silver-grey of moonlight, but a flat, matte grey, like television static. He crouched, touching it. It crumbled under his fingers into a fine, cold powder that held no scent. It was not dead. It was *undefined*. As if the concept of “heather” was slowly being erased from this patch of earth.

A wave of vertigo washed over him, not spatial, but existential. For a heart-stopping moment, he could not recall the colour green. The memory of it slipped from his mind like a fish through numb fingers. He clutched the cold pouch inside his jacket, gripping it until the pain of the hard contents against his palm grounded him. *Salt. Iron. Ash.* Real things. The colour green rushed back, vivid and desperate.

This was the hunger Ruaridh had spoken of. This was the un-making, beginning at the frayed edges.

He raised his head, looking back across the glen. From this vantage, he could see the faint, sickly glow of the compound in the distant corrie, a pustule of artificial light in the ancient dark. The geometry of it was clear now. Locke’s operation sat directly on the Focus. His machines were vibrating the stone, agitating the pressure, straining the capillary traces. This grey dust on the eastern ridge was the first sign of systemic failure.

A cold, tactical calm settled over Vail. Sentiment was ash. The glen was a patient in septic shock. Locke was not the doctor; he was the source of the infection. The stone was the failing organ. The traces were the collapsing circulatory system.

He could not save the patient. Not anymore.

The only remaining question was one of containment. How to cauterise the wound. How to ensure the infection died with the host.

A plan began to form in his mind, dark, absolute, and terrible. It did not involve stealing data or sabotaging machinery. It was geological. It was about pressures and releases. It involved using Locke’s own arrogance, his desire to tap the power, against him. To turn the siphon into a detonator.

To make the opening of the door not a controlled experiment, but a catastrophic collapse that would seal it forever, burying the would-be masters under a mountain of un-becoming.

He needed to get closer. He needed to see the layout, the machinery, the heart of their operation. Not as a guardian, but as a sapper. Not to protect the veil, but to bring the entire mountain down upon it.

As he turned his gaze from the grey dust back to the distant glow, a new sound threaded its way into the profound quiet. It was not mechanical. It was organic, but wrong. A skittering, clicking noise, like many dry sticks being shaken in a pot, coming from further along the grey ridge. It was a sound with no source he could see, a sound that seemed to bleed from the air itself.

It was the sound of the hunger, noticing him.

The hook pulled, deep and cold. The glen’s defences were crumbling. The Thin Traces were becoming active wounds. And Vail was no longer just a man caught between two sides. He was a variable entering a final, devastating equation. He had come for knowledge to prevent a disaster.

He was leaving with the blueprint for a sacrifice.

The skittering sound faded, but the sense of being measured, of being *tasted* by the silence, remained. Vail touched the pouch at his chest, felt the solid reality of the rifle on his back—a tool for killing things that lived. He would need different tools now.

He moved off the ridge, away from the hungry grey, back towards the world of substance and consequence. His path no longer led to observation or interception.

It led to the corrie. To the Focus. To the stone.

He would help Locke open his door. And he would make certain nothing, and no one, ever closed it again.



---

# Chapter 20

## **THE STONE AND THE VEIL**

### **Chapter 20**

The corrie was a wound in the mountain’s side, and the Focus was its infection.

Vail approached from the high, eastern shoulder, a shadow moving against the greater shadow of the cliffs. The air here was different. It had lost the clean, clarifying bitterness of the high ridges. It tasted of ozone and hot metal, a sterile tang that coated the tongue and left an aftertaste of burnt stone. The natural silence of the peaks had been supplanted by a low, subcutaneous hum, a vibration felt in the teeth and the marrow more than heard by the ear. It was the sound of civilisation drilling into a nerve.

He crouched behind a tumble of lichen-crusted boulders, the remnants of an ancient rockfall, and looked down.

The floor of the corrie, once a bowl of shattered granite and hardy moss, had been scoured and levelled. Prefabricated modules, gunmetal grey and windowless, formed a stark, geometric cluster at the centre. From this hive, thick black cables snaked across the scree like roots of iron, converging on the Focus.

The stone itself was unchanged. It was a squat, irregular pillar of the same dark, glassy rock as the ridge, but here it emerged fully from the earth, twice the height of a man. Its surface seemed to drink the wan afternoon light, offering no reflection. Around its base, the earth was bare, scoured clean even of dust. It looked less like a thing placed upon the land, and more like a place where the land had simply stopped.

Locke’s people had not tried to move it. They had built a cage for it.

A latticework of titanium struts encircled the stone, a geodesic dome of human engineering attempting to contain a geometry that predated angles. At various nodes on this cage, instruments were mounted—spinning discs of polished brass, crystalline sensors that glowed with a faint internal light, parabolic dishes aimed at the stone’s heart. The black cables fed into these devices, and from them, a single, thicker conduit ran back to the largest module. A siphon. A straw plunged into the heart of the impossible.

Figures moved between the modules, ant-like in their purpose. They wore dark, utilitarian clothing, not the tactical gear of Locke’s security, but the clean, unadorned coveralls of technicians. Their movements were efficient, devoid of the wary tension Vail had seen in the soldiers. These were not men guarding a secret; they were men servicing a machine. The profound wrongness of the place was, to them, merely a set of anomalous readings to be logged and calibrated.

Vail’s eyes tracked past them, seeking the architect.

He found him standing before a bank of monitors set up under a stretched awning beside the main module. Alistair Locke was a study in focused avarice. He did not pace. He stood perfectly still, his hands clasped behind his back, his gaze fixed on the screens. Even from this distance, Vail could see the raptor-like intensity of his posture. The man was not watching data; he was drinking it. The glow from the screens lit his face in pulses of pale blue and green, washing him of all warmth, making him a statue of cold ambition.

This was the mind Vail needed to understand, to turn. Not a soldier’s mind, but a scholar’s, corrupted by a singular, terrible greed. Locke did not want to conquer the other side. He wanted to own its physics. To patent the un-becoming.

Vail’s plan, conceived in the desolation of the grey ridge, was a cold, sedimentary thing in his own mind. It had layers. The first was observation. The second was infiltration. The third was the act itself—the careful, precise sabotage that would turn the siphon into a drill, the drill into a fracture, the fracture into a collapse. He would need to learn the rhythms of this place, the vulnerabilities of their technology, the moments of inattention. He would need to become a ghost in their machine.

But first, he needed to get inside the fence.

A perimeter of high, mesh fencing, topped with coils of razor wire, encircled the entire operational area. It was a statement of territorial claim, not a serious defence against what truly threatened this place. The single gate was guarded by two of Locke’s private soldiers, their faces obscured by helmets, their rifles held at a low ready. They were watching the outside world. Their backs were to the stone.

Vail waited. The mountain light was failing, bleeding from silver to lead. Work lights mounted on tall poles flickered on around the compound, casting hard, overlapping pools of white illumination that made the surrounding darkness seem thicker, more absolute. The shift changed. A group of technicians trudged out of the gate, their voices a low murmur of exhausted conversation about calibration drift and harmonic feedback. They were replaced by a fresh team, their steps quicker, their faces alert under the artificial day.

In that exchange, in the brief focus on the交接, Vail moved.

He went not for the gate, but for the perimeter fifty yards to the north, where the fence met the sheer rise of the corrie wall. The engineers had been forced to leave a narrow, triangular gap here, where the rock face was too unstable to anchor the posts. It was choked with fallen debris and shadow. Vail approached on his belly, the grit grinding beneath him. The hum of the machinery was louder here, a physical pressure. He could feel the strange, deadening aura of the Focus, a chill that had nothing to do with temperature, leaching the vitality from the air around him.

He wriggled through the gap, his pack scraping against stone. He was in.

He melted into the deeper shadow at the base of the cliff, becoming just another irregularity in the rock. From this new vantage, the scale of their operation was clearer. The main module had an extension—a clean-room environment, visible through a large plexiglass window. Inside, figures in white hooded suits moved around a central pedestal. On it, resting in a cradle of glowing filaments, was a fragment. A piece of the grey, glassy rock, no larger than his fist. It pulsed with a slow, sickly light, in time with the hum that filled the corrie. They were breeding it. Cultivating a piece of the Thin Place.

A hand fell on his shoulder.

Vail did not startle. He let his body go loose, then twisted, his own hand coming up to trap the wrist, his other driving forward—not to strike, but to muffle a cry against a mouth.

He found himself looking into the wide, terrified eyes of Elara.

She was pale, her face smudged with grease and what looked like dried salt from tears. She wore a technician’s coverall, several sizes too large, the sleeves rolled up. Her breath hitched against his palm.

He held her gaze, his own implacable as the rock behind him. Slowly, he saw recognition, and a desperate, pleading intelligence. He eased his hand from her mouth, but kept his grip on her wrist. It was thin, bird-like, trembling.

“They have my brother,” she whispered, the words barely audible over the hum. Her voice was frayed, a bowstring about to snap. “In the module. He’s… he’s linked to their primary array. A biological resonator, they call it. His sensitivity… they’re using him to tune the siphon.”

Vail released her wrist. “Why are you outside?”

“I serve the food. I clean. I am… permitted.” The words were ashes in her mouth. “I was looking for a way to disable the generators. I saw you crawl through the gap.” She looked at him, a wild hope warring with bottomless fear. “You came back. To stop it.”

“I came to finish it,” Vail said, his voice low and without inflection. “A different way.”

Her eyes searched his face, reading the grim finality there. She did not ask for details. The language of sacrifice was one she understood. She nodded, a quick, sharp jerk of her chin. “What do you need?”

“Access. To the control hub. To the siphon’s core.”

“Locke is always there. Or Dr. Aris, his lead physicist. The security is… not just men. There are sensors. Motion, thermal. But they are calibrated to ignore the bio-resonance signature. It creates a blind spot, a sort of… quiet zone in their field.”

“Your brother’s signature.”

“Yes. They make him wear a transmitter. It creates a bubble of perceptual static around him. A side-effect they consider negligible.” A fierce, bitter light came into her eyes. “I can get you the transmitter. For a few minutes. He is sedated for the deep-tuning sessions. It would be enough for you to get close.”

“And you?”

“I will be with him. When it happens.” She said it with a flat certainty. “Whatever you plan… we are already buried here.”

Vail looked from her desperate face to the glowing clean-room, to the monolithic stone in its cage. He saw the pieces of Locke’s arrogance. The use of a boy as a tool. The disregard for the bleed-effect of the very energies they sought to harness. The blind spot created by their own technology. It was a pattern of vulnerabilities, a fault line waiting for a seismic tap.

“Get the transmitter,” he said.

She slipped away, a ghost in the oversized coveralls, blending into the stream of technicians moving between the modules. Vail settled back into the shadows. He watched the ritual of the corrie. The technicians made their offerings of data to the screens. The stone accepted nothing, gave nothing. It merely was. The hum deepened, fractionally. On the monitors, Locke pointed at a cascading waterfall of numbers, his expression one of voracious delight.

Twenty minutes later, Elara returned. She did not approach him directly. She stopped near his hiding place, pretended to adjust her boot, and let a small, flat disc of brushed metal, warm from her skin, fall to the gravel. She walked on.

Vail retrieved it. It was featureless, save for a single, tiny green LED that glowed on its edge. He slipped it inside his shirt, against his sternum. A moment later, a subtle wave of dissonance passed through him, a feeling like hearing two notes almost, but not quite, in harmony. The air around him seemed to grow slightly indistinct, the sharp edges of the work lights blurring as if seen through old glass.

He moved.

Walking not with a infiltrator’s stealth, but with the bored, direct pace of a technician on a task, he crossed the open ground towards the main module. He passed within thirty feet of a guard. The man’s gaze swept over him, paused for a microsecond, then slid away as if his eyes could find no purchase. The perceptual static. Vail felt the wrongness of the stone intensify with each step, a pressure building behind his eyes, a taste of chalk and static on his tongue.

The entrance to the main module was a heavy, airtight door. A keypad glowed beside it. Vail’s hand hesitated. Then, from inside, the door hissed and swung open. A technician exited, carrying a tablet, his head down. Vail caught the door before it sealed, and stepped through.

The interior was a cathedral to noise. The hum was a physical force here, vibrating the metal floor grates. Banks of servers lined the walls, their indicator lights a frantic constellation of red and green. The air was cool, recycled, smelling of electricity and sweat. At the far end, in a raised command area, stood Locke and a thin, anxious-looking woman in a lab coat—Dr. Aris. They were silhouetted against a massive central screen showing a real-time, three-dimensional rendering of the Focus. It was a map of torment. Vail could see energy currents, depicted in pulsing reds and yellows, swirling around the stone like a trapped storm. At the base, a single, coherent thread of brilliant white light was being drawn out, fed into the siphon conduit. They were not just observing the Thin Place. They were tapping a vein.

To the right of the command dais was the entrance to the clean-room extension. Through the plexiglass, Vail could see the pedestal, the pulsating fragment. And beside it, on a reclining medical chair, lay a boy. Kael. Wires snaked from his temples, his chest, to the banks of machinery surrounding him. His face was pale and peaceful, deep in chemical sleep. Elara stood beside him, one hand on his forehead, her back to the room. A sentinel.

Vail’s target was the siphon core. It would be here, where the data and the energy met. He saw it—a cylindrical chamber of thick, leaded glass, directly beneath the main screen. Inside, suspended in a field of coruscating energy, was the terminus of the black conduit. It was a nightmare of crystal and copper, a technological flower designed to bloom in non-Euclidean soil. Cables and waveguides fed from it into a control console, manned by two focused technicians.

This was the heart. The point where their control was most absolute, and most fragile. To sabotage it here, to overload the harmonic stabilisers, would not simply break the machine. It would send a feedback pulse of catastrophic force back down the siphon, into the stone. A shout into the abyss, designed to provoke an avalanche.

He needed a distraction. A shift in the delicate balance of their attention.

His eyes found a secondary monitoring station, away from the main hub. It displayed power grid readings, geothermal taps from deeper in the mountain, coolant flow rates. The life support of this arrogant outpost. One of the readouts, for a secondary generator bank, flickered yellow.

Vail moved to an empty workstation. He called up a schematic, his fingers moving with a borrowed certainty. He had spent a lifetime understanding systems—ecological, meteorological, the slow systems of the land. This was just a faster, more brittle kind of system. He found the regulator for the flickering generator bank. He inserted a command to simulate a critical overheating protocol. A staged, gradual ramp-down.

He executed it.

A moment later, a low, urgent alarm chimed in the command area. A yellow warning flashed on the edge of the main screen. Dr. Aris started, her head snapping towards a subsidiary monitor. “Geothermal feed to secondary bank is destabilising. Pressure spike.”

Locke’s face darkened, not with worry, but with irritation. “Aris, see to it. We cannot have fluctuations. Not now. We are approaching coherence.”

The physicist hurried down from the dais, brushing past Vail without seeing him, her mind already wrestling with phantom pressures. One of the technicians from the siphon console left his station to assist her, pulling up diagnostics.

The heart was less guarded.

Vail walked to the siphon core console. The remaining technician was engrossed in maintaining the energy flow, his hands dancing across holographic controls. Vail stood behind him, looking at the chamber. Up close, the energy within was hypnotic, wrong. It moved in patterns that suggested shapes just beyond comprehension. The hum here was a tone, a single, deep note that vibrated in his fillings.

From his pocket, he took the small, grey stone he had taken from the ridge. It was inert now, a piece of the dead place. But it was of the same substance. A key of sorts.

The console had a physical access panel, for manual calibration. It was sealed with a magnetic lock. Vail placed the grey stone against the lock housing. For a second, nothing. Then, the stone in his hand grew cold, so cold it burned. The status lights on the lock flickered, cycling through colours before dying to a dull grey. The panel clicked open.

Inside was a nest of optical calibrators and harmonic dampeners. The safety buffers. Vail’s hands were steady. He did not need to break anything. He needed to re-tune. Using a small multitool from his belt, he began to adjust the dampeners, minutely, incrementally. He turned them not towards stability, but towards amplification. He set them to resonate not with the siphon’s controlled draw, but with the raw, chaotic frequency of the Thin Place itself. He was aligning the lens to focus the sun, not to capture its light.

He worked by feel, by the deepening of the wrongness in the air. The hum began to change. The single, solid note developed a tremor, a harmonic overtone that was distinctly organic. A skittering, clicking echo of the sound from the ridge.

The technician at the console stiffened. “Sir,” he said, his voice tense. “I’m getting anomalous feedback in the stabilisation matrix. It’s… it’s like an echo.”

Locke, from the dais, did not look away from his main screen. “Compensate. We are too close.”

“I can’t isolate it. It’s coming from the source. It’s as if the source is… responding.”

That got Locke’s attention. He turned, his eyes sharp. “Responding? To what?”

Vail closed the access panel. The grey stone in his hand was now warm, almost feverish. He slipped it back into his pouch. His work was done. The equation was primed. The collapse would not happen now. It would happen when they pushed their machine to its limit, when they opened their door wide. He had simply ensured the door would become a mouth, and the mountain would bite down.

He began to move back, towards the clean-room, towards Elara. He needed to return the transmitter, to leave her with her brother before the final act.

He was halfway across the floor when Locke’s voice, cold and clear, cut through the hum.

“You.”

Vail froze. He turned.

Locke was staring directly at him. Not through him. At him. The perceptual static from the transmitter… it had wavered. Perhaps the boy’s sedation had lightened, altering the bio-resonance. Perhaps the rising, wrong energy in the room had disrupted it. It didn’t matter.

Locke’s face was a mask of cold fury and dawning, horrific understanding. He looked from Vail, to the siphon core console, to the technician’s worried face. He did not see a saboteur’s handiwork, not yet. He saw a variable. An infection in his sterile equation.

“Seize him,” Locke said, his voice devoid of all its earlier scholarly passion. It was the voice of a man ordering the excision of a tumour.

The guards at the door, their perception now clear, moved. So did the technicians, blocking the exits. Vail was ten paces from the clean-room door. Elara had turned, her face a mask of terror, her eyes meeting his.

He had a choice. Fight his way to her, and likely die in the attempt, triggering a lockdown that would doom them all before the equation could complete. Or trust the plan, and the sacrifice.

He gave Elara the faintest nod. A promise. A farewell.

Then he ran—not towards her, but towards the main entrance, the heavy door now swarming with guards. He was not trying to escape. He was drawing the poison away from the heart, giving the plan time to beat.

A guard lunged. Vail dropped, swept the man’s legs, sent him crashing into a server rack. Sparks fountained. Alarms shrieked, layering over the deep, trembling hum. He was up, driving an elbow into another’s throat, using their momentum to shove himself towards the door. He hit the keypad, the door hissed open, and he burst out into the cold, electric night of the corrie.

Shouts followed him. Lights speared the darkness, hunting him. He ran for the cliff wall, for the labyrinth of fallen boulders. A shot rang out, ricocheting off granite with a vicious whine.

He reached the shadows, his breath raw in his throat. He had drawn them out. The corrie was in uproar. But inside the module, Locke would be turning his attention back to his prize, to the coherence he was so close to achieving. He would not stop. Arrogance would demand he push forward, now, to prove his control over the chaos Vail represented.

Vail climbed, finding a high crevice in the rock. He turned and looked back down at the infected wound of the compound. The work lights swung wildly. Figures scrambled. But in the command module, the main screen still glowed, a beacon of terrible ambition. He saw Locke’s silhouette return to the dais, saw him raise a hand, not in retreat, but in command.

*Proceed.*

The hum from the Focus deepened, shedding its tremor, solidifying into a note of profound, gravitational pull. The light around the stone in its cage brightened, not in a flash, but in a slow, awful swell. The black siphon conduit throbbed with captured energy, a luminous vein pumping poison from the heart of the world.

Vail had set the charge. Locke was now pressing the detonator.

And from the edges of the corrie, from the shadows between the floodlit pools, a new movement began. Not human. A shifting, a gathering of darkness that was more than absence of light. It was the grey dust, stirring. It was the skittering, clicking hunger, drawn to the beacon, to the opening door.

The hook pulled, deep and final. He had come to bury the door. He had not considered what might be waiting on the other side, already pushing its way through. The equation was in its final term. The mountain would fall. But it would not fall alone.



---

# Chapter 21

## **THE STONE AND THE VEIL**

### **Chapter 21**

The mountain was singing.

It was not the wind, which had died to a watchful stillness. It was not the burn, whose voice was lost beneath the electric thrum from the compound. It was a deeper resonance, a subsonic chord struck in the bedrock, a vibration that travelled up through the soles of Vail’s boots and into the marrow of his bones. It was the sound of a great door, groaning on hinges of stone and time.

From his crevice high on the corrie wall, he was a speck of grit in a closing fist. Below, the compound was a frantic, luminous hive. Men scrambled like ants whose hill had been kicked, their shouts thin and brittle against the thickening air. The main screen in the command module glowed with a cold, blue-white ferocity, a false dawn in the bowl of night. Locke’s silhouette was a black cut-out against it, arms raised not in alarm, but in conductor’s ecstasy. *Proceed.*

The Focus had found its note. The erratic tremor was gone, replaced by a hum so steady and profound it felt less like a sound and more like a change in pressure, a fist slowly squeezing the air from the corrie. The cage around the Veil-stone was no longer a container of light, but a sun in miniature, its radiance pulsing in time with the hum—a slow, systolic rhythm. The black siphon conduit, that obscene umbilical, throbbed with a captured, bruise-purple luminosity. It was no longer drawing energy out. It was feeding it back in, a forced transfusion, overloading the stone, hammering against the door it was meant to gently pick.

Vail’s breath plumed, not in the ragged gasps of his flight, but in short, sharp clouds. The cold was intensifying, a dry, crystalline cold that bit deeper than the winter air had any right to. It was the cold of a vacuum, of an absence being invited in.

And the invitation was being answered.

At the edges of the floodlit pools, where the manufactured light frayed into the natural dark, the shadows were breeding. They thickened, pooled, coalesced into forms that shivered with a motion not born of wind. The grey dust, the residue of consumed stone and life, was no longer inert. It swirled like ground fog with purpose, gathering into hummocks that rose, fell, and skittered on unseen limbs. A clicking chatter, the sound of shale sliding over shale, multiplied into a chorus. It came from the boulder fields, from the scree slopes, from the very cracks in the cliff face. The hunger, dormant and scattered, was now a congregation. Drawn to the beacon. Drawn to the rip in the world’s fabric.

Vail had wanted to collapse the tunnel. He had not stood at its mouth and listened to the scrabbling from the other side.

A new light joined the tableau—a frantic, orange stutter. Fire. One of the smaller utility modules, a fuel pod perhaps, had been breached. Flames licked up its side, casting hellish, dancing shadows that made the gathering grey things seem to leap and caper. An alarm began a whooping, mechanical scream, a sound of pure civilisation in panic, which was swiftly drowned by the mountain’s deepening song.

He had to move. The crevice was a trap. He needed height, a vantage to see the whole dying play. He scanned the cliff above him. It was sheer, but fractured. A chimney, choked with old rubble and ice, promised a route to the northern rim. He would be exposed for a dozen heartbeats to any marksman below, but the marksmen were otherwise occupied—shooting into the skittering dark at shapes that melted and reformed.

He pushed himself out of the stone embrace and climbed.

His hands were numb, his muscles burned with fatigue and the after-shock of the electrical surge, but the climb was a clean, simple problem. A crack for fingers, a ledge for a toe, the pull of his own weight against gravity. It was an ancient dialogue, and in the face of the profanity below, it felt like a prayer. He moved not with speed, but with a grim, deliberate economy, a beetle on a wall. A shot did ring out, but it went wide, sparking off rock two yards to his left. The shooter was not aiming; he was firing at phantoms.

Vail heaved over the lip of the rim and lay on his back on the frozen turf, the vast, star-fretted bowl of the night sky above him. The hum was clearer here, purer. It vibrated in his teeth. He rolled onto his belly and looked down.

The corrie was a diorama of two worlds ending.

The human world was unravelling in fire and noise. Another module bloomed with flame. Figures ran, some towards the central command module, some away, towards the perimeter gates that were now choked with shifting, granular shadows. Muzzle flashes dotted the darkness like angry fireflies. He saw a man dragged, screaming, into a pool of darkness that seemed to swallow him whole, the scream cut off with a wet finality. The order of Locke’s ambition was dissolving into its constituent parts: fear, violence, and the desperate fuel of self-preservation.

But it was the other unravelling that held Vail’s eye, that chilled him deeper than the air.

The light from the caged stone was not just bright. It was *opening*. The air around it shimmered, not with heat haze, but with a terrible liquidity, like oil on water. The very substance of reality there was becoming ambiguous. And from that shimmer, things were emerging.

Not the skittering grey hunters from the edges. These were different. They were suggestions of form, made of the same iridescent black as the siphon conduit, as if the conduit’s essence was bleeding into the world. They unfolded from the shimmer like dark origami—spindly, multi-limbed, impossibly angled. They did not crawl or run. They *flowed*, moving across the rock and metal of the compound with a silent, viscous grace. Where they passed, the steel of walkways frosted and corroded, pitting instantly as if with a century of rust. A man turned and fired a full magazine into one. The bullets passed through it with no more effect than stones thrown into a black pool, leaving only concentric ripples in its form before it enveloped him. He did not scream. He simply stiffened, then crumbled into a statue of grey dust that the form absorbed as it moved on.

They were not consuming matter. They were translating it. Turning the solid, the complex, the lived, into the inert, the neutral, the base. They were the verbs of the world on the other side of the Veil.

Locke saw them. Vail, from his height, could see the silhouette on the dais turn, could see the raised arms falter. The conductor faced a symphony that had escaped its score. But instead of retreat, the figure straightened. Locke’s voice, amplified by the module’s system, cut through the chaos, not with fear, but with a furious, gleaming triumph.

“Magnificent! Do you see? The translation is pure! The protocol is *active*!”

He was not horrified. He was vindicated. The man was not looking at annihilation. He was looking at data. At confirmation. The ultimate reduction of all things to their simplest, most stable state—this was his coherence. The grey dust was not waste; it was peace.

A wave of nausea, profound and spiritual, rolled through Vail. He understood now, fully, the cancer he was fighting. It was not malice. It was the absolute, serene conviction that the magnificent, painful, beautiful complexity of a living world was a correctable error.

One of the black, flowing forms reached the base of the command module. It did not attack the glass. It simply pressed against it. The laminated pane, designed to withstand Arctic storms, clouded, then crystallised, then dissolved into a cascade of fine, grey sand that poured inward. The hum of the Focus flooded out, unfiltered, a physical force.

Locke finally took a step back. But it was too late.

The form entered the module.

Vail did not see the end of it. His attention was ripped away by a movement closer, on his own rim. A scrabbling, clicking approach. He rolled onto his back, the knife from his belt in his hand—a useless sliver of steel against such physics.

It was one of the grey skitterers. It was the size of a large dog, a jumbled collection of sharp angles and dust-clotted limbs, with a central maw that was simply a vortex of shifting grit. It pulsed with a faint, internal luminescence, a faded echo of the stone’s terrible light. It paused ten feet away, its body vibrating as if sensing him. It had no eyes, but Vail felt the weight of its attention, a hollow, wanting thing.

It did not charge. It began to circle, herding him towards the cliff edge.

Vail rose to a crouch, his mind cold and clear. He could not fight it. He could not outrun it on the open rim. His eyes darted, finding nothing but star and cliff.

Then he heard it. A sound that did not belong. Not a hum, not a click, not a scream.

A whistle.

Low, soft, carried on the still air from the opposite side of the corrie. A three-note phrase, repeated. A hill call. A human call.

The grey thing shuddered, its circling interrupted. It turned its vortex-maw towards the sound, its vibration spiking with confusion.

Vail did not question. He moved. He broke from his crouch and ran, not away from the creature, but parallel to the cliff edge, towards the source of the whistle. The thing skittered after him, its claws scraping on stone.

The whistle came again, closer now. And with it, a thrown shape, spinning through the starlight. It landed between Vail and his pursuer—a small, cloth-wrapped bundle. It burst on impact, not with an explosion, but with a flash of intense, white-hot light and a smell of burning thyme and ozone.

The grey thing recoiled as if scalded, emitting a shriek like grinding glass. It writhed, parts of its dust-clotted form flaking away, the internal light guttering. It was not hurt, but it was *repelled*. The simple, organic chemistry of the burning herbs, the sudden flare of clean combustion—it was an affront to its nature.

Vail sprinted past the smouldering bundle. A figure emerged from the shadow of a standing stone, arm beckoning.

“Here! Now!”

It was a woman’s voice, rough with peat-smoke and command. He saw a glint of pale hair, a face etched by weather, a long coat of oiled wool. She held a wooden staff shod with iron.

He reached her. She did not look at him, her eyes fixed on the recovering grey thing. “Down,” she snapped, shoving him behind the standing stone. She raised the staff and brought its iron-shod end down on a specific point of the rock at her feet.

A *tonk* sound, deep and resonant, like a stone dropped into a deep well, echoed out. It was a tiny sound against the symphony of destruction below, but it had a peculiar quality of penetration, as if it travelled through the ground, not the air.

The grey thing stopped its advance. It quivered, its clicking chatter reduced to an uncertain rattle. It seemed to lose interest, turning its attention back towards the greater beacon below. After a moment, it scurried away, flowing over the rim and down the cliff face towards the feast.

Vail leaned against the cold stone, breathing hard. He looked at the woman. She was older than him, perhaps in her sixties, but with a straight-backed, tensile strength. Her eyes, when she finally turned them on him, were the colour of flint.

“You are the one who walks the old paths,” she said. It was not a question. “I am Moira. Of the Gleann Taibhse.”

A keeper. One of the scattered few who remembered the true names of places. The Glen of Shadows.

“Vail.” He nodded towards the corrie. “I tried to stop this.”

“You poured water on a grease fire,” she said, her voice grim. “You spread it.” She looked down at the nightmare panorama. “He has forced the lock. The door is not just open. It is broken. The things that come now… they are not just passing through. They are looking to stay.”

“The stone—”

“—is a wound. It must be closed.”

“How? They have it caged in more power than you can imagine.”

Moira’s smile was thin and held no humour. “You imagine I came alone?”

She gestured with her staff, a sweeping motion along the northern rim. Vail peered into the starlight. He saw them now. Figures, a dozen or more, standing at intervals along the edge of the world. They were still as the standing stones they stood beside. Some held staffs. Others held nothing at all. They were not soldiers. They were points of stillness in the maelstrom. They were the land, awake.

“The old ways are not spells, man from the south,” Moira murmured. “They are conversations. You have shouted into the hill. Now we must answer. And the answer must be a ‘no’ so loud it breaks the shouters.”

Below, the command module was dissolving. The black, flowing forms were everywhere, turning technology into tomb-dust. The human resistance was crumbling into isolated pockets of futile gunfire. The Focus still hummed, but its note was changing, warping, taking on the harmonics of the shrieking grey things and the silent flow of the translators. It was becoming a chorus of entropy.

Locke’s silhouette was gone, swallowed by the beautiful, terrible truth of his coherence.

“What is your ‘no’?” Vail asked, his voice hoarse.

Moira looked at the caged star at the heart of the compound, at the shimmering wound in the world. “That does not belong here. So we must remind the mountain what *does*.”

She raised her staff high, then drove it down again on the same spot. *Tonk.*

Along the rim, the other figures moved. One beat a drum, a slow, deep heartbeat that Vail felt in his chest. Another raised a horn to his lips, and a low, bovine moan swept across the corrie, a sound older than metal. A woman began to sing, a wordless, descending melody that wove between the drum and the horn, a thread of sorrow and defiance.

They were not attacking. They were asserting.

The effect was not immediate. But Vail, his senses stretched raw, felt it. A tension in the air, a slight stiffening in the fabric of things. The shimmer around the Veil-stone flickered. One of the black, flowing forms, gliding towards a group of fleeing technicians, seemed to hit an invisible wall. It rippled, its progress slowing, as if moving against a current.

The mountain’s song was being challenged by an older song.

The grey skitterers on the fringes hesitated, their clicking chorus losing its rhythm. They were creatures of the open door, of the invitation. This was a revocation.

But the door was still open. The Focus, though faltering, still pulsed. The wound was too deep, the forced lock too broken, for a song alone to heal it. The black forms rallied, pressing against the newfound resistance. The shimmer solidified again. The hum regained its dominance, absorbing the drumbeat, distorting the horn’s cry.

Moira’s face was set, strained. Sweat beaded on her temple despite the cold. “It is not enough,” she gritted out. “The wound is in the flesh of the world. The song must become a suture.”

She looked at Vail, her flint eyes sharp. “You. You have touched both sides. You carry the mark of the stone and the will of the hill. You are the needle.”

A cold deeper than the night seeped into him. “What do you mean?”

“The song defines the boundary. But something must cross it, to pull the thread tight. Something must go into that light, and speak the true name of this place into the heart of the false one.” She pointed her staff directly at the caged, pulsing star. “You must walk into the wound.”

The hook pulled, deep and final. To bury the door was one thing. To enter its screaming mouth, to walk into the blinding, translating heart of the wrongness, was another. He looked from Moira’s implacable face to the dissolving world below, to the figures on the rim holding the line with nothing but breath and belief. The equation was in its final term. The mountain would fall. But it would not fall alone.

He had come to set a charge. Now he was to be the detonation.

He met Moira’s gaze and gave a single, slow nod. The chorus of the keepers rose around him, a wall of sound against the void. He turned towards the cliff edge, towards the path that led down into the singing, dying light. The needle, poised above the torn fabric of the world.



---

# Chapter 22

## **THE STONE AND THE VEIL**

### **Chapter 22**

The chorus of the keepers was a wall. Vail was to be the breach in it.

He turned from Moira’s flint gaze, from the strained symphony of breath and belief holding the line at the cliff’s rim. The path down was not a path. It was a geometry of collapse, a scree-slope of shattered shale that gleamed like wet scales in the pulsating light from below. He began his descent, and the world unstitched itself around him.

The song of the keepers—the drum-thump of feet, the deep-throated hum from chests, the high, reeling cry of the horn—faded behind him, not with distance, but with absorption. The humming from the Focus was a solvent. It did not clash with the hill’s song; it digested it. The drumbeats grew muffled, thick, as if heard through a coffin lid. The horn’s defiance warped into a thin, animal shriek of feedback. The wall of sound was becoming a membrane, and he was passing through it.

The air changed. The clean, iron-tanged cold of the high corrie was gone, replaced by a static warmth that carried no comfort. It was the warmth of a closed room where a machine has run too long. It smelled of ozone and hot stone and, underneath, a sweet, meaty decay, like forgotten blossoms in a sealed jar. The light was no longer something that fell upon the landscape; it was the landscape. It poured from the wound in the summit, a liquid, singing glare that bleached colour and depth from the world. The boulders were not grey, but a luminous, bone-white. The shadows they cast were not black, but a vibrating, bruised purple. Sound itself seemed to slow, the crunch of his boots on the scree arriving at his ears a heartbeat late, the skittering clicks of the grey watchers now a viscous, popping static at the edge of hearing.

He was inside the event horizon of the wrongness.

Halfway down, he looked back. The rim of the corrie was a serrated black cut-out against a sky that was no longer sky, but a dome of swirling, mother-of-pearl haze. The figures there were silhouettes, their movements stiff, marionette-like against the glare. He saw the sweep of Moira’s staff, the defiant set of Catriona’s shoulders beside Euan’s broad bulk. They were statues in a bleaching flood. The sight was a hook in his gut, pulling him back. He was leaving the world of substance for the world of idea. He faced forward again.

The Focus was not a thing he approached. It was a condition he entered.

The ground levelled into the floor of the high corrie, a bowl of ancient ice-scoured stone. At its centre, the wound gaped. It was not a hole, but a tear. The edges of reality were raw, flickering, like film burning in a projector gate. Through it, the light did not simply shine; it *poured*, in thick, slow rivulets that crawled over the rock, painting it in colours that had no name. The hum was a physical pressure here, a subsonic throb that vibrated in his teeth, in the marrow of his bones. It promised translation. It whispered of a peace that was not peace, but perfect, static alignment. A place where no hook could catch, because there was no depth to catch it. A flat world.

And the grey ones were no longer skittering at the fringes. They were the fringe. Dozens of them, perhaps hundreds, lined the circumference of the tear, their spindly forms perfectly still. They were not watching him. They were facing the light, their featureless heads tilted as if in rapture. They were conduits. They were the individual threads of the veil, held taut, singing the same single, devastating note. Their clicking was the static of the signal, the noise of the world being erased.

Vail stopped at the last ring of stones, a natural circle of lichen-crusted granite that felt, for a heartbeat, like a threshold. The heat from the tear was intense, dry, like the breath of a kiln. The true name of this place. Moira’s instruction echoed in the hollow the hum had made of his mind. *Speak the true name into the heart of the false one.*

What was the name? It was not ‘Coire na Saobhaidh’, the Corrie of the Fox Den. That was a label given by men who saw only the surface. The true name was the sum of its history: the grind of glaciers, the patience of lichen, the scream of the hare in the talons of the eagle, the slow seep of water through peat, the memory of fire in the granite, the root-grip of the dwarf pine clinging to a crack against a thousand storms. It was a song, not a word. A song currently being strangled.

He could not outsing the Focus. He was one man, and his voice was a reed against a gale. He was the needle. A needle does not shout. It pierces. It pulls a thread.

The hook within him, the old, deep ache of the hill that had been his burden and his compass, twisted. It was not a pull now, but a push. A final, inexorable ejection. He was the splinter the world was pushing out. He took a breath of the dead, electric air, and stepped across the stone ring.

The change was instantaneous and total.

The hum ceased to be sound and became the fabric of being. The blinding light resolved, not into clarity, but into a pervasive, shadowless illumination. He stood on a plain of smooth, seamless white stone that stretched to a horizon that was too sharp, too even. The sky was the same uniform, luminous grey. There was no sun, no source. The air was still and temperatureless. The corrie was gone. The mountain was gone. The keening wind, the smell of peat and cold stone—gone. In their place was a profound, humming silence, a silence so absolute it pressed on the eardrums.

This was the translation. This was the world rendered as concept, stripped of accident, of history, of friction. A world without hooks.

But he had brought a hook with him.

His own body felt monstrous here. It was a catalogue of imperfections: the rasp of his wool jacket, the uneven rhythm of his heart, the sweat cooling on his skin, the grit of mountain soil under his nails. He was a jagged, discordant thing in this seamless place. He looked down at his hands. They were real, solid, stained. They were the most real things he had ever seen.

A figure stood before him, a hundred paces away on the featureless plain. It was a man. Or the shape of a man. It wore his own face. It was Vail as he might have been rendered by a careful, yet utterly dispassionate, artist: the same height, the same colouring, the same clothes. But the details were wrong. The eyes were the correct shade of grey, but they held no topography, no memory of lochs or storms. They were flat, polished stones. The beard was neat, each hair in place, holding no fragments of mist. The boots were clean. This was Vail as label, as identifier. A signifier without a soul.

“You are the anomaly,” the figure said. Its voice was Vail’s own voice, run through a filter, stripped of timbre, of the slight rasp left by years of mountain air. It was pure information.

Vail said nothing. He was listening to the silence, feeling for a seam.

“This is the process of reconciliation,” Not-Vail continued, taking a step forward. Its movement was fluid, efficient. “All data must be integrated. All patterns resolved. Your pattern is persistent. It creates interference. It must be harmonised.”

“Harmonised,” Vail echoed, his own voice a shocking crack in the perfect air.

“Made consonant. Your memories, your sensory impressions, your… attachments. They are variables. They introduce noise. Here, they can be given correct weight. Assigned their proper value. Made static and clear.” It gestured with a smooth hand at the white plain. “This is clarity. This is peace. The fox does not scream here. The stone does not weather. The hook… does not pull. It is resolved.”

The words were a seduction. They spoke directly to the weary, harried core of him. To lay down the burden of feeling, of memory, of constant, aching connection. To be a fact in a catalogue. The peace of the specimen pinned in a case.

The hook in his chest gave a savage, twisting yank. It was pain. It was life.

“This is not peace,” Vail said, his voice gaining texture, becoming his own again. “This is death. You’ve just… stopped the tape.”

“Irrelevant metaphor. Process continues. Optimisation continues. You are a complex dataset. You will be integrated.” Not-Vail took another step. “Resistance is inefficient. It prolongs discomfort. Submit the data.”

From the seamless white ground around the figure, shapes began to rise. They were not the grey skitterers. They were memories, rendered in that same sterile, luminous substance. A perfect, miniature model of his father’s bothy, the one that had burned. A flickering image of Moira, younger, her face unlined by wind or worry. A stag, frozen mid-leap, elegant and empty as a heraldic symbol. They were replicas, flawless and dead. A museum of his life, curated by the thing before him.

“See?” said Not-Vail. “Preserved. Perfect. No loss. No decay. No unresolved tension.”

Vail looked at the model of the bothy. There was no smell of woodsmoke and wet dog. No crack in the lintel where his father had hung a lucky horseshoe. No chill draft from the ill-fitting door. It was a shell. It was the word ‘bothy’ made manifest, not the place.

He understood his purpose now. The needle does not argue with the fabric. It pierces it. To speak the true name was not to utter a word, but to be the contradiction. To be the anomaly that could not be resolved. To be the grit in the perfect, humming machine.

He did not attack the figure. He turned his back on it. He knelt, ignoring the protest of his knees on the hard, alien stone. He placed his palms flat against the ground. It was warm, vibrating faintly with the omnipresent hum. It felt like plastic, like bone.

He closed his eyes against the shadowless light. He sought, not the silence, but the noise within himself. The discord. The hook. He followed it down, past the fear, past the fatigue, into the raw, unprocessed data of a life lived in the teeth of the world.

He did not speak of the corrie’s name. He remembered.

He remembered the taste of the air the moment before a snowstorm—flinty and charged. He remembered the exact, crushing weight of the silence in a cave, a silence so deep it rang in the ears. He remembered the muscular flinch of a trout in a cold stream, the smell of its scales like wet stone. He remembered the rotten-sweet stench of a bog in high summer, the give of the sphagnum underfoot. He remembered the grain of the old steel in his knife, each microscopic pit a record of use. He remembered the sound of his own breath, ragged and personal, on a climb. The burn of lactic acid in his thighs. The way the dawn light could hit a slope of quartz and for a moment, make it a river of fire. The stupid, stubborn loyalty of a dog. The taste of peat-smoke in whisky. The feel of wool stiff with frost. The particular, hollow ache of a landscape after a death.

He remembered the fox’s scream. Not as a symbol, but as a raw, tearing sound that had once frozen his blood on a moonless night. He remembered the fear in it, and the wildness.

He poured these things out, not as words, but as sensations. He was a vessel tipping over, spilling the chaotic, beautiful, painful mess of a felt life onto the seamless plain. He was introducing grit, sand, moisture, decay, irregularity. He was being the flaw.

The hum changed. A waver entered it. A dissonance.

He opened his eyes. The white ground beneath his hands was no longer seamless. A darkness spread from his fingertips, a stain. It was the colour of peat, of weathered granite, of old blood. It was texture. Cracks appeared, fine as hairline fractures in ice. A smell bloomed in the sterile air—damp earth, cold stone.

“Cease.” Not-Vail’s voice was no longer perfectly modulated. A flicker of something—irritation, the first shadow of an emotion—passed over its smooth face. “You are introducing corruption. This is non-optimal. This is error.”

Vail looked up, his own face grimed with the effort, his eyes the fierce, storm-grey of the world he was forcing into this one. “No,” he said, his voice raw. “This is data you missed.”

He pushed himself to his feet. The stain on the ground grew. A single, tiny shoot of heather, brittle and brown, forced its way up through a crack. It was a dead thing, a fragment of a season. It was infinitely more real than the perfect, frozen stag.

The Not-Vail figure shimmered. Its edges blurred. “Integration… requires… compliance.” Its voice stuttered, breaking into packets of sound. The museum-memories around it flickered, the bothy’s walls turning transparent, revealing the emptiness within.

“I am not here to comply,” Vail said, taking a step forward. Each footfall left a faint, scuffed impression on the white stone. “I am here to remember. And to make this place remember what it is.”

He walked towards the figure, not with aggression, but with the relentless, weathering pace of the hills themselves. He was erosion. He was the frost that splits stone. He was the root that finds the fault line.

“This place is a wound,” he said, and his words were not just sounds, but carried the chill of the corrie’s wind. “A tear in the flesh of the world. Its true name is *absence*. Its true name is *hunger*. It is not a place at all. It is a verb. It is *taking*.”

With every word, the dissonance grew. The humming splintered into harmonics, some of them screamingly high, some grindingly low. The perfect light began to strobe, throwing jagged, real shadows for the first time. The shadow of the heather shoot was a long, desperate claw. His own shadow was a giant, a thing of depth and chaos.

The Not-Vail figure recoiled. It did not step back; its form simply receded, as if the space around it was stretching. “You are… damage. You must be… quarantined.”

From the luminous grey sky, tendrils of that same sterile substance descended, swift and silent, aiming to encase him, to wrap him in a cocoon of perfect, humming stillness. To make him a static exhibit in this dead museum.

Vail did not try to dodge. He raised his hands, his real, scarred, dirty hands. He let the tendrils touch him.

And he remembered the burn.

Not the sterile heat of this place, but the savage, consuming fire of the bothy. The smell of burning wool and pine resin. The blistering pain on the back of his neck. The sound of rafters cracking. The terror that was not clean, but smothering, thick with smoke. He remembered it all, in a single, concentrated burst of sensory agony.

The tendrils that touched him blackened. They did not burn; they *corroded*. The perfect substance crumbled into a dry, grey ash that fell to the stained ground. The corruption raced back up the tendrils towards the featureless sky.

The Not-Vail figure let out a sound—a flat, digital shriek of feedback. It clutched at its head. Its perfect form was breaking up, pixelating at the edges. The flat, stone eyes were now cracked, and through the cracks, for a fleeting second, Vail saw not emptiness, but a terrifying, infinite hunger, a void that wanted to be filled with all the noise and colour and pain of the world, to digest it into silent, seamless grey.

“You… cannot…” it glitched. “The pattern… will be… resolved…”

“The pattern is broken,” Vail said, and he took the final steps, closing the distance between them. He stood before his own disintegrating replica. He did not strike it. He reached out and placed his hand, his living, feeling, remembering hand, on its chest, where the heart would be.

He poured into it the last, deepest memory. Not a sight, or a sound, or a smell. A feeling. The feeling of the hook. The deep, unshakeable, aching *connection*. The love that was not soft, but was as hard and demanding as granite, as relentless as the sea. The pull of place. The belonging that was a wound that never healed. The beautiful, terrible burden of being part of something that was dying, and fighting for it anyway.

The figure of Not-Vail did not shatter. It *unmade*. It dissolved into a cloud of luminous dust, which then darkened, coarsened, and became a swirl of common peat ash on a sudden, cold wind. The wind that now blew across the plain was from the north-east, and it carried the salt-tang of the distant sea.

The sterile world screamed.

The seamless plain heaved. Great jagged fissures ripped across it, and from the fissures, reality roared back in. Not the curated memories, but the raw, chaotic stuff of the living world. Torrents of black, peat-stained water geysered up. Shards of dark, lichen-crusted granite thrust through the white stone like teeth. A gale howled, bringing with it the smell of rain and rotting bracken. The uniform light shattered, replaced by the chaotic, stormy gloom of a real sky, boiling with clouds the colour of bruises.

The translation was reversing. The wound was vomiting out the world it had consumed.

Vail stood at the epicentre of the cataclysm, lashed by wind and water, pelted by fragments of stone. He had been the needle. He had pulled the thread of reality back through the tear. And now the thread was unravelling everything in its path.

The white plain was gone, replaced by a chaotic, overlapped nightmare. He saw glimpses of the corrie—the real, solid rock of it—superimposed with the dissolving geometry of the Focus. The humming was a physical collapse now, a deep structural groan, like a mountain giving way. The grey skitterers, those conduits on the edge, were caught in the backlash. They did not flee. They simply came apart, unravelling into threads of grey light that snapped and whipped in the storm before dissolving into nothing. They were stitches being torn out.

Ahead, the tear itself was convulsing. The raw, flickering edges were knitting and tearing, knitting and tearing, in a rapid, violent stutter. The pouring light was now a gushing, incoherent riot of colours, spewing forth fragments of the sterile world alongside clods of real earth and splinters of mountain stone. It was a door being slammed shut a thousand times a second.

He had done it. He had spoken the true name by being its antithesis. He had buried the door from the inside.

But a door, in its death-throes, can still crush the one who closes it.

The ground beneath him—a impossible patchwork of white floor and heathery hillside—buckled violently. A fissure opened, a yawning mouth of grinding rock and screaming energy. He stumbled back, but the edge crumbled. He fell.

Not into the tear. Into the mountain.

He landed hard on a slope of loose, wet scree, the real scree of Coire na Saobhaidh, and began to slide, tumbling, down into the heart of the chaos. The world was a blur of violence: the deafening roar of the reversing translation, the strobing light, the lash of rain that had begun to fall—a cold, real, drenching rain. He crashed against a boulder, the impact driving the air from his lungs. He slid to a stop, half-buried in sliding gravel, at the very foot of the dying wound.

Above him, the tear gave one final, apocalyptic pulse. The hum peaked in a sound that was beyond sound—a silent, pressure-wave that flattened the heather for a hundred yards and made his ears bleed. Then, with a sigh that seemed to suck the warmth from the universe, it collapsed inward.

The light vanished. Not into darkness, but into the ordinary, blessed gloom of a stormy twilight in the hills. The humming ceased, replaced by the howl of the wind and the drumming of rain on stone. The tear was gone. In its place was a raw, smoking scar on the mountain’s flank, a depression of freshly-fused, glassy rock, already being scoured by the downpour. The door was shut. Sealed with a scar.

Silence rushed in, broken only by the wind and the rain. The static warmth was gone. The cold returned, a physical, soaking, merciful cold. He lay on the ground, breathing in the smell of wet earth and ozone. He was alive. He was in the world. The real, flawed, singing, dying, beautiful world.

From the rim of the corrie high above, a new sound reached him, thin and torn by the gale. It was the horn. Not a cry of defiance now, but a long, low, mournful note that bled into the wind. A lament. A salute.
